MALTHOID is the one roofing that produces satis-
factory results on every class of building.
From the most Artistic Bungalow to the Shearing
Shed sounds like a far cry; yet in both these instances,
as in all others, MALTHOID has no equal.
MALTHOID is a ready-to-lay roofing which adapts
itself to every necessity by virtue of it being absolutely
waterproof, weather-resistant to the last degree, and
practically remains intact as long as the building itself.
To buy an imitation is to court serious disaster.

The Red Page
The People’s Books,” sold in Australia at
9d. each, are marvellous value. From T. C.
Lothian, Melbourne, come three of the
latest volumes: The Growth of Freedom, by
Henry W. Nevison ; Francis Bacon, by A. It.
Skemp, and The Brontes, by Flora Masson
(published by Jack).
As the Rev. John Clifford in his preface
says : “Baptists all over the world will be
specially delighted to accompany Mr. J. A.
Packer [of The Watchman, Sydney] on
his pilgrimage Among the Heretics in
Europe" (Cassell). The frontispiece is a
photograph of the first national congress of
Russian Baptists.
Every yachtsman in Australia will find
something of interest in The Complete
Yachtsman, by B. Heckstall-Smith and Cap-
tain E. du Bulay (Methuen, per George Rob-
ertson, Sydney; 15s. in England—more
here). The first author is secretary of the
International Yacht Racing Union, and the
second a well-known authority on marine
motoring. This comprehensive book is fully
illustrated with photographs and diagrams.
It treats of yacht-building, rigging, sailing,
seamanship, tides and tide-tables, racing
rules and racing illustrations, motor din-
ghies, hydroplanes, yacht designing, ice
yachts, sea-fishing and a general history of
yachting. Given an understanding of this
book and a yachtsman’s cap, anybody, even
the present writer, could pass as a yachts-
man.

Trackers. The scene is laid on Mount
fjyllene, in Arcadia, and deals with cattle-
stealing and the invention of the lyre by
the child Hermes. The cows in question be-
longed to a dairv-farmer named Apollo,
who, discovering the absence of **my milch-

otters a reward to gods and men for their

black-trackers, the Satyrs, set out to find
the cattle. When they find the footprints
coming from a cave, it does not take this
astute band of trackers long to discover
that the cattle-thief had made the animals
walk backwards into the cave; but suddenly
the.\ retreat in panic from a strange noise

It is a secret in the gods’ abodes

and the principal boy is only six days old.
SSo there seems little chance of it being put
on by the Firm for Easter.

It is the illustrations rather than the
letterpress that give its charm to Stories of
Old, Sydney (Angus and Robertson; 3s. 6d.).
This daintily printed little book contains 50
pen-drawings by Sydney Ure/Smith. Some
of them have that slight stiffness that seems
the chief weakness of this artist; but, having
pointed to this small crudity, the critic finds
in the rest of his work ample evidences of
the practised hand and the artistic taste of this
young and promising craftsman. The stories,
prefaced by some uninspired verses, include
‘‘The Ghosts of Hunter Street;” an'interest-
ing account of the old windmills of old Syd-
ney ; rambles round the Rocks, examples of
whose rapidly disappearing architecture
Sydney Ure Smith was fortunate enough to
sketch in time; and other reminiscences of
the past. The author, Charles H. Bertie,
who contrives to be dull in a distinctly infor-
mative way, aptly asks of critics who object
to the adjective “old” applied to Sydney:
“How many cities in the world can claim
that the national history begins with them?
This is the proud boast of Sydney, for the
shore v f - Sydney Cove was the cradle of the
Australian race, and her old buildings and
streets are invested with , a dignity beyond
that of mere age.”
The next best thing to a trip to Europe is
a copy of Ethel Turner’s Ports and Happy
Havens (Ilodder and Stoughton, per Angus
and Robertson), well illustrated by photo-
graphs, and written from the fresh Austra-
lian standpoint with all this writer’s careless
charm.
One sign of our increasing importance to
ourselves is the substitution of local text-
books for imported text-books. The latest
is Invalid and Convalescent Cookery, for the
use of Australian nurses, by Kate Harriott,
with recommendations from the Aust.
Trained Nurses’ Association (N.S.W. Book-
stall Co., Is.).
New editions of Hearts of Gold, by Will
FI. Ogilvie, and of The Poetical Works of
Brunton Stephens have been issued by Angus
and Robertson, Sydney; ss. In the case of
Ogilvie’s book, this makes the fourth thou-
sand printed.
The 16th edition of Ethel Turner’s delight-
ful first book, Seven Little Australians
(Ward Lock), is to hand from Dymock’s,
Sydney, with a preface from the author in
the same vein as the story. The eight new
illustrations by John Macfarlane, however,
are scarcely typical enough: one would think-
that Norman Lindsay’s knowledge of the
Australian kiddie should have been made use
of in a book that threatens to become a
children’s classic.
AUSTRALIAN WRITERS: No. 6.
Edward Dyson
Born at Morrison's, a mining district
adjacent to Ballarat, March, 1805. Itsemns
only yesterday. Earliest definite recollec-
tions are of Alfredton, a sort of bush-side
suburb of vthe Garden city, a very excellent
place for a youngster of 12 months meditat-
ing a life of letters to locate himself in. Here
all the staple materials of Australian litera-
ture were easy and accessible. Before our
skillion door boomed half a dozen deep
mines, on the edge of the famous Sebastopol
Plateau; behind was the big bush teeming
with agricultural and pastoral interest;
east was a very serviceable plain ; west more
bush and the grand cattle yards,, a maze of
pens to which once a week came the splendid
drovers with their drifting sheep and their
mobs of sullen cattle, and from which on the
following day the sheep and cattle streamed
again, the latter still more terrible after 20
hours in the pens.
EDWARD DYSON.
I am asked for an article on my “career
as a writer, and-methods of work.” and all
this belongs, for the soaking which began
at two made the seepage possible. The
Alfredton experiences came from my
pores in stories, verse and paragraphs,
how many hundreds of columns I cannot
say, but the stories have ranged from two
“sticks” to a long novel, and with the skele-
ton work and inspiration this one district
supplied a dozen novels —and twenty times
as many stories might have been written,
So much is due to Alfredton—“Wad'dy,”
of The Gold-stealers, and half a hundred
short stories —that an annual pilgrimage is
called for, but the towering poppet-heads
have gone, the gleaming tips have been
smoothed away, or planted oyer into orna-
mental hillocks, the township has fallen
into line, and is prim and decorous ; trains
now bring in the cattle, and even the bush
is pruned and orderly. Waddy is a memory,
a grateful memory of stirring life, a feast of
local color. More especially is it remem-
bered as.a kaleidoscope of ardent character.
What vital figures those early townships
contained! The men were all personalities.
To me they are vivid still, peculiar, appeal-
ing, the “Geordies,” the “Cousin Jacks,” the
Irish, and the sprinkling of Swedes. Italians,
Germans and Frenchmen, genericallv known
as Dutchmen, each with his marked traits.
Men are not so individual in large towns.
Here, too, came the waning tribes of
natives on • periodical visits, whining for
“tickpen,” greedy for tobacco and beer.
There were occasional batches of Chinese
fossickers, too, hated with a vindictiveness
that only passed after wise legislation had
robbed the Yellow Agony of its terror.
Along the wide ironstone road went the wag-
goners, with their eight- and ten-horse teams,
carting seven-foot wood to the mines. When
a waggon stuck it was gala day in Waddy,
while the teamsters demonstrated their faith
in the Australian dogma that profanity will
shift mountains. Waddy was surely the
profanest place on earth. With drovers,*
stockmen, bullockies, carters, shepherds,
quarrymen, stone-breakers and “Geordies”
of all trades, profanity was a business me-
thod and a necessity. No wonder the chil-
dren of Waddy cursed before they could
talk. It is a cherished family tradition that
at 14 months I swore at the clergyman who
christened me.
Jt
From Waddy to Melbourne for a year, a
year that provided only one useful experi-
ence, a casual drowning in the Yarra. At
any rate I was drowned so far as a drown-
ing can be of _ any literary value to the
drowned, that is to the point of insensi-
bility. From Melbourne we went to Bendi-
go, and I indulged in much prowling in creeks
and gullies, “specking” and fossicking, much
jubilant “wagging it” in company with an
elder brother, hunting rats in underground
Bendigo, fossicking with old handleless fry-
ing-pans for prospecting dishes, lingering
around the tips seeking specimens, and fore-
gathering with experts in batteries and en-
gine houses. Back to Ballarat, at nine,
living at Soldier’s Bill, adjacent to the Yar-
rawee and Black Hill. Here I presently be-
came one of a vainglorious syndicate of
small boys owning a digger’s cradle with the
aid of which much fossicking was done on
worked-out fields of historic interest.
The family drifted to Waddy again when
I was 11, but now the great mines were all
shut down, the engine-houses were dark and
silent, the tips were given over to rabbits,
and the puddling plats to the birds and the
bats. It was a new interest and a new in-
fluence. The deserted mines took hold of
me. I haunted them like a familiar spirit,
rarely without a prospecting pan under my
arm. While the other boys prowled the
bush and climbed trees, I crawled and
scrambled about the old mines, scaling the
poppet legs, venturing down the ladder
shafts, breaking into musty offices, forges
and engine and boiler houses, and fearfully
penetrating their dark mysteries.
For years there had been school-going.
The long trial began in a certain dim kit-
chen presided over by an indefinite sort of
ogre in skirts. Later “crept like snail un-
willingly” to a “private” school, the master
of which was a bottle-nosed dipsomaniac
who taught readiug, writing and sums in-
differently with the aid of a knobbed stick.
After that dull years in various State in-
stitutions, years of bewilderment and loath-
ing. At 11, to my surprise and horror, I
found myself in the fourth class, having
reached that eminence by chicanery and
thieving. A little learning is a dangerous
thing, and my school life at this period was
liberally illustrated with “cuts.” Then sud-
denly, like an awakening from a bad dream,
I discovered how, and lessons became easy
to me. The acquisition of knowledge' was
now a pleasant, spontaneous process; and
had I remained at school a few years I
should have learned so much that I must
have devoted the remainder of my life to
the correcting of other people’s errors. For-
tunately, however, I was rescued from criti-
cism.
At 121 I was abroad in the land in a
two-horse covered waggon, a travelling
home, doing mining fields and navvies’
camps as assistant and house-keeper with a
hawker of drygoods. Things seen and felt
on these expeditions go to the making of
1 ommy the Hawker. From the position of
journeyman draper I went to that of
whimboy in a mine in a Ballarat backyard,
but they gave me a young horse, untrained
to the “demrritiou grind,” and after we had
nearly torn the' shaft up by the roots and
wrecked the whim, and had come within an
ace of killing the whole four o’clock shift. I
took the sack cheerfully, and presently
found myself among the big reefs in Clunes.
Later, fed a tinpot mill in the wilds of Bun-
garee. From Bungaree to Lefroy, Tas-
mania, and a j r ear's experience of shallow
alluvial on the Pinafore.' Sluiced for pyrites
here, and was bracemau on a little, lone ‘ mine
in a deep gully. All good copy-mak-
lng matrix. Back to Vic. and a job, trucking
in a deep, hot mine at Smeaton. From there
to battery building at Gordon’s. From Gor-
don's to Melbourne, .and the two or three
years of factory life responsible for Facfrtj
Ands. From the factory into a sub-edi-
torial position in a Melbourne weekly; then
adrift in the wide field of freelance journal-
ism. A plunge iflto the horrors of owner-
sllli C°ll° wc, G." a nd for two years I ran a
mad little paper and had quaint experiences
of Australian artists, poets and bailiffs yet
to be told. Now a productive contributor,
writing largely to order, paid by measure,
and with just sufficient grievances to make
life enjoyable.
As to my manner of doing things—other
writers I have known insist on ray capacity
for hard work. After hearing a hundred and
fifty assurances, I decline to admit the hard
work, possibly for the reason that to the man
vvho has really toiled with stubborn things
like bricks, and clay, and reef, and rock, the
mere spinning of yarns is a pleasant relaxa-
tion. Certainly it was many years before I
could • beat my conscience into regarding
writing as toil deserving of monetarv reward.
During that time I turned out a great deal of
matter, most of which was printed, but for
which not one penny was paid. If was only
on learning that one benefactor was selling
in country supplements the stuff I provided
gratis that I began to display a vulgar, mer-
cenary craving. Even after securing space
rates for contributions, I was long develop-
ing confidence in literature as both stick «<ind
crutch, and hung on to my “stiddy job,”
writing at night and on Sundays with a per-
severance that looked like a form of dementia
to my relatives. I carried the cacoethes scri-
bendi into the factory, and at 19 composed
surreptitious verse and prose for the local
market, amid the babble of 80 girls and the
clatter of the machines it was my duty to
attend.
My earliest artistic promptings were to
the making of pictures, and served to fill a
huge ledger with masterpieces of flower
drawing and studies of Australian fauna. An
incidental death when I was 12 drove me to
the higher muse, and a touching “In Memor-
iam” was inscribed on my bedroom wall. For
a couple of years after winning a way into
print, I wrote purely fictitious stuff, sup-
posedly humorous extravagance, mere word-
spinning, and felt no impulse towards my
own material, the gatherings of experience.
That was saving up and seasoning. Mean-
while, I was learning to use the tools of
trade, and to plav with sentences to some
tune. Early Bulletin sketches breathed a
more intimate note, and with The Golden
Shanty and The Haunted Mine I began the
systematic use of my reserves. Since then
the process of soakage and seepage has gone
on. Never is the soakage deliberate, and ex-
perience convinces me that matter is of little
use for literary purposes unless it has come
by chance, and till it is thoroughly digested.
Perfect assimilation is not attained under
five years. This capacity for absorbing and
storing is the fictionist’s most valuable endow-
ment. Ability in expression may come with
learning and effort, but the faculty of uncon-
scious collection is the true “gift.” If you
have that gift everything and everybody con-
tribute to your store.
I can recollect scores of people who were
good to me. Old miners who had been
“through it all” were numerous in the Waddv
days. Their yarns, their tricks of speech,
their faces come back in the oddest way.
There was a woman who dressed as much
like a man as she dared, and smoked a black
pipe, who had “kept shanty” on half the big
rushes. She talked by the hour, and her
reminiscences w r ere all raw material of the
best. My father landed in Victoria in ’52,
and saw most of the sturm und drung of the
big rushes, and in ’5B the building of the Gee-
long to Ballarat line, every furlong of which
has its story. My mother came from a life
of refinement in England into the roughest
and most animated phases of the digging
fever. To these, more than any others, l owe
the fact_ that I am never short of a theme.
Much of what came to me from the sources
enumerated I put into The Roaring Fifties,
hoping to give the book some historic value
as a faithful and intimate picture of the
times and the manners.
The composition of a novel has been to
me wholly head work. I have no notes worth
mentioning, but beat out the trunk and limbs
of my story, carrying the plan in memory ;
when the work of writing is begun, 1 keep
close to that skeleton plot, building on bark,
boughs, foliage and blossom to make the
complete tree. In each case the plotting of
the novel has been a work of odd half hours,
extending over a long period, and a small or-
chard of skeleton trees is part of my present
stock-in-trade. It is highly probable that
most of these frees will never put forth
leaves.
For the rest I have industry and system,
and only since my eyes began to enforce con-
sideration have I respected the Eight Hours
principle. So far the need of special in-
spiration has not worried me, and a story
of sorts can be started at a moment’s notice,
and 'written while you wait. To succeed
fairly well from the Bread-winner’s point of
view, as a semi-detached contributor in Aus-
tralian journalism, this machine-like produc-
tiveness is essential. .Quantity pays better
than quality. Having been a mercenary
workmd-n for five days of the week, you may
ie an artist for two, and indulge yourself in
the luxury of the task your heart craves.
EIDWARD DYSON.
An altogether charming feminine romance
is The Dream Girl, by Gertrude Hart
(George Robertson). Not for masculine
consumption is this impossible story; for
the author lives in a world of sentiment,
and her hero, 6ft. lin. in height, who rescues
a child from a burning house in quite the
approved heroic manner, is the feminised
ideal of a young girl; but there is real sen-
timent and a touch of true pathos in the
innocuous romance. The plot is simple:
the story is told by the hackneyed method
of letters written to an unknown girl, and
the denouement is, even to the inexperienced
reader, early foreseen; but the writing is ad-
mirable and the extravagances femininely
pardonable. In this popular class of femi-
nine fiction The Dream Girl stands high.
The book is Myrtle Reidish, of -course ; but
wij:h an Australian difference. From it it is
evident that Gertrude Hart will some day
write. It may innocently be added that
the author-, a young girl, was regarded till
recently as a hopeless invalid. For seven
years she had never stood on her feet. Some
friends called in the aid of a skilled Mel-
bourne surgeon, and on the day she got the
first copy of her book she was able to walk
across the room. Complete recovery is only
a question of time. One reads this sort of
thing in clever publishers’ announcements;
but this is not a publisher’s announcement.

ANTHONY HORDERNS’ FINE ART GALLERY
CERTAINLY the most notable addition to the many features that are presented at ANTHONY HORDERNS’
NEW PALACE EMPORIUM is the FINE ART GALLERY. This new section of our enterprise represents
collections of wares in varied branches of art, which appeal to the connoisseur, and all who have an innate
love for the beautiful. Many of the articles are eminently suitable for Gifts, being pleasing examples of the com-
bination of artistic talent with industrial enterprise.
The New Department may be regarded as a permanent exhibition of the Fine Arts, ranging from genuine
Bronzes, and exquisite French Enamels, Cut Crystal Glassware, to Pottery and Porcelain, which embraces many Fine
pieces of Irreproachable Style and Beauty in Royal Doulton, Royal Worcester, Royal Crown Derby, Coalport, Cope-
land, Minton, Wedgwood, Belleek, Royal Copenhagen, Lancastrian, Bernard Moore, Gouda, and Austrian Terra
Cotta Ware.
Merit in design and craftmanship entitles these artistic present-day productions to stand cheek-by-jowl with the
Antiques. The Fame of the Fine Art Department has extended far and wide. Visitors are cordially invited to
inspect the art treasures that have been created by the handiwork and genius of man. All articles are marked in
plain figures, at prices which are extremely moderate, and no one is importuned to buy.
ART TERRA COTTA, WARE
Comprising' Statuary, Groups, Vases, Fig'ures,
etc.
The artistic excellence of this collection shown in the FINE ART GALLERY
is of the highest order. The range on view contains many classical and
other popular subjects, all finely modelled, each piece being a signed replica.
, The following are very attractive specimens and remarkable value at the prices quoted.
LA FORCE. —A group piece of exceptional merit, finished
as bronze. The modelling of the lion, held in check by the
gladiator, armed only with a trident, is a wonderfully strong
piece of work. The rough and rugged’ base adds strength to

Business, Robbery, etc.
Of late, Melbourne cargoes from overseas have been
broached so systematically that there is evidently
an organised scheme of robbery afoot. From the
Themistocles a carrying firm humped away, among
other goods, a case alleged to contain several gross
of razors. It was apparently as intact as when the
British packer nailed the lid on, and the brands
were all correct; but nary a razor was inside. An-
other case, of cutlery, contained only 14 packages,
when it should have held 48. A sweeter instance of
nice attention to detail was given when the Ballarat
was opened out. A case of gloves, shipped in Eng-
land, was missing, and the ship’s officers and the
agents were marvelling how it had been carried away
holus bolus, when a motor-body importer _ casually
mentioned that one of his packages, which came
by the same vessel, held fragments of another case
containing cardboard boxes and an odd glove or two.
The thieves had presumably wrecked the case m
opening it, and, fearing early discovery had gone
to the trouble of opening up a motor-body case and
bestowing the wreckage therein. Somebody must
have had plenty of time and opportunity to do the
job in the ship’s hold.
“Billy T.” :It was in Bourke-street. He looked
like Dave down for the Cup, with all his imperfec-
tions and inexperience upon his head. Apparently he
was from Toobeelook, a district to which civilisation
penetrates with difficulty, and where the fashions are
always 15 years late. He walked wide, as if ac-
customed to accommodating a draught horse between
his knees, and he put his heel down first, afterwards
hitting the pavement a resounding slap with the flat
of his paddle. The party with him seemed more ac-
customed to halls of light and haunts of men ; com-
paratively speaking, he was a ball of style. the
latter gentleman was pointing out the gay exterior
of the Theatre Royal to his angular companion,
when the detective walked in between. Here,
sonny,” he said, “you from the wild woods, do you
know this man?” “No,” said Dave, “I dunno him
much.” “Has lie had anything from you? Cnpes,
no!” “Get out. Plaster,” the D. added, addressing
the superior person, “or I’ll put you up for a few
months.” Plaster got out. Then the officer addressed
a few wise, easily-remembered mottoes to the yob
from Toobeelook, ' and bade him beware of kindly,
well-spoken strangers with affluent relatives m rlji
and France. “Cripes!” said Dave, and he waddled
off like a disturbed duck in a puddle. (Not much
in this item— except that Dave is really the bigger
take-down of a bright pair, and is a Collmgwood
native who has never seen Teddywaddy or Toolum-
blook in the whole course of his life. His duty is
to inveigle the back-blocker and win his verdant
affections before the arrival of the Croesus of the
Pacific. After which David is a fellow-sufferer with
the genuine bucolic, whom he subsequently dissuades
from taking action that might lead to the recovery
of their missing money. He is a most successful
operative. As a rule, what the police don t know
is worth looking into.)
“Y Z.”: There have been complaints lately in and
about Toorak, South Yarra, St. Hilda and adjacent
places, of alleged domestics taking office in the after-
noon, staying over-night, and disappearing in the
grey dawn. The happening is so frequent that Chief
Commissioner O’Callaghan is expected to invite an
intelligent officer to investigate and report. this
is not necessary. Flossy, and Fay, and Fanny, and
the other members of the somewhat-soiled sisterhood,
have awakened to the Met that, when down to it and
sorely pressed for bread and board, immediate relief
can be secured bv taking a place as household or gen-
eral” with Mrs. Brown-Smith, enjoying a couple of
meals and a good bed, and then getting out bright
and early when domestic duties are almost due. A
few forged or borrowed, credentials are the only
stock-in-trade required _in the effective working of
this little confidence trick.

The S. M. Herald enthusing about the recent
manufacturers’ week: “There is little between blan-
kets and pianofortes that we do not make, anc * a
special word is perhaps due to several most credit-
able displays of agricultural machinery— as good as
could be seen anywhere in the world. In extending
domestic industry we are providing _ work for the
worker under the best possible conditions. Our * ac "
tory legislation leaves very little to be desired, and
the worker is, in nearly all cases, assured healthy
and reasonable conditions of employment. _As he
also takes very good care to assure himself good
wages and short hours, we may at least have the
satisfaction of knowing that the growth of manufac-
ture is not doing anyone any harm.” Shades of
Cobden! For, behold! the manufacturer’s advt.
waxeth fat on the copy file and maketh many con-
versions.
“Inchiquin”: There seems to be a swindle some-
where. The Australian farmer pays 6id. for his
wheat bag. When the farmer sells his wheat the
buyer weighs the bag in. That is to say, the farmer
gets paid for the bag as though it were wheat—
which means that he gets 2jd. for what has just cost
him 6id. It looks a swindle, doesn’t it? Are the
Departments of Agriculture inquiring into this mat-
ter of costly wheat bags, by the way ?
A LAY OF FINAL MUTTON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Taking all things into account, there seem to be
wonderful times ahead for the thickly peopled coun-
tries, with still-growing populations, which depend
almost wholly on foreigners for their daily loaf and
their hourly sausage.— The Bulletin.
Room for the Sheep! The heralds blew
Their brazen music down the street;
The people, fed on pumpkin stew,
Gazed on the last fair thing to eat;
The splendid mutton walked in pride,
Bedecked with ribbon, lace and frill,
And armies marched on either side
To save from shame the King’s last grill.
The memories of ancient beef
Were still the poet’s thrilling theme —-
The cow that faded as a leaf
And left the world devoid of cream.
He sang the rooster on the spit,
The fatted swine foredoomed to fry,
And told how ancient molars bit
The meat that filled the crusted pie.
Drear was the cabbage of content,
And sadly drooped the daily bean,
The salad’s Verdant hope was spent,
The turnip’s top was coldly green.
With longing eyes the people saw
The wool-clad food their sires had known,
The last fat cutlets walking raw
To fill the larder of a throne.
On jewelled feet the jumbuck stept,
The heralds blew a louder blast,
As down their whiskers all men wept
To know it was the very last.
Forlorn of meat the sad world lay,
And statesmen’s eyes were pained to see
The final mutton pass away
Like blossoms from the dinner tree.
Room for the Sheep ! The trumpets sang,
And like a solemn passing knell
A heavy-visaged butler rang
The brazen palace dinner-bell.
Boom for the Sheep ! With parsley curled
About it in a verdant ring,
The last brown brisket in the world
Was set in pomp before the King!
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Superman.
The moulded thoughts of die-cast men
Are hampering my brain ;
They bend the bridge-planks of my ken
Almost to breaking strain.
With law-made, “musts” and moral “don’ts”
That guard each ancient lie,
They seek to chain the strong “I won’ts”
And strangle what is I.
The welded ’ism, the steel-clamped ’ist,
The long-established law —
Ho ! how the little thoughts insist
The big are stuffed with straw.
But ’ism is kith, and ’ist is friend,
The law mv brothers’ place.
How shall I strive for each one’s end,
Yet smile in each one’s face?
The seeming right of yesterday
To-day is proven wrong •
And he who blazes first the way
To-morrow is a throng.
But will the trailers compensate
For old friends tried and true?
My mate was none the less my mate
Because he scorned my view.
Nay, more ! A brave antagonist,
He forced me with dissent,
And at the end must co-exist
In the accomplishment.
For I am not a mere machine
Of unconsidered cost;
I am the is of what has been,
Of men who won and lost.
And as they fought so shall I fight
That haply, as I live,
The supermen may bring to light
The grand superlative.
QUEENSLAND.
E. S. EMERSON.
The Song of To-day.
Sing me a sonnet of sad to-day,
Orpheus, sing me a sonnet, sing,
One that will shatter the idol’s clay,
Orpheus, swiftly the lyre string ;
Man is enmeshed in a thousand schemes,
Trammeled and bound by the “trust” and
“ring” ;
Mammon is God where the woman screams —
Orpheus, sing me a sonnet, sing.
This is the knowledge that men have found,
Sought and found in the vigils lone,
Hemmed by ethics, convention bound,
Deeply bred in the human bone,
Lieth the savagery known of old.
Latent, but biding its time to spring,
Hoarding its strength is the sabre cold.
Orpheus, sing me a sonnet, sing.
Bone of the mammoth with woad and spear,
All have passed in the ages’ van,
Slumbering under the thin veneer,
Lieth the old primeval man;
Power to ravage and power to loot,
Arm for javelin, sword or sling;
Lo, degenerate man, the brute —-
Orpheus, sing me a sonnet, sing.
Clash and clamor of arms; the field
Quivers beneath the squadron’s tread,
Cries I hear where the stricken yield
Life to the blood-birds overhead,
Unto their heritage, pride of race,
Here at thine altar. Mars, they cling;
Stiffening sinew and blanching face.
Orpheus, sing me a sonnet, sing.
These they have given for meed of years,
These for the spans they hold in trust,
Gold they’ve garnered mid siahs and tears,
Souls encased in a cankering lust;
Sing me a sonnet: the world is red ;
Still they to Moloch the off’ring fling,
Children are reared in the bouse of Dread,
Orpheus, sing me a sonnet, sing.
Hallowed by peace in the a ttic bare;
Sing me a sonnet, magician, sing;
Over the crowd and its earking care,
Sing till the hills at their bases swing;
Tamed and led by the trusting child,
I to thy shrine. Madonna, bring,
Meek as the Nazarene, man the mild—
Orpheus, sing me a sonnet, sing.
N.s.w.
L.A.R.
To a Boy Lover.
You are as young as spring leaves barely
green,
As young as frisking lambs but lately born.
As tender grass up-springing in the morn.
You are so young, you cannot reach the
heights
Of love, nor feel the sadness that it brings,
You are a butterfly on trembling wings
That lives on sun and air, and careless takes
The bloom and dew of dawns white magic
wrought,
Nor gives wherefrom it takes a passing
thought.
You are too young for sorrow, life to you
Is but a blossom-tree whereon birds sing.
And death but leaves you gravely wonder-
ing —
And I would place my hand upon your head,
And bless you for the tender boyish way
You loved me—for a year, and for a day.
VICTORIA.
G.J.
The Awakening.
Oat of my dreams I come wide-eyed and
strange,
For I have seen the magic cities sleeping.
All blue and silver ’neath the crystal range
Above whose summits vast god-thoughts
are keeping
The everlasting watch of time and change.
The world of streets and houses tall and grey
Floats dimly by me with its loud wheels
turning.
To-morrow faints with vanished yesterday,
And in my heart a larger light is burning
Of some clear sun that shall not pass away.
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and

Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some cure as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
epen loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice. •
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political. Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short 'Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications ivill be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
imless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,

illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any pic-
ture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS,

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 21, 1912.
THE GENTLE ART OF ANTI-
REFERENDUMING.
Last year’s Referendum campaign was char-
acterised by some fairly strenuous lying, but,
in this respect, the struggle which has just
begun promises to beat its predecessor hol-
low. A writer in Sydney Telegraph’s com-
mercial column has shown what we may ex-
pect. In the course of a brief three-quarters
of a column last Friday, he committed him-
self to a collection of mis-statements and
absurdities calculated to alternately stagger
and convulse the merest hedge-lawyer. The
tragedy from Australia’s standpoint lies in
the fact that there are many thousands of
Telegraph readers —commercial men (no-
toriously the most illiterate class, in many
respects, in the community), women, par-
sons, squatters, surf-bathers, Empire-
leaguers, bar-ioafers, temperance enthusiasts,
Christian Scientists, vegetarians, and others
too numerous to mention—who know even
less of law, more especially in its bearing on
political issues, than the callowest of hedge-
lawyers. The besotted ignorance of the
many constitutes the opportunity of the few
whose business it is to defeat, at any price,
the Australian Government’s efforts to con-
trol injurious monopolies, institute the New
Protection, and do generally those things
which it was put in power to do. The
Telegraph’s performance exemplifies inter-
estingly the lines on which it is hoped to
repeat the confidence-trick that came off
with such success in April, 1911.
Here is the easily demonstrable untruth
with which the paper began its remarks: —
The Commonwealth has always had the power to
legislate for the good government of banks, insurance
offices, and trading and financial companies —the last
covering a wide range—as well as for foreign com-
panies. But those powers are not enough for our
present Federal administrators.
The simple answer is that the Common-
wealth has not, and never has had, the
powers mentioned. If it had them, the
present Ministry would not be risking its
political life in the attempt to get the people
to concede them. The Commonwealth can
touch only such institutions as operate in
more than one State. But the concerns
which fulfil this condition are too few in
numbers almost to be worth discussing. In
1907 P. M. Glynn, afterwards Attorney-
General, prepared a memorandum, dealing
with monopolies, for the Deakin Govern-
ment. He pointed out that 33 monopolies
had been reported to the Crown Law Depart-
ment as existing in Australia. Amongst
the articles thus mishandled were bricks,
kerosene, tobacco, confectionery, manures,
trucks, shoe machinery, flour, meat, jam,
coal, timber, sugar, etc.; and he contended that
their proceedings had had a powerful effect
in raising the cost of living. “The majority
of these,” he went on—“in fact, 29 or 30
out of the 33—are within the jurisdiction of
the States 0n1y.... and none can be com-
pletely dealt with without co-operation of
the States, or an amendment of the Consti-
tution.” It is pretty certain that the elec-
tors of Australia did not intend that this
should be so. The framers of the Constitu-
tion never anticipated any such ridiculous
anomaly. Moreover, Parliament firmly be-
lieved, when it passed its various Anti-Trust
measures, that it had “power to legislate
for the good government of banks, insurance
offices, and trading and financial com-
panies.” But the High Court decided other-
wise. In Appleton’s case the usual
conservative majority of the Bench laid it
down that, by virtue of the States’ right,
under the Trade and Commerce clause, to
completely control their own internal busi-
nesses, it was not allowable for the Federal
Parliament to interfere in the remotest de-
gree with any trading or financial company
which had once commenced operations. As
the High Court interpreted the Constitu-
tion, the only thing that Parliament could
do was to prohibit a dangerous-looking cor-
poration from starting. All those rings and
trusts which are safely under way at the
present time are as immune from legislative
attack, so long as they confine their depre-
dations to one State, as so many dead Arch-
bishops.
* * * *
Giving direct mis-statement a miss in
baulk, so to speak, the Telegraph passed
on to an idiotic form of objection or argu-
ment or what not that was extremely popu-
lar 18 months ago with Tory orators wher-
ever a few palpably uneducated and foolish
electors were gathered together. A frag-
ment :
The added powers cover all companies, including
mines. What is the use of this State legislating for
gas companies if they are to pass to the control of
the Federal Parliament ? Then the trade in such
companies in the purchase of coal and in the sale of
gas, coke, and so on, and the labor they employ, .
will all be under Federal control, with the right to
declare them monopolies, to take them over, and
dissolve them. We are not at all clear how far the
powers of the Federal Government will extend. Take
the Silverton tramway, the Deniliquin and Moama
railway, the Chillagoe railway, the Emu Bay railway
and various others —they, as corporations, not belong-
ing to the State Government, will pass under the
control of the Federal Government, which may dis-
solve them, and by declaring them monopolies take
them over and run thetn in opposition to the State
lines.
As this paper pointed out, until it was tired,
during the last Referendum campaign, there
is all the difference" in the world between
what a Government may do, and what, so
long as it remains sane, it is likely to do.
There is a Labor Government in N. S. Wales
at the present time, and it has held office
for some years. Within its own sphere its
power is absolute. Legally speaking, it
might, at any time during its career, have
nationalised the Hotel Australia, the Daily
Telegraph Newspaper Co., or a George-
street oyster saloon ; it might have passed a
law insisting on the removal of Dr. Ward’s
whiskers, in which case nothing but flight
would have saved the famous face-covers;
it might have forced everybody to be washed
14 times a day, or contrariwise have for-
bidden anyone to wash at all, or to brush his
teeth, or drink anything but gin and cloves.
Why has the McGowen Administration done
none of these things? For the simple reason
that, with all its multitudinous defects and
weaknesses, it is not composed of lunatics.
Its members are not searching for unneces-
sary trouble. They are on a good wicket.
The toil is light and the pay satisfac-
tory. And they are there, strictly, as the
old phrase goes, quarn diu se bene gesserint.
One really demented or inequitable move, and
they would inevitably be bumped roughly
out of place and power by the scandalised
electors. For precisely the same reasons, the
Federal Government, whatever powers are
conferred on it, is quite certain not to seize
the Silverton tramway, the Deniliquin and
Moama railway, and all the rest; just as the
State Government may be relied on not to
expropriate and nationalise every fruit-bar-
row and grocery and butchery of Australia.
Enough has been said to indicate some of
the impudent means by which the Anti-
Referenda case is seemingly to be buttressed
during the next few months. Part of the
propagandist value of such plain lying and
embroidered misrepresentation arises from the
depressing fact that Fortune leans for so
long to the side of, the courageous and fluent
liar; part from the stupidity of electors, and
their unwillingness to get the hang of any
political ouestion which involves any mental
effort. Between now and polling-day the
Nationalist section will be called upon un-
ceasingly to answer mendacities. The pur-
veyors of the shoddy goods have, roughly,
about 40 newspapers to present them in. for
every on the other side. They have
more money wherewith to hire halls, public-
house balconies, stumps, and so on for the
purposes of having their misrepre°“ntations
distributed by word of mouth. The intel-
lectual resource of their leaders is consider-
able. Almost invariably, last year, the
moment one Anti-Referepda untruth was
felled, another equally bright lie arose and
got to work. If the day is to be gained for
Australia and honest trade, one matter is
clear: an educative campaign must be
started at once. Also, it must be of the most
strenuous description, and its organisers
must stick to business right up to the
moment the issues are decided.
THE FALL OF THE CITY.
Fob the third time in history a Bulgarian
army is at the gates of Constantinople. In
813, and again a century later, the people
whose guns now threaten the old capital of
the Grecian world demanded entrance. In
times past Goths, Avars, Saracens, Crusa-
ders and Turks besieged the place and smote
it with the horror and dread which only an
invested city may know; but it is doubtful
whether ever in all its long history it has
been filled with such an agony of fear as
rends it at this present moment. At any
hour the shells may begin to shriek; at any
hour the maddened people may begin their
orgies of massacre and rapine. The terror
within is even greater than the horror with-
out, and on the appearance of things, all
Europe’s boasted civilisation cannot stay the
tide, or offer protection to the weak and
helpless. Normally, the city has over a
million people; but this throng has been
swelled by an influx of refugees, and by a
horde of defeated soldiers whose miseries
may cause them to throw off even the ap-
pearance of discipline, and from whose eyes,
hard with famine, looks forth an insanity
of fanaticism which demands the solace of
blood. *
It would be very little different with any
other great and overcrowded city should an
enemy knock at its door. The picture of
London or New York invested by alien
armies is almost too awful for the imagina-
tion to dwell upon. The great fear in the
hearts of the starving multitudes, the blood-
lust loose in street and alley, the shrapnel
shrieking through the smoke, all bonds of
restraint broken and every outlet of safety
and refuge closed, the pitiful helplessness of
the masses, the pitiless death ever creeping
nearer and nearer —these are the nightmares
which afflict statesmen in their sleep.
Wherever in the past the red foot of war
has been planted such things have been, and
the past is the only guide to the future. All
the growth of humanitarian feeling is coun-
terbalanced by the tremendous advance in
engines of destruction. Before the march
of armies and the onrush of fighting fleets,
kindly human sentiment fades out, and the
primal savage asserts himself for a red
hour. To talk- of civilised warfare is to
waste breath. No war is civilised when
once the bullets sing; and though there may
be degrees in its horror, the best that it has
to show is enough to make the safest peoples
grave with fearful anticipation.
* * * *
Constantinople, which now rests under
the black shadow, has lung been regarded as
a place of great strategic importance. Its
value may have been overestimated, but
the fact remains that the place is tremen-
dously desired by half a dozen European
nations, who regard it as among the greatest
of territorial prizes. But it does not stand
alone as the first and only prize. The strat-
egy of modern warfare must give highest
place to the question of food supply, and
the outlets of food supply are the places
upon which most of all the world’s eyes of
greed and envy burn. To-day, Australia’s
cities are the food-gates of largest possi-
bility. Behind them lies the widest area of
productive and undeveloped land; and the
statesmen of many nations, both Asiatic
and European, are fully aware of their
immense value. It is no matter of doubt or
controversy, but a plain fact, that the strat-
egic importance of a Constantinople or Gib-
raltar 50, or even 20, years ago is equalled
by that of Sydney at the present hour. The
future is with the nation which holds the
food supply, and the key to that food supply
is something for which nations may be pre-
pared to take enormous risks.. At any
moment —for war moves with speed these
days—the doom may be upon us and the
horrors of Luleh Burgas and Chorlu may
be repeated at Darlinghurst and St. Peters.
Bulgar and Turkish corpses that block the
icy river-bars with their piled and festering
horror are far away, and the trembling re-
fugees in Stamboul can rouse only a distant
pity; but the cause of war—the chief and
first cause of all wars—is here in Australia
in the form of an unprepared nation and a
territory of enormous value. In six or seven
weeks Sydney could be in the condition of
Constantinople ; and, as far as we can judge,
we are about as well prepared to meet the
emergency as a mob of Quakers, armed to
the teeth with the profits of the cocoa trade
and a faith in the efficacy of prayer.
DEMOCRACY AND THE PARTY
GOVERNMENT SWINDLE.
Thomas Carlyle held that the secret of
“best government” was to find the ablest
man and put him in supreme command. Un-
fortunately, the selection of the ablest man
is a matter of such difficulty that life itself
is usually too short for the business of sift-
ing him out; and somewhat able men, of
whom there are many, have so many weak-
nesses, so much shortness of vision in so
many directions, and such a lack of the
blood, honey and iron of the supreme leader,
that Democracy finds it necessary to arrange
a compromise and elect a, number of such
men to do what might conceivably be best
done by one gigantic personality. Yet the
idea of securing the is never wholly
absent, and the ballot-box is a machine
specially devised to enable the united intelli-
gence of the country to select the fittest and
send them to frame public laws and conduct
public business. The health of the body
politic depends upon the true and faithful
working of that machine, and anything
which interferes with it must be a menace
to the community.
* * * *
Australia makes its boast that it possesses
one of the most complete democratic sys-
tems in the world. Every man and woman
has a right to a free voice in the selection of
those who are to make laws for the nation.
Every man in the Commonwealth has a
right to offer his services, and to be chosen
or rejected by the free vote of the electors
In theory at least, Australia should have its
very best political ability always at com-
mand ; but, unfortunately, Party Govern-
ment is on hand to twist and warp the re-
sults. Nominally, any man may stand for
Parliament; practically only those selected
by small party rings and associations have
any hope. To say that the various leagues
and committees that make the choice select
the fittest men to serve the various consti-
tuencies, is to speak a vain thing which is
against all evidence of recent years. If Aus-
tralia can find no better representatives than
those put forward by party to fight the partv
battle, the country must be strangely lacking
in men of sincere purpose and strong intel-
lect. As a plain fact, the amount of petty
intrigue and string-pulling necessary to
obtain party nomination affords almost a
guarantee that the best men will not be
chosen. Under an open system, free from
party influence, the people would at least
have wider choice and some reasonable hope
of electing the best men offering; under the
party system by which democracy is bound
and gagged, there is a big and ever growing
chance of electing men who are as unfit to
conduct the affairs of the nation as a Syd-
ney landlord to sit on a decentralisation
commission.
Further, the system of party selection
makes it possible for the incapable legisla-
tor and political waster to make a fairly
good showing on a platform. Men who could
never think out a serious political problem
for themselves, who have a superb ignorance
of economic questions, history and affairs
and who possess neither opinions nor princi-
ples,_ can talk an audience into a moribund
condition in elaborating the planks of a plat-
form supplied them ready-made. That plat-
form is the growth of years; and each in-
capable, taking his cue from the few
stronger minds of the organisation (many of
whom cannot themselves secure selection as
candidates), acquires by constant practice
a glib facility in expounding a few stere-
otyped ideas which may often pass for
ability and the voicing of deep convictions.
Outside that platform his mind is a blank ;
and were he compelled to state a policy of
his own, and give a reason for the faith that
is in him, he would be known of all men
for the fool he is. That very test which
would reveal the strong man of grip and
knowledge is the one which party
system the electors cannot apply.
* * * *
Party Government thus adds to its sins
in the direct field of legislation and adminis-
tration the crime of robbing democracy of
that freedom of choice which alone can give
it health and vitality. It takes away the
citizen’s right to offer his services to the
nation, and stand or fall by the opinion the
electors may form of his integrity and
ability. It corrupts and confuses issues by
building up large professions of political
faith, about many of whose articles there
is room for infinite diversity of conscien-
tious opinion, and imposing those professions
in their entirety upon every member off the
party. In a word, it takes away the rights
of the citizen, both as elector and candidate,
and warps and narrows the free political
thought of the nation. As a return for all
this, it offers the public an occasional reform
after long and vexatious delays and hun-
dreds of stupid and utterly useless compro-
mises which satisfy no one. The formation
of a new party is often talked of, and occa-
sionally is justified under existing condi-
tions ; but no permanent remedy for the ex-
isting evils is to be found that way. You
cannot cure a cancer by grafting a tumor
upon it, or keep foxes out of a fowlyard by
introducing ferrets. Party Government is
too serious a disease for a palliative. It
is not merely crooked and deformed; it is
venomous and destroying, and the people of
this Commonwealth will not know what true
democratic government is until they refuse
any longer to keep the animal on the
premises.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Jubilee of Lost Opportunities.
The city of Bathurst (N.S.W.) celebrated
its jubilee last week. There was a proces-
sion through the streets, and several of the
industrial and historical features of the
place were symbolised after the customary
fashion. The organisers omitted, somehow,
to present the figure of a Geebung riding
on a rail; there was no representation of a
typical N. S. Wales Ministry being boiled
in oil; there wasn’t even a set piece casting
scorn on the differential railway rate system
which has done so much to make Bathurst
the stagnant place it is. Admirable good-
feeling and generosity this; or perhaps it
only signified lack of capacity to appreciate
the infamously selfish way in which Bath-
urst has been treated by those who have
made it their business to bloat Sydney. In
the dim past Bathurst promised to be some-
thing considerable in the manufacturing
line. A fiscal policy framed with an eye to
the interests of the Sydney importers killed
all hopes in that direction; and when the
Federal tariff, for what it was worth, came
into operation, a railway policy, framed
largely by Sydney for Sydney, slew Bath-
urst’s prospects some more. The Year
Book of Austbalia (1900 and 1911)
shows that during the 10 years in which
the population of Ashfield grew from 11,607
to 14,329, of Canterbury from 2426 to 4226,
of Balmain from 23,475 to 30,076. of Mar-
rickville from 13,507 to 18,775, and of other
Sydney suburbs in proportion, Bathurst
swelled with painful gradualness from 9162
to 9223 —a matter of 61 inhabitants. De-
spite the extraordinary prosperity of recent
years, and despite the fact that the different
State Governments have been deluging Aus-
tralia with immigrants, so that house-
famines are raging in most of the big coastal
cities. Bathurst stands more or less where
it did. The bulk of the business which
ought to be done locally is done in Sydney
and environs, with the natural result that
the district loses not only cash but those
human assets which it can least afford to
sacrifice. This is the ingenuous way a
Sydney morning paper put it a day or so
ago:—
A good many of the bright boys of the up-country
towns come down to Sydney, naturallv enough. What
else are they to do? Sydney is growing so much

And with quaint fatuousness the journal
proceeded to applaud the efforts of the coun-
try technical college, inasmuch as it “at
least helps a young fellow to come down to
fhe metropolis with some wage-earning
skill.” It is of no use whatever to places
like Bathurst that their bright young men
should be systematically drained from them
into “the metropolis.” In fact, to avert
that calamity they ought to be prepared to
fight like Bulgars. One means to the end
is the formation of a genuine Country
Party—an organisation pledged to battle
for the backblocks as against the Port
Jackson octopus, irrespective of the two
more or less fraudulent factions which are
represented in Macquarie-street. Such a
body would oppose any candidate who didn’t
declare for decentralisation and the imme-
diate opening up of the starved ports of
N. S. Wales. It would make railway • re-
form one of its planks—reform in the mat-
ter of the swift transport of stock and pro-
duce to the nearest coastal outlet, and re-
form in the matter of freights and fares.
In brief, it would aim at making the aver-
age country centre go ahead, in the way of
prosperity and population, as fast, at least,
as the average cheap Sydney suburb. Pend-
ing its arrival the bright youths of places
like Bathurst will flee from them, for there
will be nothing else for blight youths to do.

The Farmer, and, Incidentally, His
Man.
Thebe is an uproar in Victoria amongst
the farmers, because the union of laborers
—called the Rural Workers’ Union —has
given notice that it intends to ask the Arbi-
tration Court to make an award. This in-
formation has had about the same effect on
the farmers as a kettle of boiling water
poured down an ants’ nest has on the ants.
In the midst of the commotion a union has
been formed; in fact, several unions, the
most important being the Rural Producers’
Association. A characteristic meeting of
one of these bodies has been held at Nhill,
which is the heart of the Wimmera wheat-
growing area. It considered the demands of
the Workers’ Union, and met them with
something like dynamite. The workers, for
example, asked for eight hours a day, or
48 hours per week. The “producers” re-
plied with “60 hours in harvest, 50 in win-
ter.” The “producers” went right through
the rest of the schedule, and decided to op-
pose every item but one, which related to
time and a half for holiday work. Now,
what do they think they will gain by this
mulish attitude? The truth is, farmers, as
a class, need to get a few stupid notions out
of their heads. For instance: (1) The
notion that the farm hand is an unavoid-
able nuisance, to be “kept in his place”;
(2) the notion that the world, and all that
is in it, was made solely for the farmer;
(3) the notion that the farmer isn’t a
‘‘worker” himself. In place of those old
ideas, which are decayed, the following are
offered: (1) The farm hand, if well paid
and looked after, will more than pay for
his wages; (2) the farm hand is a man,
just the same as the farmer himself; (3)
the “worker,” instead of being the farmer’s
enemy, is his best customer; (4) better
wages and conditions for labor in the coun-
try have got to come. The farmer, having
to sell most of his wheat in the world’s
market, complains that he cannot raise
prices to meet increased expenditure. But
he overlooks the fact that prices have raised
themselves of recent years, and promise to
raise themselves still more in the future.
He forgets, also, that the price of land is
always moving up. And therein lies more
than half the trouble. Thousands upon thou-
sands of men are land-jobbers first and
farmers after —a long way after. They are
more concerned about pushing up the mar-
ket value of land than getting a living by
cultivating it. Aiming thus at dear land,
they are almost of necessity driven to bar-
rack for cheap labor. So the thing goes on :
Brown sweats his labor, shows a big profit,
and sells out at an extravagant price, know-
ing quite well that he will not be able to
sweat the labor indefinitely. Smith, who
buys, is almost driven to practise sweating
because of the extravagant price he has
paid. High-nriced land is the major curse ;
and that farmer who, being a land-jobber
firs!,, aims at high prices is the enemy of
-the industry, inasmuch as he is doing some-
thing that tends to drive good workmen out
of the business. The general community
has a lot of kindly feeling for the farmer ;
but it and every court or other authority
which handles the farmer’s affairs will
blunder very seriously if it doesn’t distin-
guish between the farmer who is primarily
a land-jobber, or a would-be land-jobber, and
the one who is essentially a producer, aim-
ing to make a living not by selling land but
by using it. The former’s schemes can’t be
discouraged with too sharp or heavy an axe ;
the legitimate farmer, on the other hand,
deserves to have his bed made very com-
fortable.
A Foreign Minister’s Limp Back.
Minister for Foreign Affairs Thomas,
whose supineness in the Poon Gooey affair
made the White Australia policy look ab-
surd, is up against more cases of a similar
kind. The bland Chinee, trying hard to get
some of his womenfolk in here, is ready with
all sorts of artful dodges, A Chow woman
recently arrived in Australia to meet her
husband at’Bendigo. Josiaii Thomas gave
her permission to stay for three months, be-
cause, “if he had not granted this permis-
sion, she would have had to go back by the
boat in which she arrived” —the precise thing
she should have been requested to do. When
the three months had expired somebody
asked that she be allowed to stay six months,
as had been done in other cases. The Min-
ister again meekly gave in. Then, later,
came the doctor’s certificate that the woman
could not travel, and, of course, six months
extra was asked for. And Thomas, finding
himself now in a cleft stick, practically had
to say Yes—all through his slack adminis-
tration to begin with. There is another case
in which John was told that he could bring
out his wife. Instead, he brought out some
other woman and somebody else’s daughter.
At time of writing the doctor’s certificate
stage has been reached in this flagrant case
also; likewise, foolish people were writing to
say that the ends of purity or something
would be frayed if the Minister drove the
lady away. Certainly the Minister says he
is going to be firm; but it begins to look as
though it will be necessary to freeze him
first.
Honorary Hospital Management.
The inquiry into the management of Mel-
bourne’s Infectious Diseases Hospital still
wanders along. The committee goes on de-
clining to be represented at the inquiry, or
to give evidence on its own behalf, or to
admit that it is in any way concerned in the
matter. It is nothing, it seems, to these
Bumbles to have witnesses swear that at
the institution under their control the food
is poor in quality, badly cooked and scarce,
and is “thrown” to patients; that bed linen
and clothing are served out unwashed and
dirty; that patients have been admitted as
diphtheritic when repeated serum tests gave
negative results; that patients have been
discharged as cured, and have straightway
conveyed infection to others; that children
who cried for their mothers were smacked ;
that little, dying toddlers, crying through the
night, were unheeded, and were only soothed
on the long journey by older child-patients;
that patients were operated on without the
knowledge of parents, and bodies post-
mortemed without the consent of relatives ;
that the system of isolation was a farce, and
the protection from infection a burlesque;
that patients were taken in suffering from
measles merely, and put into the same beds
with scarlet fever cases—-that, in fact, the
whole place was. run with less care, less
method, less consideration for relatives and
less humanity than an average slaughter-
house or pigeon-shooting match. There
shouldn’t be much -trouble about deciding
what should be done with that committee.

N.S.W. State Government last week laid
up trouble for itself in the P.L.L. Confer-
ence when it forced through the second read-
ing of the Bill to assist the Broken Hill
Prop. Co. to establish iron and steel works
at Newcastle. No less than 14 Labor mem-
bers broke away, despite the fact that the
Bill had been considered in caucus, and had
the Liberals not come to the rescue of the
Government it would have been defeated by
its own followers. That there will be a row
over it among the Labor leagues is certain.
In the first place, it is the Labor policy to
establish State iron and steel works; and,
though the Government says the establish-
ment of private works will not bar the State
from going into the industry, it is dead-cer-
tain that Delprat would never have recom-
mended his co. to embark if he hadn’t got a
more or less definite' assurance that the
Ministry would do nothing. And on top of
that is the fact that the B. 11. Prop, treated
the Labor section so scurvily a while ago,
shutting down the mine for two years rather
than obey an Arbitration award to pay cer-
tain wages—wages which all the other mines'
found themselves well able to pay. That the
Labor Government should give a hand to
such a co. to defeat or even delay an im-
portant feature of the Labor policy is
naturally puzzling the leagues, which will
certainly want to know what is the milk in
the cocoanut.
On the same day that the cable steamer
Silvertown was to begin laying the new
1200-mile Sydney-Auckland cable, news came
by another deep-sea feeder of newspapers
that a San Francisco wireless station, on a
recent clear night, had overheard the flying
gossip of steamers in the North Atlantic,
8000 miles away. Last • week Reuter’s re-
ceived first news of the capitulation of
Nazim Pasiia, commander-in-chief of the
main Turkish army, by the system that can-
not be cut even by an aeroplanist. The
cable —especially the short-length cable —-
begins to look a rather faded investment in
the light of these achievements. The air
is full of shooting dictionary these days, and
the addition of another screw or something
equally unimportant may so cheapen the
wireless system that all deep»-sea cables and
land lines will look like laborious commer-
cial jokes. And when that happens, some
more of the “permanent and reproductive
assets” that have been provided with bor-
rowed money will be due for scrapping. It
looks as though there isn’t any such thing
as a permanent work of engineering nowa-
days ; wherefore no honest people will carry
out any engineering work with a loan which
hasn’t got a solid sinking fund attached to it
for the repayment of the debt.
The proposal of the N.S.W. Ministry to turn
the late Federal Government House into a
museum has roused the S. M. Herald to
such ire that it denounces museums as use-
less and cumbering ornaments, for which the
public has no desire. It pains the Tory
heart that the building has not reverted to
the State Governor, and the idea of using
chambers once sacred to vice-royalty for
storing specimens which may be viewed by
John Smith and his wife has something
about it that is particularly shocking. As
between the practical value of a museum and
of a State Governor, the general public
should have no very great difficulty in decid-
ing. At best a State Governor is merely a
survival of an order of things that has
passed away, and can never by any chance
return. To devote a whole building to him,
however, seems quite superfluous. At most
a room containing a complete array of vice-
regal exhibits should be sufficient to meet the
case. The question as between a museum
and a State Governor is thus reduced to the
simplest kind of mathematical problem. The
whole being greater than its part, a complete
collection of'specimens must be greater than
a single specimen or even a small group of
specimens. It may be true that the people
are not exactly pining for another museum,
hut it is equally true that nobody wants the
State Governor at Macquarie-street for any
purpose which is not inimical to the interests
of larger Australia.
♦
Bruce Smith has been applying a subtle
test to various Labor members, and finds
that they fail lamentably. Whenever he
gets a chance he shakes a popular politician
by the hand and tenderly feels for the corns
which, according to his theory, hard work
should have produced there. Some of them,
he tells us, have hands as soft as those of
women. But if Mr. Smith and his kind
could spare a moment from the horror of
this discovery, they might well consider an-
other little matter to which their attention
is hereby directed. The sense of touch is
one of the joys of life, and that sense is
most finely and keenly developed in the
tips of the fingers. Has Mr. Smith ever
considered the enormous price which the
worker pays through the loss of that finer
sense? It is a loss almost as bad as semi-
deafness or partial blindness; yet men are
called to suffer it for the barest livelihood,
and superior persons talk about the unskilled
labor which endures it as if it were worthy
only of the barest crust that society could
throw. When Bruce Smith has done fum-
bling politicians’ palms in pretended friend-
ship, he might ask himself whether the world
gives a decent recompense to the men who
are regularly maimed in their most delicate
senses for Bs. a day or thereabouts.

“Viuilans” : Dr. Dawson, who has been
acting Government medical officer in the
Northern Territory for a few months, has re-
turned to Melbourne and unburdened him-
self to the following effect: —
(1) The problem of attracting settlers to the
Territory is immense.
(2) There is no hope, bar Buckley's, of attract-
ing them, without a proper railway system.
(.3) The weather is hot day after day.
(4) The mosquitoes are annoying.
(5) It is too hot for navvying by whites.
(6) The cost of building the railway trunk line
by white labor would be too great. The natural
course would be to indent colored labor.
(7) The health of the residents is remarkably
good; but there are too few grandchildren of' Terri-
torians to judge whether the type would deteriorate
in the course of generations.
(8) The pioneers are a splendid lot of bushmen,
hospitable and courageous. ' <
(9) Pity there are not more of them.
As for the weather, no doubt it can be hot
in the N.T.; but the harder you wofk in
that climate the better your health. ,It is
the man who does not perspire freely and
drinks square gin all day that suffers. White
men have been navvying all along on the /Pine
Creek railway. Some have been there for
15 years. Which recalls that an ex-Terri-
torian named Coop is now in Melbourne
after a trip to Europe. He is 81, and looks
51, although he spent 37 years in the North,
droving and squatting. “The tablelands
climate is the best in the world,” he .says.
“For 20 years I never saw a doctor. Couldn’t
if I’d wanted to.” Speaking for myself,
I never had better health in my life than
during my four years in Northern Aus-
tralia. I always lived in the open, and
never had malaria or any other fever. The
transcontinental railway can be built with
white labor, and it must be, to attract set-
tlers. It is because Chows were employed
on the Pine Creek line that the country has
so few white residents to-day. Had those
5000 Chinese who were imported in the
’7o’s been white men, the Territory would
have had a different tale to tell now. It
would be madness to repeat the mistake.

“K.” gets a front view of Capital:—
I fell into bad company the other day, and dis-
covered to my surprise that my old newspaper friend
Capital was really glueing himself to the country
instead of fleeing from it. Down Warrnambool
(Vic.) way I discovered that Capital had, during
the last three years, spent £lOO,OOO on a milk
condensery that is now treating 20.000 gallons of
milk daily. A brother of his has also built a big
woollen mill, costing some £30,000 ; and at the
time of my arrival a cousin was just finishing a
fine box-making- factory for the Western district
butter people. A little further inland, near Terang, I
discovered more Capital, which dries milk into
a powder and is exporting the product. This factory
cost about £20,000. ■‘Going thoughtfully on to
Camperdown, I found a new casein factory, and
heard of another at Cobden, nine miles away. On
top of this, I heard of the start of a large bacon
factory. All of these outbreaks have occurred in the
electorate (Corangamite) of Labor-member Scullin.
If Scullin is re-elected again next year, I’m d:s-
tressed to think what Capital may do. Already, in
expectation of it, I hear that he is considering
the question of enlarging the woollen mills and the
condensery, and thus making it still harder to clear
out. In fact, I’m beginning to suspect that Capital
doesn’t want to clear out.

“Hamer” : The time-payment person—
male and female —doesn’t allow many op-
portunities of doing business to escape. The
citizen who desires to extend his liabilities
over long periods can do it in pretty nearly
anything from coffins to cough mixtures.
The t.p. funeral is now a long-standing in-
stitution ; dentists announce that teeth are
sold on extended terms ; and the latest t.p.
person I have struck is a midwife, who sup-
plies her services on the long-distance plan.
She has found the schekie so successful that
she has established a staff, and the whole con-
traption is kept busy throughout the year.
The baby bonus has afforded this enterpris-
ing S. Gamp an opportunity for advertise-
ment and profit. She has notified her
clients that as the fiver may come in handy
for many things she has decided to con-
tinue the t.p. system to those who desire it,
but that in future her charges on such terms
will be slightly increased. The Australian
has a fine opportunity now of passing his
life in the cool shadow of the promise to
pay. He may be born —heir to a substan-
tial share in the National I O U —under the
auspices of a time-payment nurse, and a
doctor who is paid on very long terms, if at
all. He will sleep in a time-payment cot,
and probably wear time-payment clothes
purchased from the obliging firm which is
willing to supply even clothes on the pay-
when-you-can system. His own furniture,
when he blossoms into manhood and matri-
mony, will be t.p. furniture, and so will his
teeth, and his periodical suits. Finally, he
will be put into a t.p. coffin and buried on
terms extending over six months to await
a t.p. resurrection. The growing ramifica-
tions of the system would seem to indicate
an amazing amount of honesty amongst the
people, or the reckless increase of credit

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On one fine but fatal morning in the early
Eocene,
Lo, a brawny Bloke set out to dig a hole;
First of men to put a puncture in the ter-
tiary green
Was this early, neolithic, human mole.
Gladsomely the toiler hefted his ungainly
wooden spade,
As he scarified the bosom of old Earth ;
And our Progress forthwith started when
his first spade-thrust was made,
While the cult called' Work, or Graft, was
given birth.
Oh, he flung the clods about him with a gay
and prideful jerk,
Did this bright and early anthropoidal
Bloke.
With the crowd that gathered, goggle-eyed,
: f . to wathh him at his work
He would crack a pleasant, prehistoric
joke.
And, they gazed at him in wonder; for the
custom of the mob,
When not occupied in inter-tribal strife,
Hitherto had been to eat, and sleep, and
hunt, and cheat, and rob —
Quite a simple and uncomplicated life.
Wherefore, being new and novel, he was
treated with respect,
This, inventor of the job of shifting sand :
And with fresh-killed meat and fruit and
furs his cave the tribesmen decked.
While his praises sounded high on ev’ry
hand.
And the chieftain bade his artist in crude
pictures to inscribe
On the shin-bone of a Dinosaurom.vth ;
“ Lo, the gods have sent a thing called Graft
to bless this happy tribe,
And a scheme of Public Works will start
forthwith .”
Ev’ry day, from early dawn till dark, the
delver labored on
Till the tribesmen grew accustomed to the
sight;
And the hunters, on their way to slay the
mud-fat mastodon,
Would delay to say he wasn’t doing right.
And the loafers from the Lower Caves, who
lived by stealing meat,
All the day around the contract used to
lurk ;
And, whene’er he paused to wipe his brow
or took time off to eat,
They would yell at him in chorus : “Aw,
git work !”
Fat and lazy fur-skin-traders —wealthy men
of such a size
That it took five hides to make them each
aves /
On their way to cheat their neighbors,
paused awhile to criticise;
Calling, “Loafer!” ev’ry time he stopped
to rest.
They no longer stocked his larder with the
trophies of the chase,
Or the neolithic substitute for beer;
For the chief said: “He’s a worker; we
must keep him in his place!”
And the bloated fur-skin-traders cried,
“Hear, hear!”
And he soon became the scapegoat and the
butt of all the tribe,
And lie dwelt within the smallest, meanest
• cave,
While the rich and idle troglodytes were
readiest to gibe,
, Till they worried him into an early grave.
Then the minstrel (and I wot he was a wise
prophetic bard,
And an anthropoid philosopher of note).
Took another mammoth shin-bone and
scratched it with his shard
In his picture-script; and this is what he
wrote: —
“Here lies the simple silly coot who first
discovered Toil,
Him who started Progress onward on her
way;
Though he didn’t get much fun from it, he
moved some tons of soil:
But, ’tis said, he never fairly earned his
pay.
Lo, this thing called Work is blessed, for it
shifts a lot of sand !
And f this Progress eases him who lives by
tricks.
But the Bloke who lumps the Bundle, down
through ev'ry age and land,
Shall be paid for harder work with, harder
kicks.”
Now that Paleolithic prophet on some sand-
stone stratum lies,
With his shin-bones of the Dinosauro-
myfh,
But the Bloke who shoves the shovel still
his thankless calling plies,
And his name is Michael Burke or
Peter Smith.
In the highwav doth he labor, in the search-
ing uublic gaze,
And he dare not pause, his aching back to
rest,
Lest he cause a howl of protest from the
trader of these days
With the large gold chain across his con-
vex vest.
Lest he cause a howl of protest from “Pro
Bono Publico,”
And Pst “Constant Reader” cry his shame
aloud,
He must keen his shovel moving—-and he
moves it all too slow
For the critics in the great White-handed
crowd.
Till they get a patent navvy with a dynamo
for head.
Or a petrol-tank for stomach, take my
word,
He’ll be ever lip again«t it who shifts sand
to earn his bread,
And the howling of the traders will be
heard.
