WHERE
EVERYBODY
GOES
which generally goes before a slump. For
a firm to supply furniture on mortgage
seems reasonable enough. The furniture
remains (unless it is burned, or the old man
breaks it up in a fit of beer) as a security
for the payment of instalments—at least it
remains till it is worn out, and then the re-
mains of it are of no value to man or beast.
But it would be very little satisfaction for
an undertaker to foreclose on a corpse
(though this sort of thing used to be done
in Ireland, and Venice once levied a tax on
dead bodies), and the time-payment nurse
might find herself in a difficulty if parents
offered to surrender the baby in liquidation
of the deferred nursing account. These
people must depend solely on the honesty of
their clients.
The following statement has been sent to
The Bulletin with a request that it may
be given due publicity:—
We, the of the Society of Friends in
Victoria, desire to re-affirra our belief that it is only
by just dealing, the recognition of human brother-
hood and the interdependence of nations that last-
ing peace can be attained, and this we believe to be
possible. It is our conviction that all preparation for
war engenders distrust and separation, weakens the
sense of brotherhood, and, in place of just dealing,
sets up reliance upon force. As followers of Christ
we know no enemies and are content, should mis-
understanding- or'other circumstances place us in
positions of difficulty or danger, to rely wholly upon
Divine; protection ; and, in the case of international
disputes, to use the medium of arbitration rather
than force. We declare our uncompromising re-
sistance to the Commonwealth Defence Act in its
violation of conscience, and shall do all within our
power to secure the repeal of the compulsory
clauses ; and we would appeal to our fellow-Christians
to unite with us in combating the growth of mili-
tarism, which is essentially antagonistic to the
growth of the Kingdom of God, the foundation of
which is peace and goodwill amongst men.
In reply whereto The Bulletin takes its
friend the Quaker by the collar and pours
this into his ear :
The yarn sounds very nice and sweet and
Christian, and all that a manifesto from the Society
of Friends should be ; but it is in plain fact about the
most dangerous, blood-thirsty and peace-destroying
sort of document which could well be conceived. If
the, nations of the world were actually in a condition
of profound peace and had forgotten the art of war,
there might be something in the contention that
warlike preparation made for distrust and separa-
tion ; but, as the distrust and separation are there
already, and as all nations stand armed to the teeth,
the perfectly logical conclusion is that thorough
preparation for war is the one thing that can secure
permanent peace. Again, followers of Christ are
stated to know no enemies, and to be willing to rely
entirely on Divine protection. Do the members of
the Society of Friends leave their doors unlocked at
night and trust to Divine protection against the
Melbourne burglar? Again, the appeal to arbitration
rather than force is in every way to be desired ; but
all experience shows that only those who have a
sufficient force to give backing to their demand for
arbitration can be sure of a fair deal in any inter-
national court. Finally, the Society of Friends, in
its uncompromising resistance to the Defence Act
and its declaration that militarism is opposed to the
growth of the Kingdom of God, is lumping together
two ideas that are as opposed to one another as
light andjidarkness. The Kingdom of God is alleged
to have been steadily growing for 1900
years, yet that growth has done nothing to check
armaments, and the spirit of militarism is most
evident where there is most profession of Christian
principles. On the other hand, the Commonwealth
Defence Act is a direct blow at militarism in its
demand that each man shall be soberly trained to
defend his home. Militarism ramps and yells where
professional armies are trained to lust of conquest;
militarism declines where a citizen soldiery is
taught to regard war as a hideous evil, which only
by their efficiency they can prevent.
Plainly put, the Society of Friends desires to see
Australia left at the mercy of any foe which may
envy the Commonwealth’s possessions. The Society
wishes, by encouraging the nation to negligence of
the means of preservation, to make war of the most
dreadful kind not only possible, but certain : to ex-
pose every family in this continent to the degrada-
tion and horror which have, during the last few
weeks, been making themselves grimly felt in Eastern
Europe, and to throw the biggest temptation to
international crime in the road of those other mem-
bers of the “human brotherhood” who have their
guns ready and their marching boots laced. This
may be Christian sentiment, but it looks much more
like the spirit in which the Devil is supposed to
do business. In the New Testament there is a plain
statement, which it might do the members of the
Society of Friends good to copy out and paste in
tii6]r iia,ts • 1
Or what king, going to make war against another
king, sitteth not down first, and consulteth whether
he be able with 10,000 men to meet him that cometh
against him with 20,000.” —Luke xiv., 31.
The words are recorded as having been uttered bv
Jesus Christ, and they explain the whole basis of
the Commonwealth defence system. Our men are
being trained— or ’ rathcr > our boys are, and our men
should be—to make any possible foe sit down and
count the cost of an Australian invasion. If our
preparations are forceful enough, he will keep sit-
ting .down all the time; and if to make a foeman
sit down and not want to fight is anti-Christian in
spirit, then the Friends may have it their own way
and associate with Christian doctrine all the sloth
indolence, suffering, crimes and atrocities that their
manifesto appears to directly call for.
“Fitzjohn’s reproof to “Fitzwilliam” :
I want to chide “Fitzwilliam” (B. 14/11/’l2)
for, the brick he hurled at N. S. Wales Minister
Larmichael s remarks on the University Bill 18.
7/11/12). The Bill, coming as it does after 15
years of strenuous agitation for a more democratic
constitution, even if not perfect, is the best
thing of its kind yet attempted, and, as such, de-
serves the respect of every good Australian.
Fitzwilliam” complains that in an exposition of
the principles of his measure, Carmichael “dodged
most of the specific objections to the University
, , •., y, r J vv what are the “specific objections” other
than (1) the natural fear of the Senate to extinction
by the abolition of life tenure of their office ; (2) ditto
ditto of the University professors (five) on the exist-
ing Senate; (3) the objection of (certain) professors to
free scholarships which will stop fat fees from roll-
ing into the professorial pocket; (4) the objection
of those excellent unionists, lawyers and doctors, to
making entrance to their unions possible to the son
of Demos; (5) the. objection to linking up a con-
servative University with the democratic State
schools by means of the leaving certificate; (6) the
making of the University “a place td gather all
adult seekers after knowledge” instead of main-
taining the tradition which makes excellence an ex-
clusive quality and not a general possession. It
isn’t necessary to dodge objections like these, for
except to tradition-bound troglodytes, so to speak,
the answers shriek themselves into one sentence:
The University must be a process for carrying out
the social purpose of the adult world, and must have
the same purpose as the schools—the increase of
human wealth.
If “Fitzwilliam's” inferences and deductions are
not more reliable than his facts, he had better re-
turn to his cave. Dr. Cole, whom he quotes as a
member of the teaching staff at Harvard, has been
Vice-Principal of the Sydney Teachers’ College for
the past several years. (Anyway, it was Columbia,
and not Harvard, that he adorned.) But whv auote
two cases? Everyone admits that the Sydney Uni-
versity has turned out many brilliant scholars. The
complaint is that through want of opportunity there
are not more. Last year, with a State population
of 1,650,000, only 211 students were admitted to
matriculation at the Sydney University. Taking
the ages at which such students might be expected
to matriculate, there were 135,000 boys and girls in
the State from 17 years to 20, with a similar num-
ber between the ages of 20 and 24. Will anyone
claim for a moment that 211 is a fair proportion of
the young people of the State who are capable of
benefiting by this higher education, or who could
not subsequently find an outlet for its practical
application ?
“Fitzwilliam” derides the idea of Parliament
being represented upon the* Senate. Does he know
that of the first Senate six were selected from the
Legislative Council, including the Speaker, the
Attorney-General, the Colonial Secretary and Went-
worth, Darvall and Stilart Donaldson? Does he
forget that of the present Senate, Barton, Grif-
fith, O’Connor and Cullen, to name a few, were
members of the Legislature, while the Chancellor
himself is a member of the Legislative Council?
(It is no use “Fitzwilliam” saying that the pre-
sent is infinitely worse than previous Parliaments,
or I will quote The Bulletin's reply to the corres-
pondents who say The Bulletin is not what it used
to be.)
Then to come to his main objection, that the
remission of fees is like offering a motor-car to a
man too poor to run it. There are hundreds of
parents willing to strain a point to keep a bright
boy at school or at the University, but who cannot
possibly pay the fees. In America, it is a common
thing for undergraduates to earn enough money dur-
ing vacation to keep them during term. Wood-
chopping, waiting, boot-blacking—nothing is too
laborious or undignified, provided the end is
attained. What would be the life of a Sydney
undergraduate who was discovered by some of the
local University nuts in a similar position? Any-
how, if Carmichael gets a fair chance the proba-
bilities are that he* will ■go farther than abolishing
the fees, and so meet the objection of “Fitz-
william” that “it isn’t the cost of preparation for
a profession that hurts; it is the five or more
unproductive years that are spent in training,” etc.
“’William” is too pessimistic as to the disabilities
of the poor man’s son—too conservative in his views
of future possibilities. However, that’s a common
failing. Who could have convinced our grandfathers
in the trees that not only : would tails be abolished,
but that cocoanuts would merely be useful to the
chemist and the Aunt SalLy man?
Parson F. C. Spurr, of Melbourne, says
the prevalence of crime is due largely to the
fact that “no friendly cat purrs along the
robbers’ backs.” Trust a parson to have
faith in the power of the purrs !
S. E. Fryer, who writes the notice of
Cardinal Moran in the new volume of the
“Dictionary of National Biography,” pro-
fesses to lift the curtain and show how his
subject got his red hat. The Archbishopric
of Dublin was vacant-, and Dr. Moran was
“known to covet it.d The British Govern-
ment was anxious that he should get.,it, be-
cause he was the hephew of the previous
holder, Cardinal Cdllen, a “Castle prelate”
—in other words, a bulwark of law and
order. The Pope, wishing to cultivate
friendly relations with the British authori-
ties, decided to appoint Dr. Moran, and
summoned him from Sydney to Home for
that purpose. But while he was on the
water the Irish Nationalists of Dublin made
it clear that their candidate was Dr. Walsh.
Thus there arose “a Papal dilemma”; and
the Pope, while bowing to Dublin opinion,
made Dr. Moran a Cardinal as a solatium.
The march of the eighteen thousand: —
The cadets have come and conquered, as far as
Melbourne is concerned. No more talk of Saturday
afternoon being kept sacred to Sport! No more
protests against “teaching the young idea how to
shoot.” Eighteen thousand boys have put their best
leg foremost in an inspiring procession, and their
parents, brothers, sisters, cousins and aunts have
been stirred by the show. There wa§ a catch in the
throat all along the lines. The marching was good,
middling and very ragged in places—a satisfactory
average. The companies from St. Itilda and South
Yarra, with a stiffening of public school boys in
their ranks, were the pick of the procession, per-
haps ; but the “working-man” districts of Colling-
wood and Brunswick ran them close. They will all
improve on this first display, which was confined to
the town radius, and seemed to lack the spirit of
emulation. When the Gzar of Russia reviews an
army before breakfast, he concludes the ceremony
by shouting, “You have done very well, my chil-
dren!” And 100,000 voices reply as one, “We hope
to do better next time.”
G.-G. Denman, horSebacked in front of the steps
of Parliament House, was the watchful, silent Papa
who returned the salute of each brown officer of a
brown contingent. As Commander-in-Chief of the
Commonwealth Forces he made quite an impressive
first appearance. The job of returning all those
salutes seemed a labor of love. He was having the
time of his life.

When a lady is rescued, ainrd cheers, from
a river, and comes in as dry as Reid’s dog,
and everybody remarks that she is sopping
wet, and nobody suggests that she should get
out of her clothes into a blanket or some-
thing, and she herself refuses to move from
her chair, on the ground that she wants to
dream over her old memories, there is no
necessity for you to look up an atlas to find
out where you are. You recognise instantly
that you are on the Isle of Melo Drama, far
across the Footlight Sea. And when further
exploration reveals “the papers,” “the
cheild,” and a villain in a silk hat, you will
feel quite at-home, and not be surprised at
anything. For one thing you will notice
that the birthrate in the Isle of Mplo Drama
is always shockingly low. The heroine
always has only one child. What the pre-
sent scribe would like to see would be a de-
serted heroine with seven children, the last
two twins of three months.t The chances are
that she would be just the sort of heroine
a husband would desert. And the villain,
for his sins, should be compelled to marry
her in the first act. But none of these
things happened in “The Gambler’s Sweet-
heart,” revived at Sydney Criterion on Sat-
urday night. As the gambler', Mr. 11. R.
Roberts showed a fine actor wasted in a poor-
part. contriving to make the character
almost human. Miss Maggie Moore was
cast for a part altogether too juvenile, yet
“A WOMAN OF IMPULSE,” AT THE
SYDNEY PALACE.
The Woman of Impulse (Madge Mclntosh)
is here seen fainting. She aims for something
soƒt to break the fall. The something soƒt is
Harry Halley , her drunken and dishonest father.
by sheer art she sometimes managed to con-
vince the audience that she was 17. Good
work was done by Mr. Roland Conway as
the villain, sometimes termed a “cu-he'rr”;
by Mr. Charlie Taylor as a kindly and ulti-
mately penitent understudy in villainy; and
by Miss Edith Lewis as the dry heroine. The
thing was splendidly staged, ‘and the audi-
ence seemed to like it.
The Quaker Girl, aided by her co-religion-
ists and their Parisian friends, is still earn-
ing a good living at Her Majesty’s (Syd-
ney). When she decides to go into unre-
productive retirement, the fact will be duly
notified in these pages.
“A Woman of Impulse” arrived at the'
Palace (Sydney) last Saturday. It is the
sort of neatly-constructed play that de-
serves to be welcomed at the box-office,
both for its own sake and that of the com-
pany. The foundations of the melocomedy
are the facts of the cable column. Sir
Matthew West’s son and his expedition have
been swallowed up by Africa, while making
for a place called Malatanga. The sus-
pense is playing up with the health of the
father, who fatuously numbers amongst his
friends an engaging foreigner, one Navourac
This gent, subsequently reveals himself to
the pretty and extravagant Ladv Lang-
ford as a sort of ruthless international spv-
boss. The lady recoils on receiving this in-
formation, and asks him freezinglv what it
has to do with her. She is told that her
noble but busted pa, a trusted official of the
Foreign Office, stffie the Malatadga plans
and sold them to him (Navourac) for £5OOO
thereby enabling a foreign Power to arrange
the extermination of the expedition, includ-
ing Matthew West’s son. To save her
father, she will have to agree to spy upon
and influence her Cabinet-Minister husband
Poor, busted pa wanders in at this moment
The plans, which Navourac has produced to
convince the daughter, are lying on the
table. Pa promptly heaves them into the
Are, and the maddened Navourac tricks the
daughter into writing and signing an ac-
knowledgment of pa’s guilt by showing her
pa’s signature on a perfectly harmless let-
ter. With that document locked away in
Navourac’s safe, and a jealous husband being
prodded towards the Divorce Court by a
death-adder matron with a marriageable
daughter, the life of the Langford lady be-
comes one long brainstorm. Instead of
throwing herself on Langford’s inhospitable
chest, she knuckles under to Navourac and
holds out till the next act, when the news
of the safety of the expedition enables her
to tell the shocking truth. Her priggish
husband believes her with difficulty; pa
obligingly starts dying; and Navourac flies
with the help of Matthew West’s boot.
Madge Mclntosh, as Lady Langford, acts
with distinction and charm, and wears some
noticeable garments. George Bryant, as
Navourac, is impressive and occasionally
thrilling. Gerald Souper does sound work
as Sir Matthew West. Maggie Knight
makes Mrs. Dudley a fluffy and agreeable
drawing-room serpent. Harry Halley’s
Colonel Challace is a likeable old dipso-
maniac. John Forde gets in a few sure
touches as Brentop, of Scotland Yard. And
E. Gilburt Howard (Bertrand) is excellent
in his one scene. Indeed, looking at the
thing in a large way, Richard Bellairs (Sir
George Langford) is the only one of the
first line who fails to convince.

boy crouches before an examination paper
and puts ink on his ears, while he dismally
plumbs the awful depths of the accumulated
ignorance of 12 misspent months. “Ben
Hur,” at Sydney Royal, having been bailed
by a few reverends as a valuable and in-
structive play, and the management having
assisted with reduced matinee rates, it is

“Cripple Creek” will be dryblown off the
Adelphi premises to-morrow night, and on
Saturday, when everything has been per-
fectly dehydrated, Sydney will have a chance
to become acquainted with “What Women
Will Do for Love.” Most of it knows
already what and who they will do for
money, or a new hat; but George Marlow
promises some additional information.
Jim Jeffries couldn’t come back; Ada
Ward was doubtful; but Will Whitburn
arrived with a rush out of the shadows of
the past. Whitburn is more than a come-
dian; he is a humorist. “Everythin" he
says has a laugh in it—his railway porter
is one huge joke, and his subseonent blither
is delightful. The Rallis and Wilson Trio,
who have been braving the mosquitoes of
Rrisbane, have also come back to the Tivoli.
D’Arc’s Marionettes are in their last nights ;
but Nella Webb, Marini, Burns and Bently
and De Bierre still hold their own. Next
Saturday the Max Franklin Troupe of
gymnasts and the three musical Keltons will
begin to hold theirs.

At. the Crystal Palace John Bunyan’s
Pilgrim has been progressing over 3500 ft.
of film, to his own and the house’s satisfac-
tion ; and if Bunyan, Esq., has of late been
turning in his grave, this picture is on no
account to Marne. It carries the interest of
the old classic to the last inch. Second and
third places have been filled by “The Charge
of the Light Brigade,” a rather picturesque
cavalry film ; and that portion of “Williams’s
Weekly” which shows Col. Onslow’s tea-
party to “Liberal” ladies and their grown-
up daughters. One could almost hear the
tea bubble at the very idea of an Onslow
being ordered out of the Assembly by a
person-n-n like Willis.
He He ❖ He
“Niglitbirds,” at Her Majesty’s, loses none
of its. naughtiness as the weeks roll on.
Speaking as a mere, modest man, one is
glad to mention that it was a lady who put
so many pinches of salt on the Niglitbirds’
tale, and who endeavored to bring the strictly
respectable Countess Clicquot into line with
the other characters, as it were, by requiring
her to shed a garment early in the proceed-
ings. Had a masculine hand wrought the
libretto one might have felt expected to
cough for his sex, whereas lie can now laugh
with pure, unsullied glee at the lady-writer’s
little jokes. The ballet, with Miss Talleur
as toe-dancer, continues to give pleasure to
all, and the souls that are filled with the
melody of Strauss’s music have no ears for
the dubious moral tone of the Turkey-trot
and Bunny-hug.
** * *
Melbourne Princess’s is now the roaming
ground of “A Girl Without a Home.” None
of the other ladies, for that matter, are
troubled with household cares. The villain
has an extraordinary villa residence where
all the characters come and conspire with
him, or scorn and defy him ; and a money-
lender has an office opening into the street
which is a temporary place of concealment
for the godly and the ungodly alike; also,
there is a mysterious corner pub that serves
as a sort of rabbit warren for the full
strength of the co., though in a general way
the sons and daughters of Ishmael walk
about the streets of London and meet one
another by accident.
No air of possibility blows into this riot
of circumlocution from first to last. If any
sort of reason is offered for certain happen-
ings, the circumstances of their occurrence
are always fraught with madness. The long
arm of coincidence shoots forth mechani-
cally, biffing common-sense in the jaw every
time it looks for an explanation. Dramas
built by deplorable Mrs. Morton Powell and
her kindred balderdashers. are not, however,
intended for audiences of moderate intelli-
gence. Such entertainment is for the few
who want nothing better, and these were too
few to crowd the house even on the first
Saturday night of the present production.
The mounting and acting of George Mar-
low’s shows will attract the larger and less
unenlightened public when they are associ-
ated with better-class material, and the
world is aware of the improvement. Under
existing conditions, the co. and the scenic
artists cannot count for anything to speak
of. Hugh Buckler, coming in fancy convict-
costume to a police station, where he is tried
for wilful murder and sentenced to penal
servitude for manslaughter, is the limit of
absurdity. A street fight between Miss Har-
vey and Miss Mortyne (the. latter of whom
spends most of her stage time in drunken
assault and battery) is a revolting farce.
Fortunately, all the dramas on the Marlow
list are not like this. “The. King’s
Romance,” now in rehearsal, is said to be
one of the best.

The gentle boobs of Battlesburg continue
to be taken down by design and uplifted by
accident. They certainly don’t deserve their
uplifting. The community, ranging from
Ediie Lamb to Judge Lampton, that could
shut its eyes to the transparent wiles of
Wallingford and gather confidence from the
comic-relief antics of his pal Blackie Daw,
should have been fleeced, skinned and quar-
tered without the option of a fluke. In the
face of this criminal libel on Yankee uncute-
ness,' nobody should complain that the ex-
portation of “On Our Selection” will be a
bad advertisement for Australia. Dave and
Dad and Sarah strike one as being intellec-
tual giants compared with Eddie Lamb and
the older members of the Universal Covered
•Carpet Tack Co. There have been some
-changes in the boobocracy of Battlesburg
since “Wallingford” came to Melbourne
Royal. Beatrice Holloway’s promotion to
•the part of type-writing girl, in place of the
invalided Miss Cohan, has brought Enid
Bennett into the shoes of the verdant young
creature who plights her troth to Blackie.
Also, a new boob has taken over the ’bus-
driver’s job from Fred Cambourne.
This is the third or fourth week of The
Span of Life,” at the King’s, and still that
hardy melodrama is waiting for its notice to
quit. The camel of the desert, and the
Arabs who yell at the bidding of Dunstan
the Deceiver get through their nightly duties
in the smoothest way possible; but the
human bridge has episodes of unwonted lum-
piness. Then the lady who isn’t. Miss
Duggan finds it hard to keep her footing on
the curved spine of the middleman. One
night last week she seemed in a fair way to
wobble off into the chasin; so the curtain
came down suddenly and hid her agitation.
*** * ?
Gregan McMahon is staging Shaw’s “Man
and Superman,” at his next Repertory
Theatre innings in Melbourne. The mysti-
cal third act doesn’t matter, for it is never
played in either professional or amateur
productions. The first, second and fourth
acts give the whole story consecutively, and
the introduction of the essential motor-car
is the only serious difficulty the manager has
to deal with. “Man and Superman” will be
played on the 6th and 7th December, at the
Athenaeum Hall, and a revival of “The
Pigeon” is to happen on the 7th, with Shaw’s
“Dark Lady of the Sonnets” as curtain-
raiser or afterpiece. At Christmas time the
Repertory Co. will get a holiday trip to
Tasmania, thanks to Edward Branscombe.
Branscombe, who has the theatres, and is
taking all the fisk, has engaged McMahon
and the whole co. to show' for three weeks
in Hobart, and one week in Launceston.
They take six plays, “Chains.” “The
Pigeon,” “Man and Superman,” “The Prodi-
gal’s Return,” “Two Mr. Wetherbys,” and
“John Gabriel Borkman.” When they are
through with that lot, Tassy will reckon
itself the headquarters of intellectual drama.
** * *
The Chapman-Alexander mission company
left Melbourne for W.A. last week, amidst
the muffled groans of the guarantors. The
Australian tour has resulted in a loss of
£5OO thus far, and unless W.A. makes up
the deficit, these entrepreneurs will trouble
no more about this country’s immortal soul.
One of them told his audience at Ballarat
not .to expect smother visit from him. He
has no intention of running the co. to poor
business on his own account; and it is cer-
tain that the guarantors will not re-engage
him at enormous expense.

Hugh Mcßickards is making a praise-
worthy effort to popularise the one-act play.
There is no reason why the manufacture of
potted dramas should not in time become a
flourishing Australian industry. Meanwhile,
the imported sample, “A Curate’s Misadven-
ture,” put on at the Opera House on Satur-
day by the Page Comedy Co., is not a bad
thing of its class. A lady who races as
“Mr. Id.” advertises for a trainer, while
about the same time Mrs. H. next door is
pining for a tutor for the young Aitches.
Of course the reverend tutor happens upon
the racing Aspirate, and is amazed at the
announcement that she has 12 she wants
trained, and is expecting three or
four more next week. If “Mr. H.”
has not yet fixed upon her racing
colors, • this journal v would suggest blue,
with purple spots. Fanny Powers,
who is soon to exchange the glare of the
footlights for the- glow of the domestic
hearth, has returned to give the boys a final
treat, and on Saturday they signified their
gratitude in the usual way. Florence
Baines. Taylor and Arnold, and the Max
Frqnklin Troupe, are among others who are
keeping the- wolf from the Mclnsplosh door-
mat.
Last Saturday, West, of Melbourne, shoved
into notice-a three-act drama called “A Cruel
Fate,” which contained a lady who looked
like an ddition-de-luxe of Monna Lisa, and
fooled with bloques when the lights were
low. In accordance with the rules of the
dramatic game, her promiscuous giddiness
led to trouble; and towards the finale she
dropped opt of the picture with an ugly-
looking carver sticking in her bust. There
was an uhpleasant mediaeval aspect about
the conclusion of the lady; still she deserved
something for making flirtation a business
and “sweating” herself at the occupation.
There is humor in “The Beauty Parlor of
Stone Gulch”; and “The Convalescent” is
a bright narrative of a man whose bump of
magnanimity develops from the size of a
pimple to the significance of a peanut.
** * *
The talk of the congregation at the P’ran
Lyric (Vic.) last week was mostly about
“Saved,” the tale of a lady who is prevented
from becoming the victim of an unseemly
passion by the masterly cleverness of her
darling che-ild. “Only an Outcast,” which
also ran, was a yarn of burglary and heroic
self-sacrifice, with a cast including a squire,
a butler, crusted old port, and all the appur-
tenances necessary to a picture of English
country life. “A War-Time Romance” and
“A Pueblo Legend” were Amurlcan films of
sentiment and excitement; while the best
comedy on the bill was “Mr. Grouch at the
Seaside,” displaying sidelights on the life
of a jealous man.
* * *
At the King’s, in Adelaide, Paul
Pedrini, making his first appearance
in Australia, puts up a clever “Ris-
ley” show. All liis work is done
with the feet; as he lies on his back, he
juggles with just about anything, from a
postage-stamp to a cannon. The Obracs
give another satisfying turn, mixing fun
and agility. At the Tivoli, Claire Romaine,
the cheeky boy-impersonator, is immensely
popular, even though she dragoons audiences,
after the style of a Yankee evangelist, into
chorus-singing. “All married ladies who
love their husbands sing this time. Come on
all the three of you!” Cliinko is a mar-
vellous young juggler; in some of his quick
and agile work he really recalls the great
man on whom his stage-name seems to be
unwisely modelled. La Freya has just
started some beautiful artistic poses. Allen
Doone is still going strong at the Royal.
In “Molly Bawn” he introduces his Robert
Emmett oration. And his days he spends in
swift motor-car flights over Adelaide’s
plains and hills. West’s pictures, Wonder-
graph, Empire, Pav. and Central are nearly
all offering stronv drama —generally well
acted by named artists, French or Danish,
or American, but never Australian or Eng-
lish. These pictures they mix with the open-
ing of the local bowling club, etc. Tugs-of-
war for a Mount Lyell fund have been filling
the Exhibition, and the Dandies, clean and
clever, have reached the dignity of being
fined for overcrowding.
AT POVERTY POINT.
In the half a dozen pages given to Henry Irving
in the new vol. of the “Dictionary of National Bio-
graphy,” credit is duly given to that old-tirpe favor-
ite Australian actor, William Hoskins for
his early recognition of young Irving’s
latent talent and for securing him his first
engagement at the Lyceum. Sunderland.
He received no salary at first, but got
255. a week later on. Nothing, however,
of his later career is told that wasn’t
well known already.

“Hermes”: The new regulations governing the ex-
hibition of moving pictures in N.S.W. prohibit the
showing of films representing scenes suggestive of
“immorality or indecency, executions, murders, or
other revolting scenes... .bushranging, robberies, or
other acts of lawlessness.” It is almost impossible to
give a show which will not bump this regulation
somewhere. If it were applied to acted plays, there
would not be one theatre left in Sydney, and every
grand opera would be prohibited work. . Consider
Samson and Delilah, for example. The finest film
ever exhibited in Australia was “La Tosca,” which
contains a suggestion of immorality. In fact,
Scarpia is immorality incarnate; also, there is a
murder, when Scarpia meets his just reward; and
an execution, when the hero is shot on the ramparts ;
and a suicide, when the heroine hurls herself over
the battlement. One of the cleverest of films was that
showing the battle between the trypansomes and the
white corpuscles in the blood of a rat infected with
sleeping sickness ; but it was as revolting as it was
educative and instructive. People who go to see
picture-shows want Romance, and Romance is largely
Attempted Robbery of the Other Fellow’s .Girl, which
is suggestive of lawlessness and dishonorable conduct.
Practically all historical drama is shut out by the
new regulations, and the picture business limited to
industrial scenes, landscape and sporting scenes. It
is too sweeping altogether.
“M.M.”: The new censorship of moving picture
films in N. S. Wales appears as if it might justify
all the protest that is being made about it by
those who run the shows. The head and front of its
offending is that it places in the hands of the
policeman the power to inflict a great deal of
annoyance on the proprietors oh picture theatres.
No one who knows anything of tne police will deny
that they possess many virtues, such as patience,
well-developed feet and superb dignity, but no one
would ever accuse them as a body of possessing
either the requisite knowledge or taste to decide on
the fitness or otherwise of a picture film for public
screening. The screw and prospects of promotion of
a constable do not attract men with more than the
most rudimentary education, and the training
given inside the force is all against such a know-
ledge as is required for film censorship. Take it for
all in all, censorship of sermons by the employees
of the municipal council, or of leading articles by
boxing referees, would be just about as satisfactory.
The policeman isn’t the person for the job, censor-
ship isn’t urgently required, the public is the best
judge of its own entertainment, and the whole
business is a piece of cringing to the hysteria of a
few disgruntled faddists with the Wowser taint in
their whiskers.
“0.K.”: Melbourne Gaiety, which formerly started
its scale of prices at a modest “thrum,” has doubled
the tariff on the strength of a new and tasteful out-
fit. The new Gaiety is a bright and clean little hall
with a vastly improved vaudeville show. John
Fuller. father of proprietor Ben Fuller, was
a feature of the re-opening programme a
few weeks back. Age cannot wither his sweet
tenor warble. Mellifluous tenors have come
and gone since Fuller came into the business, but he
Sallies in our Alley just as he did many years ago.
The fact is, he’s an artist of the school that old Sims
Reeves founded ; and Art is long.
“Haresfoot”: Apropos the expected explosion of
Irish wrath over “The Playboy of the West.” The
thin-skin is evinced in a resolution passed by the
Melbourne Gaelic Society, to the effect that some
characters in Allen Doone’s plays were objectionable,
notwithstanding that Doone is Irish through and
through, and that Redmond and Donovan, the Irish
envoys, sucked in his pieces with great delight. 1
have a sneaking idea that “The Playboy of the
West” is a rattling good drama, only objected to bv
such purists as the Melb. Gaelic Society. They ought
to learn from the good humor of the Jews over
Dicey Morris, Melter Moss and so forth. Indeed,
when the first dramatic performance was given in
Jerusalem a few months ago, the Hebrew audience
enjoyed even “The Merchant of Venice.”
R., E. O’Connor, who died this week, was
one <sf the three draftsmen of the Australian
Constitution. When the Convention met in
Adelaide, it promptly showed its distrust of
Reid, not merely by choosing Barton as its
“NIGHT-BIRDS," AT HER
MAJESTY'S ,
MELBOURNE.
Edmund Sherras as the tired Prince.
leader, but by keeping him off even the'
drafting committee. For that important;
job Barton chose his colleagues—-John
Downer, K.C., of Adelaide, and O’Connor —
and the Convention accepted the nomina-
tions with scarcely a question. O’Connor,
the youngest of the three —he was two years
Barton’s junior—is the first to go.
The late Justice R. E. O’Connor had a
partly-French origin. His grandfather was
General O’Connor, of, the Grand Army of
France, a Franco-Irishman of the MacMahon
type. He went with Idoche’s expedition to
Ireland and stayed there. O’Connor’s charm
of manner was extraordinary, and it stood
him in good stead when he became leader of
the Barton Government in the Senate. As
Sydney Telegraph put it: —
Roughly, the constitution of the Rouse presented
an almost equal balance of Freetraders and Protec-
tionists, and there was a definite majority against
him on many important items of Government policy.
Yet he never suffered any sejrious defeats, and in con-
nection with the first Customs tariff he had extra-
ordinary and quite unexpected success.
Certainly, he was a born Parliamentarian,
and had a useful stock of wisdom and com-
mon sense; but these qualities would have
availed him little had he lacked his excep-
tionally attractive personality. It used to
be said that members voted for measures not
half so much because they approved of them
as because they hated to displease O’Connor.
It is a quaint commentary on our system of
party Government that though, in the words,
of a paper which had little use for his politi-
cal views, his speeches were “loaded with
wisdom of a very practical and convincing
kind,” and though “he might have been wel-
comed as Prime Minister of the Common-
wealth,” he could never get elected for a
N. S. Wales seat. He had a poor platform
manner—having nothing of the mountebank
or mummer in his composition—and he would
not play the game in the way the Party
bosses demanded.
THE GENTEEL SUPE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wanted, 80 men of gentlemanly appearance, must
be tall, as supernumeraries. Apply, Blank Theatre,
10 o’clock to-day.— Advt.
For gentlemen the adverts, call
To act upon the stage as supers,
They must be elegant and tall
To fill the eye as British troopers,
Or pose wjth proper social touches
When swanking gaily with the Duchess.
Ths management has urgent need
Of eighty such at highest wages,
That’s how the little adverts, read.
But what of blokes the boss engages?
I sei'k through all the leading houses
Whereat the super gay carouses,
And gentlemanly’s not the fit
And proper word in this connection.
He is not tall, he isn’t “It,”
Consorting with a “class” collection.
In truth, the tall and slender eighty
Are much too lean when not too weighty.
They stand against the scenic walls,
Bow-legged, in-chested, shambling dummies;
Their weird vacuity appals;
They look like resurrected mummies
Parading for some trifling payment
In stale, misfitting, modern raiment.
I have a strong belief, in fact,
That genteel pros., supremely varnished,
Who heroes and fine villains act,
—lnsist on supers wry and tarnished,
That contrast with the wilted section
May set off their divine perfection.
VICTORIA.

SOCIETY
This paper has always had a fancy for a
church on wheels, wherefore it rejoices to
know that the Rev. G. J. Mackay has just
presented the Victorian Baptist Union with
a scheme for a movable tabernacle of ortho-
dox architecture, to be drawn about by
horses for the furtherance of bush-mission
work. Our preference, we admit, is for a
motor chapel on pneumatic tyres. No doubt
that will come in time, and we shall see a
thoroughly up-to-date automobile mission-
house going licketty-split across this young
•country, hot on the heels of the Devil.
Meanwhile we confess that we are just a
little hurt that our valuable pioneering
■efforts in the cause of chapels on castors
were not recognised by the Baptist Union.
It was the least the pirate might have done.

“San Dido”: Strange people are made
..Taypees in Westralia. Went up country to
:a farming centre a while ago to defend in a
.sly-grog case. Two J’s.lh arrived on time,
and the court waited for the third to drive
in. Wanting to return by the midday train,
I asked the officer-in-charge if lie would
object to the two hearing the case. He
looked sad and said: “We’re waiting for
the penman. Neither of these two can
write.”
From an Adelaide critic: —
S.A. Society of Arts has for years been getting
together an inter-State show of pictures annually, to
give the Adelaide Art Gallery a chance of spending
a few hundreds in purchases—which it generally
does, a strong Australian room in the Gallery being
the result. The 15th “Federal” exhibition keeps up
the average, without showing any special distinction.
It is full of good landscapes, and figures not nearly
so good. Of anything bold, of compositions histori-
cal, or social, or legendary, there is hardly a sign.
Taking what there is, without mourning over what
there isn’t, one finds the late Ford Paterson with a
large Gippsland scene, faint and wan in the mist.
F. McCubbin goes to the Bush, too, for an evening
scene of rich subdued color; also he has a really
brilliant portrait of himself. Louis McCubbin' make's
a first appearance here, palpably under the influence
of Turner, with his reds and yellows and explosive
cotton-wool clouds. Not comfortable pictures to
“live with,” but welcome as showing a bolt out of
the beaten track. Delafeld Cook is another Vic-
torian of the faint-and-wan order; but Shearsby
glitters, with a striking land-and-blue-sea-piece from
Sandringham. Then Ambrose Patterson (whom
Melba claims as her discovery) hits one hard with a
Brussels boulevard under snow. It is a little in Will
Ashton’s style—or one of his styles—but less careful.
A kind of glorified scene-painting, if you like, but
effective. Sydney men are less frequent. Lister
Lister is sound with a river and a coast, and Wolinski
has a sordid, but truthful, coffee-shOp scene—one of
the rare instances of figure-composition in the show.
The Adelaide men are not obtrusive. Power is away,
painting the dogs and horses of wealthy Englishmen ;
If ay ley Lever sends out a few trifles from Cornwall;
and Hans Heysen, though on the spot, is busy with
commissions. Already he has founded a “school,”
and gum-trees, more or less like his, are seen on all
sides. Also, there is an Ashton school of seascapes,
James the father and William the son leading it
with their own works, allied, yet distinct. James
Scott, once of Maoriland and Sydney, and now of
Adelaide, has some single figures, more ambitious
than successful. Australia’s great need, to judge
from all that Adelaide ever sees, is more life-school
practice. Of the sound and enjoyable there are
many: John White, Edward Davies, Collins, Barnes
(the coming man), and Rose Lowcay. But,
strangely, the clever Hambidge sisters are little
represented.
The S.A. Gallery has bought McCubbin’s self-
portrait and Ambrose Patterson’s water-scene in Brus-
sels ; and, from Adelaide artists, a full-length por-
trait by May Grigg, and a misty English field by
Rose Lowcay. It also wants a James Ashton ocean
picture, but has run up against its own rule that no
artist shall have more than three paintings in the
Gallery. Probably an earlier work will make wav
for this one.
Another ease where silence would have
been "diamonds. A resident of an up-coun-
try Victorian town complained to the local
hospital committee that his father-in-law’s
death had not been promptly reported to the
family, and that the deceased would have
been “buried in a pauper’s grave” if his re-
latives had not accidentally learned of their
dear kinsman’s departure. The burial of
father-in-law in a pauper’s grave ‘"would
have greatly upset” them all, and the
thought of the blow that never befell them
was very hard to bear. The family tears
were still sizzling when the callous hospital
people dropped in an iceberg. They hadn’t
reported their • patient’s decease to his rela-
tives, they said, because they didn’t know
he . had any* none having been known to
visit him or contribute anything to his sup-
port during the 18 months of his illness !

Gaud Mayor Glarke, the Sydney street-
cleaning enthusiast, and Railway Commis-
sioner Johnson should get together and de-
vise some legend for the littersome tram
ticket other than the present
“Please destroy on Alight-
ing.” The careful visitor,
being unable to . either sink
or burn the document, trans-
lates the notice into “Tear
into small pieces and scatter
to the four winds.” So he
innocently strews trouble
round, and, if he is unlucky,
does it in front of one of the
Gaud Mayor’s six patrolmen
costs him a morn-
ing, some unpleasant public-
ity, and half a crown or
more. In the present state
of the city by-laws, the tram-
tickets are almost as provo-
cative as if they bore the re-
quest, “On alighting, please
spit on the footpath and kick
a policeman.” What ousrht
to replace it The Bulletin
confesses it doesn’t know —
unless it be “Please swallow
the darned thing.” The
only danger is that that might mean litter-
ing the town with corpses, and some corpses
are more objectionable than used tram
tickets.

“Sapphire”: In Constantinople stands
the famous church of S. Sophia. When the
Turks took the city in 1453 the mosaics and
pictures which adorned the building were
not destroyed, but covered up, and they
may still gladden the hearts of the devout.
In the church were 101 doors, one of which
led to a secret room. The priests entered
this _ chamber when the city fell, and, ac-
cording to Greek tradition, are still waiting
there alive, but doubtless somewhat fati-
gued. One hundred doors have been found,
but the hundred-and-first is still in safe
hiding somewhere, waiting patiently for the
restoration of the city to more-or-less Chris-
tian rule.

“Skirlaugh”: The case (B. 7/11/T2) of
the Lismore Hindoos who cremated a body
there recently is merely the exception which
proves the rule. I have witnessed several
coolie burials in Australia, and the proceed-
ings have been very simple in each case. A
shallow hole about 3ft. x 4ft. is dug, and
into this the body, after being divested of
all jewellery, is thrust. The authorities
now insist on lime being placed in the grave ;
but most of it is purloined-by the mourners
to mix with their chewing tobacco. The
idea that the coolie is a tidy person with
his dead won’t stand looking at without a
handkerchief to the nose.
THE GENTLE INQUIRY.
“Ah! Portrait of my best patron."
"Still a patron?”
Melbourne Fever Hospital is a storm-
centre owing to an allegation that some of
the doctors who have the run of the pl6.ce
have been taking liberties with the bodies
of patients who have died there. It .is for-
bidden for doctors to make a post-mortem
examination of any corpse without the chn-
sent of the friends ; but somebody suddenly
heaved into circulation a general statement
that unsanctioned. post-mortems were the
rule. An inquiry of course followed, and
some startling statements are being made.
Whatever the final result may be, one thing
is. already established, and that is that the
management of hospitals by honorary com-
mittees is a wretchedly inefficient system.
The committee of this institution, which is
more or less on its trial, could not find time
to attend the inquiry. As one of the mem-
bers explained, they had their business to
attend to. Which hit the nail right oh the
head. First, private business; after that,
public business, the latter (time permitting)
being given the fag-end of a number of more
or less mediocre intellects.
“Hardaport”: The time is mellow for a
N. S. Wales Government to compel the
crowds of tugs that swarm in Port Jackson
to line up for inspection and license. A
few days ago two of them collided, fortu-
nately without serious consequence. One of
them was filled with workmen, and if the
planking had been stove in a bit more, any
of them could have had my share of the
proceedings. The ordinary ferry has to
obtain and hold a certificate of seaworthi-
ness ; but by a loophole or chasm in the
law, the workmen who are conveyed to and
fro by an employer in his own tug, tub,
tank or colander have no protection that
way. The carrying capacity is limited only
by the number of men to be transported,
and as far as the law is concerned not even
the postal address of a lifebuoy need be
put aboard. The tug can sink, blow-up, or
burst with impunity, and nobody cares a
cuss except the woman and the kids wait-
ing for father to come home. _ I hate to men-
tion a little thing like this, particularly
when employers have more important mat-
ters to think of; but somebody has to do it,
and I’m anxious to get it done while I’ve
time. I may be drowned on the way to the
docks to-morrow.
THE ACCURSED MEAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was being recognised more and more that flesh
food was more impure than vegetarian food. The
person who never ate meat had never been known to
be a drunkard!, and a drunkard had never been known
to be a vegetarian. —Report of W.C.T.U. Convention
(Melbourne).
For years and years the frenzied soul
Has raised himself upon a cask
To give the Demon Drink his dole
Of frantic stoush ; but now we ask
That he will turn his righteous heat
Upon the wicked things we eat,
And take a turn at outing meat.
Poor Mr. Bung has had enough.
From here to elsewhere he’s been cursed ;
And all the while it’s not his stuff
That’s driving man from bad to worst.
The steak it is that blights, not swill;
Not beer, but beef, has ruined Bill.
In fact, ’tis sausages that kill!
Cold tongue, and fish, and pickled feet,
These gnaw the morals out of us.
’Tis tripe and such infernal meat
Should raise the Rechabitish cuss.
Were there no pork there’d be no port;
No chicken, no champagne. In short,
The Butcher Fiend is grim and swart!
Pigshead, not hogshead, spells despair ;
The foe is cutlets, never hops.
Who takes the pledge henceforth will swear
He’ll not taste, touch, or handle chops.
And good teetotal people must
Combine with fevered zeal to bust
That dreadful Beef and Mutton Trust.

“C.E.”: The National Parliament voted
£BO,OOO for the preparation and equipment
of Western Port (Vic.), and the money is
being industriously spent. The Institute of
Civil Engineers had a look the other week,
shoved round by Superintendent Fanstone.
Admiral Henderson’s recommendation has
been adopted, and a great scheme moves
forward. As sketched, there is to be pro-
vision for training, officering and housing
2000 naval recruits. This will be the Aus-
tralian Training Station; Sydney is ear-
marked as the chief repairing depot, and
Cockburn Sound (Westralia) for building
battleships. (Did you speak?) “Eventu-
ally” (good word, that!) Western Port is
to be a complete naval dockyard, with par-
ade grounds, basins, berthage, victualling
departments and storeyards, ammunition
stores, locating dock, and schools for all re-
quirements. There is railway communica-
tion with Melbourne (48 miles), and a deep
water channel for the largest warships
right up to the unruffled Hanns Inlet,
where the dockyard will be.
The track of the backblocks teacher has
always been fairly spiky; but if Fitzpatrick,
M.L.A., is right, the crown of prickly pear
must be awarded to one who was recently
ordered to leave Crookwell (N.S.W.), and
take charge of the school at Coffee Hill.
When he arrived at the Hill, he looked round
for the usual building with an Empire Day
bragstaff in front. He couldn’t see it. So
he asked the Oldest Inhabitant, and stood by
respectfully while the Oldest took down the
shutters of his memory. At last a gleam
of recollection shone in the old man’s eye.
Oh, yes, he remembered the school quite well.
It had been sold to a man called Brown or
White, or maybe it was Green. This Green
(or it might have been Black, now he came
to think of it)had pulled it down and carted
the timber to Cave Creek, 20 miles away.
Carmichael, Minister for Public Instruction,
at first denied the humorous story; but on
Fitzpatrick returning to the charge, he col-
lapsed into his official shell, and is now
strongly suspected of making further in-
quiries. Meantime, this much seems cer-
tain : the teacher who had been sent to Coffee
Hill has been moved on to some place where
he can find a school.

The Oldest Inhabitant sticks a pin into a
bladder: —
Apropos the sin of turning’ Svdney Government
House to any other use on account of the sentiment
attaching to it. There is far more of the vague
substance hanging to the old house at Parramatta.
The building there was one of those designed by
Lieutenant Watts, and was completed about 1818-19.
Macquarie was the first to occupy
it. Brisbane, Darling, Bourke,
Gipps, Fitzroy an4 Denison all
blade a home of it. It is a fine
sample of the better class of dwell
ihgs in Australia 90 years ago.
The walls are 3ft. thick, the rooms
lofty arid tlie ventilation perfect.
Underneath there are most capaci-
ous cellars. Bins for wines are
built in many of the excavations;
in some of them may still be
seen the huge “horses,”' made of
logs, to support the barrels. The
courtyard is paved with five layers
of brick. The dining-room is still
—or lately was—very much as in
Macquarie’s time. The ballroom
looks, small nowadays. At the
rear were guard-room and offices,
and some distance further up the
hill was the bathroom. There was
a pump at the foot of the hill,
and water was forced up from the
river into a tank on top of the
bathhouse, whence pipes were laid
on to supply the house. Close to
the bath were the stables. Such,
anyhow, was the condition of the
historic house when, a little while
ago, the King’s School authorities
got it and converted it into a
junior school and dormitory. At
that house Governor Brisbane
erected his observatory, and,
Within a few yards stood the Wool-
pack Inn, built in Macquarie’s
time—the house where the his-
toric Emancipists’ banquet was
given to Governor Brisbane on the
eve of his departure from the
colony, in December, 1825. In
this house the wife of Governor
Bourke died, and in view of its
windows the wife of Governor
Fitzroy was killed. If you want “sentiment” you’ll get
it at this old Government House, yet the “loyalists” of
the McMillan type evidently didn’t think there was
any “outrage” in letting the place for years as a
hash-house, and novy as a school dormitory. You
see, there was nothing to be gained politically by
raising the “outrage” cry before. Now any stick
will do to beat the Labor dog with.

“Kidette”: Is Defence Minister Pearce
doing the square thing with his kid army?
Each cadet has to put in for the year four
whole-day, 12 half-day and 24 night drills.
The whole-day drills fall on a public holi-
day generally, and as the kid doesn’t want
to miss his holiday, he dodges drill for that
day. He can make good by doing some half-
day or night drills instead. The rifle ranges
are in most cases some miles from town, so
the musketry practice is held on the whole-
days. As a consequence, many go through
the year with no shooting at all. This may
result in the infants feeling a bayonet some
day if they can’t make their bullets find
the enemy first—which is why I ask whether
Pearce is doing the square thing? Inci-
dentally, the Turks have decided in future
to encourage rifle-sliooting. I’m afraid it’s
a bit late —in their case.
Justus Schreiber, of Bendigo, is a much
astonished man these days. In May last he
got a divorce from his wife on the grounds
of desertion ; but before the decree was made
absolute, the lady, who had gone to Wes-
tralia, came back, interviewed somebody,
and upset everything by bringing about the
Attorney-General’s intervention. It seems
that after proceedings had been started
husband and wife met, stayed at the same
hotel, and, according to the case for the
Attorney-General, came to an honorable un-
derstanding. Justus was to give his ex-
beloved £llO, and she was to go to Wes-*
tralia and not oppose the petition. She
went to Westralia; but, through some over-

“Lantana”: Victoria’s old Government
House —Balmerino House it was called —
was auctioned at Melbourne a few days ago
for a mere £42. And at the very hour this
thing was being done hundreds of scalding
“loyalists” were seething fiery denunciations
against a Labor Government which had the
beastly audacity to ask for rent for the
building occupied by Gov.-General Denman
wheu in Sydney. A whole Government
House with its sentiment, its memories of
loyalty and champagne! and Gorsave and
hiccups sold in the market for 40 common
quid ! _ Incidentally, the place was built in
1854, its first tenant being Governor Hoth-
am. I don’t know what the purchaser pro-
poses to do with his purchase, but, so that
the old place may feel at home, I hope he
intends to keep fowls in it. For there is
not a great deal of difference between Gov-
ernors and fowls. Feathers and chortling
are characteristics of both.
The increase of house rents is having a
remarkable effect on matrimonial matters.
In a recent breach of promise case the aunt
of the girl said the arrangements for the
marriage had advanced even to the point of
“finding the boarders.” It was explained
that as the hanpy pair must live in a house
several sizes too large for their income, the
boarders to occupy the unnecessary rooms
were of as much importance as the ring or
the wedding garment. And much more
likely, by the way, to provide work for the
divorce court presently.
“Kodak”: The Universal Ad-
jective so beloved of battle-his-
torians is a fine, full-bodied
word which lends flavor to the
baldest sentence; but some
people overwork it terribly.
Feemer is one of those who
should be prosecuted for sweat-
ing it. He contrives to get
along without it when at home,
for he has a great regard for
his quiet, religious wife. I
know he has a great regard for
her, because, the other evening,
I overheard him talking to a
close friend in the homing,tram.
This was how Feemer put it:
“It’s not a fair thing to leave a
(universal adjective) woman
alone all the (adjective) night.
I used to stay at home when
the kids were young just to
keep her company. But they
don’t go to bed early now, and
she doesn’t care a (adjective)
bit if I stay out all the (col-
ored) night. , Last night I
wanted to go to the (as Usual)
lodge ; but the kids were going
up to the < same old word) pic-
ture show, so I offered to stay
at home. But, Lord bless yer,
she wouldn’t have it! ‘No.
Jack,’ she says, ‘you go, and
don’t worry about me. I won’t
be nervous, because I’ve got
some (customary adjective )
sewing to do.’ ” Right "there I
decided that Feemer was over-
doing it.
“George Street”: Sydney
tramway system is such a won-
derful affair that it seems
almost superfluous to throw an-
other brick at it, but the absur-
dity of the plan by which trams
from the populous suburbs about
Darlinghurst are run to a ter-
minus at Erskine-street calls
for the hurling of sorhething
injurious. From William-street to Pitt-
street the cars are packed to their last
limit; from George-street to Erskiiie-street
they travel almost empty. Perhaps that
doesn’t affect the tramway revenue; never-
theless, it seems a plain fact that the run-
ning of empty cars in a crowded city is a
proof of managerial incapacity. If four-
fifths of the cars turned the corner and con-
cluded their journey at Circular Quay, they
would run full to the last jolt.

THE REFORMER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One probable reason for the increase of thefts is
Che fact that crime is so leniently treated, both by
the public and the Judges. The friendly “cat” no
longer purrs along the backs of robbers.
—Parson Spurr ( Melb .).
On Sunday morn and Sunday night
I preach the gospel of the Meek
Who taught us when the wicked smite
To smile and turn the other cheek ;
On week days earnestly I toil
To finish my invention cute:
With dynamo and thrilling coil
To fashion for the sinner’s back
A rake, a sword, a fire, a rack —
My patent castigating brute.
“Forgive! They know not what they do,”
My Master said, and that is well
To warm a stale address or two ;
But I prefer the brimstone smell.
For putting down the sins of men
I’ve little faith in text or creed.
I’d tie the devils up, and then
With fiery tooth and raking claw
I’d purr their bodies rough and raw,
And make their purple shoulders bleed.
If leathern cats with tails to match,
Which shred the wretches’ backs, are good,
Much better is a beast to scratch
As my electric whipper would.
It’s armed with cunning flails of steel,
With tiny teeth it stabs and hacks,
On many a little whirling wheel,
With keen barbs set and cutters neat;
No cat —a tiger mad for meat,
With tiny teeth it stabs and hacks.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

“Silas Snell” : He was down for the Cup.
He came from Boowee, was 25 years of age, and this
was his first trip to the city. In fact, he had not
previously visited centres of commerce more popu-
lous than Boowee and Birdinalp. He was surprised
at Melbourne in a slow sort of way, and spent most
of his spare time leaning on the Town Hall, watch-
ing men and events. His engaging candor amused
the people at the hotel where he was staying, but
his habit of getting up when others were just going
to bed occasioned some inconvenience. Said he:
“This Melbin’s a orl-ri-i-ight sort o’ place, but,
cri-i-ipes, wot I can’t make out is what everybody is
doin’. When a bloke gets up in the mor-r-rnin’ the
people are in the streets; they’re in the streets just
the same when a bloke goes to b-e-e-ed; and all
da-ay long they’re in the streets. Where are they
goin’ all the ti-i-me? That’s all very well, yeh
kno-o-w, mooehin’ round the town ; but who does the
milkin’ ?”
“Eddyson”: The man in the two-horse
waggon was nodding on his seat as the neddics jogged
lazily in a cloud of their own making along the
dusty Little River road. At a spot about two miles
from the pile bridge, Shannon was diverted from his
day-dreams by echoes of an intemperate human argu-
ment rising above the jangle of the waggon. Pull-
ing up, he investigated, and discovered something
like a fight going on across a grass paddock in the
direction of a staring, white-washed farm-house.
Shannon dismounted, and strolled across the pad-
dock with that air of drifting concern which a
total stranger assumes in approaching the other fel-
low’s fight. Coming closer, the traveller observed
that the contestants were a man and a woman.
Nearer still, Shannon discovered that the woman was
a large, tough type, badly tangled in a barbed wire
fence. Most of her outer garments were ensnared be-
yond saving, and the prisoner was utterly helpless
under the castigation that a small, leathery man
with a face like a parched pippin was administering
with a waist belt. It was the vehement protests of
the tethered dame that had awakened Shannon. She
was apparently under the stress of a profound emo-
tion, and was telling the small brown man those
things about himself which a husband and a father
does not care to hear ventilated before strangers.
The small man was sitting on a stone. He was com-
paratively self-contained. When the woman’s yells
flagged, he heartened her on by slapping her bare
calves and arms with his belt. All he said before
administering the reproof was: “Well, Milly, what’s
it goin’ t’ be?” Milly retorted tnat he was a hog
and a dog, with lingual gargoyles and frills of im-
polite verbiage, and threatened torture and lingering
death to the stunted male when conditions were
altered, and she could resume her customary sway
in the old home. “Very well, Milly,” said the casti-
gator, and he belted her again. Pausing to fill his
pipe, he observed Shannon, and nodded calmly.
When he had cut his tobacco, and Milly’s eloquence
had ceased blowing a gale, he introduced us: “Mrs.
Wagstaff, Mister.” When he had filled his pipe he
added: “I’m Wagstaff.” He lit his pipe, and shook
his belt again. “Well, Milly,” he said, “what’s it
t’ be?” Milly’s retort was scandalous. Wagstaff
flapped her with the belt. Pausing again to expec-
torate, he said to Shannon, nodding sideways towards
his victim: “See, the Missus was chasin’ me with a
hoe, when she got herself hooked on the wires, and
I’m wantin’ t’ persuade her to agree t’ be a more
reasonable kind iv partner fer the future.” “I’ll
tear the pig’s ears off him when I get outer this!”
said Mrs. Wagstaff. “No, Milly, don’t say that,”
said Wagstaff gently, and he used his belt with in-
creased force. Then he smoked for two minutes
while his wife raved to Heaven. “Well, Milly, old
girl, what’s it t’ be?” said Wagstaff. Shannon
ventured to interfere. “I think I’d let her go now,”
he said. “Would you?” retorted Wagstaff. “You
wouldn’t. If you had a wife like this you wouldn’t
miss no opportunity nor take no chances.” And
Wagstaff slapped his Missus again. you’re
right,” said Shannon, and went his way. When he
looked back, driving down the dusty road to Little
River, Mrs. Wagstaff was still roaring, and Wag-
staff was still sitting on the stone, smoking and
casually putting the case to Milly with his belt.

“Gouger”: The lawyer vine takes the
place in N.Q. of bullock-hide and fencing wire in
N.S.W., S.A. and Victoria. It is used extensively
by the white, and to Binghi it is indispensable.
Whether he proposes to climb a 70ft. pine or a 20ft.
cocoanut palm, Binghi cuts a strong length of the
vine that will just reach around the tree; then he
grasps an end tightly in each hand, and walks up
the tree by his big toes, every now and then jerking
his hand-gripped vine higher. When building his
gunyah, Binghi cuts a couple of dozen 10ft. lengths
of the mature vine (stout cane), sticks both ends in
the ground and thus makes an arch, into which he
roughly plaits other canes. A few palm branches
are casually thrown over the wicker-work basket, a
hole poked through for a door, and, inside, Binghi
defies wind, rain and debil.
* » * *
“Tiaki”: I admire the self-assured way
in which “Te Kuri” (B. 24/10/’l2) shoulders his
way into “this dog controversy.” It isn’t “faintly
possible that kuri was derived from the attempt to
pronounce the English word” (presumably cur). I
thought that I had explained to “Tumumu” that
kuri, in its general sense, means quadruped. Also,
“kararehe” means dog. If Henare, who had his dog
even in Hawaiiki before he berthed aboard the fast
sailing canoe Arawa, bound for Maoriland, imitated
“cur” with “kuri,” will “Te Kuri” tell me what Eng-
lish word he imitated with “kararehe?” Also, it
isn’t faintly possible that Henare picked up his
ejaculation “ara” from some Irish jarvey who used
“arrah” to his cab-horse; neither is he copying the
white man when he calls his dad “papa.” That
word is his own. He is not drawing on the white
man’s vocabulary when he says “ngau” (which
means to gnaw). Also, “ringa” (hand) was not
filched from the fact that Henare had seen a hand
used to ring a bell —or a fowl’s neck. There cer-
tainly is a story to the effect that a Maori, strange
to a certain district, asked a grumbling bulloekv
the name of a certain hill. Bullocky replied,
“Blanked if I know.” Since that time Henare has
always referred to that mound as “Puke-ti-whai-no.”
Puke means hill. Similarly, “heketere” does not
mean secretary. “Hekeretere” is the word. The
word shilling is not pronounced “hiringi,” nor is it
spelt so on the court forms (as per writ against
“Te Kuri” attached, fining him two cases of beer
for ignorance of his subject, interpretation fees in-
cluded). It is pronounced as spelt, “hereni.” Also,
does “T.K.” mean to say that Henare waited in
M.L. for hundreds of years before he had enough
intelligence to give names to the different points
of interest in this loan land of the South Pacific?

“Marloo” : Either the person who directs
the agricultural column of a certain metro-
politan weekly has had his walking appara-
tus pulled, or he is using the backbone of
the country as a butt for an outsize in jokes.
Recently he advised the farmer of a “new and
effective method” of eradicating stumps. I had
the great displeasure of witnessing a trial of the
method a few days ago, and I reckon I’m lucky to
be alive to tell the tale. Working according to
directions, Durrum bored a hole in the sawn-off
tree, uncorked two bottles, which contained nitric
and sulphuric acid respectively, poured the con-
tents into the hole, and belted in a hardwood plug.
He then remarked that in six weeks the deadly

acids would reduce the stump to such a state that
it could be kicked to pieces. But just here the
acids exploded with such effect that Durrum fell
backwards off the tree-butt, while a portion of his
face-herbage raced into the clouds. Have concluded
that the agricultural expert has confused his plan
for the destruction of stumps with some experiments
for the annihilation of airships. I’m sending you
Durrum’s opinion in a fire-proof tank.
“Gouger” : The Queensland scrubs are in-
fested just now with that irritating pest, the “red
itch.” Have seen desperate men scratch their flesh
until the blood oozed. Close investigation in such
cases shows hundreds of minute crimson specks.
Each speck is a tiny tick, and when an odd hundred
or two get up the leg of a man’s pants he will sit
down for hours to dig out the almost invisible devils
rather than keep them for company. Turps and
vaseline mixed is a good smothering cure; but on
tender parts the sting of the turps makes a man say
things that should be whispered into an iron tank at
the bottom of a gully.

“Vagabond” : It’s all very well for all these
bushy “Abos” to keep slinging off at the immigrant
idiot, and his throw-the-cow-to-milk-her perform-
ances. But, all the same, there are
times and places where the bushiest
of ’em would be made to look
sillier at what they consider their
own special jobs than the immi-
grantest immigrant that ever im-
migranted. Imagine an Australian
crack shearer landing by some odd
chance on the Shetland Islands.
(He might freeze to death, or step
off the edge, before he’d time to
look for a job, but suppose he
didn’t.) Picture a cocky farmer
there asking him if he could tackle
a job to get the wool off a score or
two of sheep. Can’t you see that
ringer chuck a chest and brag that
it’ll just warm him and give him
an appetite for breakfast, to strip
200 or 300 while the porridge-billy
is being boiled. And what would
he say—oh, what would he say—
when he found that the sheep were
not to be shorn, but that they were
to be PLUCKED ? Plucked like a
pullet—the wool pulled out with
the hands. All the same, that is
how the wool is harvested in the
Shetlands. The sheep are allowed
to run till the warm weather has
loosened the coat—or p’raps I
ought to say till the Arctic blizzard
weather is over and allows the
sheep to exist without his winter
overcoat. Usually the women do
the “shearing,” and they hold the
sheep down, get a grip of a hand-
ful of the wool and tug gently at
it. If it comes out they take an-
other handful. If it hangs on and
threatens to bring a foot of skin vtith
it, that handful is left to loosen.
up later on, and another portion of the coat is tried.
Sometimes on the hills you’ll see sheep with one side
plucked bare, while the other has long festoons of
wool hanging to it. Can’t explain why one side
should part up easier than t’other, but it’s so. And,
finally, Shetland wool has a reputation for quality
way up as far as it can be without being toe
lonely.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He came to us from Biliouspill,
A Gippsland centre where the cow
Works over all her awful will.
Jim was her slave. I see him now,
With bended back and slouching gait,
A hide tanned like a rusted rail,
And legs bowed to accommodate
The everlasting milking pail.
He’d come to see the races run,
\nd back a horse from Biliouspill.
He told of doughty deeds he’d done,
And wondrous feats of milking skill.
We came home late that night, and Jim
Had gone to bed some time before.
We sat to supper by the dim,
Rank kerosene an hour or more.
Then Jim came, yawning; took a seat;
Next paused our racket to condemn;
Then fell upon the bread and meat.
This was at ten to two, a.m.
Ten minutes Jim in silence fed,
Then looked out on the morning drear,
And yawned again, and sadly said:
“By cripes, you breakfast early here!”
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.

“Malcolm”: Following up “Mallara-
poonie’s” treatise on the memory of the camel, I’ll
tell you a true story which, if it does not prove that
the beast of the desert has a memory, will amply
demonstrate that he can bear a grudge with the
worst of us. A teamster in the employ of Hender-
son and Macgregor, at Malcolm (W.A.), had given
a bull-camel the father of a hiding one afternoon.
Towards evening a couple of the teamster’s mates
took the camel a good three miles out into the
mulga, hobbled it closely and left it there for the
night. Hours afterwards the sleeping camp was
suddenly terrified by awful cries for help coming
from under one of the waggons. We rushed to the
spot, and found that the camel had dragged itself
back to camp, pulled the man who had cruelly ill-
used it from under his waggon, and was tossing
him as a terrier does a squealing rat. By the time
we had beaten the enraged brute off we expected to
find our mate dead, but things were not so bad as
that. He merely had two broken legs and the flesh
on several parts of the body torn from the bones.
Twelve months’ hospital and very careful attention
enabled him to dodge the undertaker.
* * * *
“Flinderino” : Re crocodiles and feathered
foods. I was navigating a flatty up one of the
numerous creeks that empty into the Cairns Inlet,
North Queensland. My mate drew attention to a
diminutive crocodile that was sunning itself on the
roots of a mangrove. The infant was less than 18in.
long—not much bigger than a full-grown Johnnie-
Jumper. Althoug’h we pelted it with oyster shells
it didn’t shift —not until I almost hit it with an
oar. Coming down the creek an hour later we again
saw the juvenile ’dile swimming across the creek,
and it had a bird in its jaws. My pal let fly with
the rifle, but all we got was a few feathers. Some
years ago an old woman who ferried passengers
across the Barron River at the Strathford crossing
told me that her ducklings had been disappearing in
a mysterious way. She suspected Hop Ivwong Ivee and
Co., whose banana plantation was in the neighbor-
hood, and she informed the police to that effect.
But one day she happened to be watching a gosling
that was sporting in the stream, and presently there
was just a gentle swirl and eddy and no gosling,
though the river remained. The ’dile didn’t take
that. A poultry farmer of my acquaintance, whose
selection was near the mouth of the Russell River,
sometimes caught juvenile crocodiles in a wooden
drain on his farm. The channel was about 100yds.
long, and ran from the back of the house to. the
river. The young crocs used to sneak up the drain
and lay in wait for the innocent ducklings that
bathed in the gutter. Sometimes the drain was a
misfit. There is an old armored crocodile that lives
on the coast near Cape Grafton, close to the Yarra-
bah Aboriginal Mission Station. This ancient shell-
back was quite grown up when Abel Tasman cruised
thereabouts in his dreadnought. It is probably the
oldest inhabitant of Queensland, and its back is
covered with a thick deposit of oysters. The mis-
sion blacks say they have seen old oysterbaek catch
kingfishers and other birds. The crocogator would
be lying motionless on the sandbank and apparently
soundly snoozing. A couple of bright-colored king-
fishers would be perched on its back, preening their
feathers. Some more birds would join in and start '
skylarking around the monster. Suddenly the big
tail would lash out, one swing to the right and one
to the left. There would be few unbroken bones in
the bird that collided with the flying tree of meat.
Old armor-clad would gather up the tit-bits and take
them to a place of safety to ripen.
McNorth”: One method of discerning
the approach of spring in Torres Strait is to watch
the mainland Binghi’s bingey. When the “winter”
TROUBLE AHEAD.
AUSTRALIAN SCOUNT: “By Cripes! Ij old
Wilson sees them Jap s fright’ n’ his cows
there’ll be a row all right.”
has given things best for the year, the luscious wild
fruit, known as wongai, comes into bearing, and
Binghi’s bread-basket blooms accordingly. All he
has to do is to send the missus up the trees, pick
up the fruit, and sit and eat till sleep cuts appetite
short. When he awakes the lady does some mo>
climbing and the gent, some more eating—and sleep-
ing. After a few days of this, our dark relation be-
comes too sleepy to eat, and his equator assumes
the proportions of a camel’s hump. In a week or
so he turns on one side and grunts a request for
more fruit, and the old order is repeated till the
bare trees tell the nigger to search elsewhere for his
provender.
“Konini”: I enter the following for the
Uncontrollable Fury Stakes. It occurred at Milton
(South Island of M.L.), and was briefed thus:—
The evidence fcr the prosecution was to the effect
that the bullock was exhausted and lay down by the
roadside, while being driven from Akatore to Milton,
that defendant broke its horns, and broke its tail
off, also that he dragged it to some tussocks and set
fire to it while it was alive.
The magistrate fined the demented savage £5, and
went so far as to say that if he could have been
absolutely certain that the roasting was commenced
before the mutilated animal was dead, the penalty
might have been even more. Judging by appear-
ances, it might have been £5 Os. 2£d.
