“Eddyson”: A tragedy of Bull crossing,
now 30 years old. A small bark hut on the side of
a moonlit hill, with a bark door, and a bark chim-
ney. Just beyond the spare trees; the bush there has
been pushed back to the brow of the hill. Below, a
thin, scattered township of iron and bark, with a
battery drumming somewhere in the distance. ' A
woman comes stumbling along the tree line, her
dark hair falling loosely about her, the front of her
white night-dress stained black with the blood oozing
from a wound in her breast. She stag-
gers a few steps at a time, and pauses
to fight for breath. She falls often, and
makes a brave attempt to continue the
journey on her knees. It is two o’clock.
The men off night shift are all a-bed ;
there is none to see, no one to lend a
helping hand. She reaches the hut door
at length, pulls the string that liberates
the latch, and falls into the darkness
within. Three minutes she lies still on
the floor, and then, lifting herself on her
hands, calls faintly : “Tom ! Tom ! He’s
shot me, Tom! Tom! Tom! Tom!
Dan’s shot me. He found out, and he
shot me. Tom!” There is no movement
on the bunk, where, in a tumble of blan-
kets and an old, torn ’possum rug, a man
is sleeping. The woman calls agaiif, but
her voice is like the thin complaining of
a dying child, and cannot be heard. She
creeps to the bunk, pulls herself to her feet, and
feels for Tom’s face. She cannot shake him into
wakefulness. Finding the matches by the bedside,
she makes feeble efforts to strike a light. When she
succeeds, her quavering hand holds the light over
Tom Walsh’s stark, white face. Tom is dead. Dan
had been there before her.
“Briefless”: Wouldn’t advise anyone to
try eating fresh-\yater turtle (tortoise). It is
sudden death on fowls —that I know by experience—
and as Ilaflz or Abdul Hamid, or someone hath it,
“What ducks cannot digest, that let the stomach of
man shun.” True, there is a market for these rep-
tiles among the Chinese. But there is no evidence
that the Mongol eats them. In fact, since he still
remains alive in large numbers, the testimony is all
the other way.
* * # •
“Mopoke” : Can any of the scientists who
disport themselves over the Abo. pages tell me how
long a water-lizard (not to be confused with his
first cousin Frill Neck) can stay under water?
Recently saw one flop into a pool and remain under
the surface for two hours 55 minutes. At the time
of my departure, judging by the indifferent ex-
pression on his face (which, by the way, is not
unlike Joe Chamberlain’s), he was in for the day.
“Giddap” : The world is full of Mystery—
fairly crawling with it. I was digging out rabbits
the other day—a favorite pastime in my part of the
world—and came upon a tunnel 40 ft. long, the
diameter of which varied from six to 10 inches.
Although it was freshly made, there was not a
shovelful of dirt at the mouth. What do they do
with the dirt? Eat it or what? Another problem
that occurs to me is: What is the greatest depth to
which rabbits will burrow? In sandy ground, in
the Rochester (Vic.) district, I and a mate at-
tempted to call on Bunny at his apartments in the
basement, and I give you my word we had to rig a
windlass when we were half —but, there, you won’t
believe me.
‘Sr” : Those Abo-liars who have been try-
ing to close on the fiercest wind stakes wouldn’t
know a decent blow if they met one. At Bridge-
water (Southern Tasmania), what we would call an
ordinary puff of wind will take the gold stopping
out of your teeth. Most of the men of the district
are bald, and the lamp posts are built underground.
All beer is frozen. If served in liquid form the wind
would blow it to Gehenna. In the summer a decent
gale will blow meat.
“Sir Octopus”: This argument concern-
ing the facial architecture of my family satisfies me
that herds of people who describe the octopus in
great detail have never seen , one. The best de-
scription I’ve met is by Victor Hugo, a man who
knew his subject. Said he:—
It (the octopus) looks like a bundle of rags, and
resembles in shape a closed umbrella without i
handle. This misshapen mass advances towards you
slowly; suddenly it opens ; eight long tentacles ex-
tend from around a face with two dull glassy eyes.
• . . .It is a spider in its shape. . . .It is like a sensi-
tive plant.... English mariners call them devil-fish
and blood-suckers. In the Channel Islands they are
spoken of as picuvre... .It has two large, hut in-
distinct eyes, resembling the color of the sea. When
the picuvre is in pursuit of prey, or lying in wait,
it grows smaller, reduces itself as it were, and is
scarcely discernible in the half light beneath the
sea. . . .A lump of slime that has an instinct. . . .The
picuvre is a hypocrite.... its approach is heralded
by no sound.... It has neither bone, flesh or blood.
.... There is one orifice in the centre of its ten-
tacles. Is it a mouth or vent? The same open-
ing serves both purposes. It is the entrance and
the exit.
Now, will “Reka,” “A.N.,” and “N. or M.” get back
to their hay ?
“Wandi” : When you have sampled many
ways and means of getting elsewhere in the great
spaces of the interior, including rides on donkey-back
and camel-back, in shanghais, sulkies and waggon-
ettes, in mail coaches, carts and bullock-drays, per
boot and per bike, you can appreciate a railway car
much more than when you come off a sea voyage,
or have been used to motoring on civilised roads. It
is the most satisfactory way of travelling when you
have to cover long distances —but as a means of
seeing the country it is a dead failure. Most over-
sea visitors, whose object is a book on Australia,
stick to the comfortable railroad, which accounts for
the preponderance of false impressions. The main
scenery on a railway line is embankments, inter-
spersed with tunnels and long lines of heavy fences,
the bleached rails whereof invite you to try Killem’s
pills, and to drink Hogg’s whisky. From the seat of a
vehicle you have a broad panorama spread around
you most of the time. On horseback you get in
closer touch with it. Travelling on a bike you see
little but the track in front of you ; like the donkey,
the wheel requires too much attention. The camel
affords you a fine, lofty view—well shaken as you
take it. Perched among the elaborate trappings with
which the Centralian fits his riding camel, and en-
joying a fragrant cheroot, you might imagine your-
self an Asiatic potentate, especially when you find a
little place called Jericho on your northern track.
But the best way of all to see the country, and to
absorb the scenery, and all the local features and
peculiarities, and to learn the habits and character of
the inhabitants, is per boot. There is no one living
who knows Australia more intimately than the swag-
man.
# # * *■
“Yarrum” : “Talk about horse sense !” said
Casey. “Dog sense licks it to a frazzle. Once, at
Waratah (N.S.W.), I lived next door to a Scotch
collie. One morning I went out to the dividing
fence, and emptied some meat into a tin the brute
used to feed out of. He looked his thanks at me.
Next morning I heard a banging against the back
door, and, when I opened it, there he wag, with his
battered tin in his mouth, and a supplicating look
about his countenance. I contributed a couple of
chops. After that he used to bail me up with his
tin wherever he saw me, and I had to clear out to
the Sydney side of Newcastle to get shut of him.
Last time I met him was in Hunter-street. He
knew me at once, and tears of annoyance fairly
coursed down his jowl. You see, he hadn’t his tin
with him.”

“W’lang” : Corio is both Italian and Aus-
tralian aboriginal, says a writer (B. 31/10/’l2). Well,
Ballarat is Gaelic as well as Australian. In both
cases it carries an “a” more than now commonly
used, and means the same, practically, in each in-
stance. In Gaelic the meaning is “The meeting of
the waters,” and in abo. “A good camping place.”
MEMORIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The world has changed a mighty piece
Since breezes used to blow , '
Across the pigs, and calves, and geese,
And through ,my sprouting mo.;
Since Mary used to coyly smile
At me beside the fire,
What time I used, with youthful guile,
To dodge her father’s ire.
The pigs have long since turned to pork;
The calves are mostly cows;
The geese have vanished—on a fork.
And what of Mary’s vows?
She married long ago, I’m told
By chaps who came from there,
. A butcher who was fat and old,
And innocent of hair.
The horses Mary used to ride
(She took a dainty seat!)
Most un-po-et-i-cally ‘ died
Of not enough to eat. 1
The book of verses that I wrote
To Mary long ago
Has shed its leaves' to gaily float
On all the winds that blow, i !•■■■'
The ribbons that I gave (ah! dregs!)
With many raptures, then,
I’m told are tied around the legs
Of some old ducky hen.
The log whereon we used to sit
And share our joy and grief
Was info pig-sty palings ■ split
By him who murders beef.
And that old stump, where every night
We met at nine o’clock
Our young affections to requite,
I? now a butcher’s block.
Thus has Romance got stained with grease
Since breezes used to blow
Across the pigs, and calves, and geese
When I was Mary’s beau.
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.

PAHI HOTELKEEPER BAD WITH COLIC.
Mr. Joseph Ryan, hotelkeeper of Pahi (N.Z.), was
very bad with colic, and tried many remedies, with-
out results. Then the Honorable Mrs. Scotland ad-
vised his wife to give him Chamberlain’s Colic 3nd
Diarrhoea Remedy. He says: “I was sceptical and
refused to take it. I reckoned such things were no
good. At last I got so bad that my wife prevailed
upon me to try it. After two doses I was right, and
have been so ever since. I was in a bad way, I can
tell you. but now f swear by Chamberlain’s Colic and
Diarrhoea Remedy, and always keep it handy.”*

A TIMELY VISIT.
"But do you think the Japs will weally come

Heard his final “stop,” W. D. Cruick-
shank, for many years chief marine sur-
veyor to N.S.W.'Government, and Board of
Trade Examiner for Engineers. Cruick-
shank was known as the father of marine-
engineer surveying in the Ma State, and
his work on boiler-construction is a text-
book all over Australia; also, his remarks
on engineers’ examinations are still studied,
though 20 years old, while “Cruickshank’s
Questions” continue to be crammed down
the valves of the budding marine engineer.
On his retirement in 1900 he was appointed
employers’ representative to the N.S.W.
Arbitration Court as then constructed, but
was knocked over by a paralytic stroke soon
Ritorwards.
Four years or so ago Edward Officer—one
of the Officers of a Lower Darling squattage
—held in Melbourne an exhibition of his
paintings, which failed to attract any special
attention. He has spent the intervening
time in European study, and now again
stands ready to receive the brick of criti-
cism and the red wafer of admiration at
Melbourne Athenaeum. A critic might be
excused for imagining the SO pictures to be
the work of two artists. Officer has achieved
a certain impressionistic prettiness in his
paintings of scenes in Normandy and Brit-
tany ; on the other hand, the Australian
landscape labelled “Relentless Summer” is
full of stony realism. In his larger Aus-
tralian works he succeeds in producing
the idea of limitless plains, by filling up two-
thirds or more of his canvas with sky ; and
in at least one effort, “The Woolshed,” he
has harnessed the Australian sunlight to his
paint waggon, and driven it into the picture
without upsetting the vehicle. Some of the
seascapes are good, but conventional. Others
are merely conventional.
BARTON, GRIFFITH AND O'CONNOR, OF THE AUSTRALIAN HIGH COURT.
Amongst the guests at Bathurst (N.S.W.)
during that town’s jubilee the other day was
Mrs. Turpin, a daughter of G. W. Evans,
the surveyor who laid out the first 'road
from the Nepean to the Macquarie, and who
also discovered the Lachlan —the Governor
of the day was Lachlan Macquarie. Mr.
Evans, in the mid-thirties, kept a book-store
in Bridge-street, Sydney. He died near
Ilobart in 1552.
Mrs. Howarth, who died last week at
Leichhardt (N.S.W.), had some sort of a
distinction: she belonged to the most-
drowned family in Australia. Five of her
brothers went down in the H.M.S. Black
Prince during the Crimean War, and a
sixth went to the bottom in a West India-
man. Mrs. Howarth’s first husband, named
Fearnside, just escaped the family He
was in the Royal Engineers, and came to
Tasmania in the Lady Montague in 1852
with convicts. Instead of returning, he de-
cided to settle down there. So the Lady
Monty left Hobart without him —and wasn’t
seen again. The eldest son joined the navy,
and afteT having been wounded in the Ash-
antee War, also joined Davy Jones. There
are seven children living, 39 grandchildren
and 10 great-grandchildren; so there is still
plenty of material for the old curse to work
on.
Some capital will be returning to the
country shortly as a result of the Austra-
lian Labor Government’s “mad experimental
legislation.” William Cooper, described bv
Debrett as Baronet, of Woollahra, is to
arrive and reside permanently here as a pro-
test against the Fisher Ministry’s scoun-
drelly doings. He settled his property some
time ago so as to leave his family a certain
considerable amount and himself and his
wife. a matter of £12,000 per annum. But
his income is almost entirely derived from
Woollahra and other Sydney suburban
house property, the taxation on which, with
the absentee impost super-added, runs into
about £BOOO a year. No self-respecting
baronet can exist on £4OOO a year in Eng-
land, so the old gentleman has decided to
attempt the feat of battling along in these
uncivilised parts on £lOO or so a week. If
he succeeds it will be yet another* tribute to
that indomitable British pluck which has
enabled the Flag of Empire—and so forth
and so on. The preliminary effort will pro-
bably be made in the house of the Herbert
Allen family—a villa residence at Double
Bay. The homing exile is a shade over 60.
He married a Hill, of Sydney (two of her
sisters reside in that city already), and he
has a son and heir.
Dunn, Labor M.L.A. for Mudgee (N.S.W.),
whose dramatic resignation some months
ago struck the Parliamentary machine in
the solar plexus, assisted at a different sort
of ceremony last week, when his parents
celebrated their golden wedding. Dunn,
sen., has been 60 years in Australia, and
the effects of the enervating climate, and
the loneliness of the vast spaces, and the
dreariness of the bush, and all the other
devils that haunt us. were clearly shown
when the family handed up the golden-
wedding gift. It was a saddle !
Archbishop Carr mourns his most liberal
layman, Tom Verga, the printer. Verga
only had a small-looking business —originally
Stubbs and Verga, in Little Collins-street—
yet, in the course of years, he spent £12,000
on St. John’s, Clifton Hill, and, dying, he
leaves it property valued at £4OOO. When
Mr. and Mrs. Verga started their benefac-
tions, St. John’s was a dismal ruin. Now
it is a thing of beauty, and the home of a
real live church, under the Rev. McCarthy,
the Archbishop’s Chancellor of the Ex-
chequer.
When Ferrer, who stood for secular edu-
cation, was shot at Barcelona, the Spanish
Premier Canalejas was promised a sudden
ending. Since then the clouds had cleared,
and the Premier was so satisfied with the
general calm that he used to walk the streets
of Madrid quite unattended. Last week he
paid for his misplaced confidence with his
life. Considering his environment, Canale-
jas was a very advanced man, though he
represented peaceful evolution as opposed
to sudden revolution. Spain’s tr< ,:ble is
that there is no large man visible ~o take
his place; and Spain wants a large man
very badly.
Poor John Murray is in dreadful trouble
over something he said at the Mayor of
Melbourne’s dinner. In his efforts to be
humorous where women are concerned, John
Weary always combines the playfulness of
the bear with the adroitness of the china-
shop bull. This time he out-Murrayed Mur-
ray. Talking of the “Queen City of the
South,” he looked at the array of expen-
sively-dressed women in the Town Hall bal-
conies—women who had come prepared to
hear themselves described as guardian angels
of. the feast, or words to that effect —and
said in his loud, ponderous tones: “Mel-
bourne, unlike the ladies, grows more beau-
tiful as she becomes older.” Whereupon
350 women of uncertain age muttered
‘ Beast!” and 550 elderly feeders remarked
that the speaker, with one little syllable, had
turned a pat of butter into a dose of physic.
Martin, the biggest Chief Justice
N. S. Wales has had :
The largest monument in the old R.C. cemetery
at Parramatta is over the remains of John Mar-
tin, of Fermoy, Ireland, who died in 1861, aged 75
years, and Mary, his wife, who died in 1876, aged 81
years. They were the parents of Chief Justice James
Martin. When Mrs. Martin died, the ‘‘Chief” jour-
neyed to Parramatta to personally see to the pre-
parations. He found the vault filled with water.
Stepping across the road he purchased buckets, etc.,
at a store, and employed a couple of men to bale out,
while he chatted with the oldest inhabitants. When
there was no water left for the buckets to work on,
James Martin had them filled with beer for the bene-
fit of the hangers-round. The “Chief” and his wife
lie in an Italian marble vault above ground, on the
cliffs by the sea at Waverley Cemetery. The wife
selected the spot, and had the monument built. The
‘Chief’s” body had been in the vault of the Longs
at Randwick ; but the wife had it removed, deciding
that, if somewhat separated in life, they should be
at least united in death.
. Hal Gye is now represented in the smok-
ing-room of the Yorick Club, London, by a
caricature in color of John Coates, the
somewhat horse-like tenor of the Quinlan
Opera Co. Coates, as Lohengrin, was done
to the order of Mr. Edgar Carter, of Golds-
brough, Mort and Co., who is a member of
the Yorick, and has two brothers of some
note in Cockney bohemian circles. One is
a well-known actor, the other is art critic
and editor of “The Year’s Art.” Alexander
Watson had the drawing when he left Mel-
bourne for London a few weeks ago, and
undertook to deliver it safely. Watson, by
the way, bought a libellous caricature of
himself from Gye, and appeared to be
tickled to death” by it. Just now the
artist is meditating over a commission from
Margaret Cooper, who wants a caricature
portrait which will “be not quite so horrid”
as the one drawn for The Bulletin. The
lady’s sense of humor will permit horridness
—in moderation.
A FAILING.
“And how do you like the new bishop?"
“ Ah, dear brother, a most worthy man; but
he gets so awfully angry when he loses at
cards."
Pymble, on the North Shore (Sydney)
railway, is having a new Anglican church
dedicated to St. Swithin, and the parishioners
are asking the Archbishop who this saint
was, and how he gained canonisation. There
is no other church in Australia named after
him, they discover. The Archbishop tells
them, and in return they might tell him, if
they know, who Jt'ymbie was. Bill was his
front name, and SO years or thereabouts ago
he kept a little, lonely, wayside pub in tne
district. It can hardly be said to have
kept him, because he had to add fruit-
growing and wood-carting, in order to knock
out a living.
"Tiny” Wilson, the detective, has just
died, in Melbourne. He came in the second
Victorian flight, working with Commissioner
O’Callaghan, Brown, Christie, Coleman,
Hartley, Jennings, Lomax, Mackey, Mahony,
Nixon, Ward, and the O’Donnells. With
the first flight were Eason, Black and “old
Jack” Williams. They had desperate crim-
inality to deal with, and were up to it. Ever
since Black’s day there has been a sighing
that the men are not what they were. Yet
such as Berliner and Wilson were smart
enough. Wilson did brilliant things; never-
theless from the front line he subsided into
mere Customs work, preceding Christie.
After that he went to the Railways, while
little lynx Lomax, at the Post Office, was
presently succeeded by O’Callaghan’s keenly
efficient partner, Nixon. All through, the
Melbourne practice seems to be to apply the
first-class men to the second-class work.
T. A. DIBBS,
45 years General
Manager of the
Commercial
Bank of Sydney.
Died last week, aged 76, Samuel Clift, a
native of East Maitland (N.S.W.), and a
very “solid” citizen of those parts. The
Clift family owned for many years the
cattle run known as Breeza, at one time
considered to be about the world’s end.
Later on, the discovery of coal in the Clift
properties put much coin in the family’s
pocket. Samuel had an occasional thirst
for public life; but he wasn’t the sort to
make a popular politician.
MRS. ONSLOW’S REVENGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With a wife’s indignation Mrs. Onslow spoke her
mind about the man who had caused her husband,
the member for Waverley, to be ejected from the
Legislative Assembly Chamber. “I don’t think,”
she said, in addressing a gathering of women Liber-
als, “that Mr. Willis is worth reading about. I
don’t know why he should occupy the best sheet
of the morning papers. I always turn that sheet
over when I see a reference made to Mr. Willis, and
I miss other matter that is good in consequence.”
—Sydney D.T., 15/11/’l2.
Oh, my dear!
Did you hear
Mrs. Onslow speak of Willis? So impressive! So
Now what
Is that word? You know the one, dear!... .Never
mind now, I’m too hot—
After hearing how he treated her. dear husband. ’Pon
my word
It’s disgraceful! Yes, disgraceful! Really, dear,
I never heard
Such a story of disloyalty, ungratefulness and spite!
If they put That Man in prison it would only serve
him right!
And to think, my dear, he's sitting in the House as
large as life,
Saying things to - Mr. Onslow that offend his poor,
dear wife!
And of course, my dear, the Onslows —well, good
gracious, who is he
To dictate to any member of that splendid family?
Why it’s awful —simply awful! That Man Willis
has no right
To correct dear Colonel Onslow, who is polished and
polite!
After all, what is the Speaker? Almost anyone, it’s
clear,
If he likes can be a Speaker. Oh, it’s very simple,
dear!
Why, the very poorest Creature in the House can
wear a wig
And dictate to all his betters, if he does not care a
fig
For Tradition, Rank and Breeding ! Any Horror can
do that!
Hut he cannot be an Onslow! That is what they’re
raging at!
That, my dear’s the only reason for these vulgar
shouts and hoots—•
They are conscious they’re not worthy to black
Colonel Onslow’s boots!
So they simply try and crush him with the help of
What’s-His-Name.
But, my dear, that brave man sits there with his
heart and soul aflame!
And why does he? He’s an Onslow and a Liberal,
my dear !
And, though in most dreadful danger, scorns to
show a sign of fear.
And she will not read her paper!—hardly glances at
a line,
Just because it mentions Willis! Don’t you think
that’s really fine?
It is more, my dear! It’s queenly ! It just shows
how Onslows can
Calmly crush a horrid upstart! Oh, I wouldn’t be
That Man!
Just imagine, dear, your feelings if you heard your
very name
Spoilt dear Mrs. Onslow’s paper! Why, you'd simply
die of shame.
So you can’t tell me this Willis, heartless as he
seems to be,
Is unruffled by her action ! It must hurt him fright-
fully!
If it’s true he doesn’t worry and refuses to be
blamed.
Well, it’s what one might expect, dear, and he ought
to be ashamed !
N.S.W.
MARION O’HEAVENS.

There is one column there which suggests that traces
of an old trouble still remain on the premises.
This trouble was born with the boom of the early
’9o’s —the same sort of curse as the McGowen Gov-
ernment has been trying to fasten on to N. S. Wales
lately. When borrowing stopped and the lenders
wanted their money back, this bank had to admit
that it couldn’t pay. Feeling rather ashamed of
itself, it offered 4 p.c. on one-half and 41 p.c. on
the other half of the old debt (deposits, etc.). As
things turned out, the offer was too generous, and
a less stringent arrangement had to be made. This
revision practically meant that the bank should pay
3 p.c. instead of 41 p.c. on that half, the extra 11
p.c. being only payable when profits would stand it.
But the old creditors drove a pretty hard bargain.
In return for the just-mentioned concession the bank
must put £15,000 a year to reserves, to be avail-
able for paying interest; and one-fourth of the pro-
fit after various appropriations have been made
must go to repurchase deferred stock, which is the
least-well secured section of the old debt. For 1911
the profit put aside in accordance with this ar-
rangement was £12,587, with which £14,000 (face
value) of the deferred stock was purchased. (That
explains the £1413 profit on repurchase shown for
the past year.) This debt of £14,000 was can-
celled ; and to square the accounts a corresponding
amount is now written off Premises account. It is
for practical purposes the same as adding that
much to the reserves.
The next table shows what has happened to various
sections of the old debt since the last arrangement:
1896. 1910. 1911. 1912.
Debenture stock £983,547 £983,547 £983,547 £983,547
Terminable re-
ceipts .. 891,454 Nil Nil Nil
Pref. insc. stock 889,419 889,419 859.419 889,419
Deferred stock.. 808,758 759,500 748,000 734,000
£3,573,178 £2,632,466 £2,620,966 £2,606,966
Profits, it will be seen, from the first table, have
mounted very rapidly; and that is largely due to
the fact that times have been getting good, and
that this bank has had the use of a very large amount
of money at a fixed rate of interest. The old debt,
as shown above, is £2,606,966; the new debt (de-
posits, etc.), is £6,535,377 ; total £9,142,343. If,
as the result of a brisk demand for money or other
causes, the bank averages but an extra i p.c. on this
amount, it means an extra £22,856 of profit. A
very little change thus makes a big difference in
this bank’s profits. It is a beautiful position to be
in when things are booming. But when things are
slumping, and bad debts are being made, the risk
is unpleasantly big. For it has to be remembered
that the paid capital is only £539,438 ; and that
and the comparatively small reserves have, of
course, to' stand the whole racket, since depositors
and other creditors will want their capital and their
interest no matter what happens to the assets.
The accounts now on the table closed on June 30,
and there was a lot better feeling in Australia at
that date than there is at the present. Still, even
so, the E.S. and A. shows an unusual gain in de-
posits for the year—£6l2,9s4. Of this amount
£372,618 was lent out. Apropos the silly whimper
of some of the boomster-Laborites that the banks
are locking up the money just for spite, it will be
seen from the next table that during the past year
this concern let out considerably more than it ac-
cepted on fixed deposit. That is to say, it lent a
good deal of the current-account money—money
which is liable to be called for at any moment: —

In spite of the pretty large growth of advances dur-
ing the year, the cash position is quite good. The
new debt is £6,535,377; and the liquid assets are
£3,556,713, or no less than 54 p.c. of those liabili-
ties. The new depositors’ position is, indeed, bet-
tered by the existence of the old debt. That debt
is represented by assets of one sort and another;
and the current business is a first charge on all the
assets. From the shareholders’ point of view, the
old debt adds to the nrofits when profits are on tap,
and to the losses when things go wrong. It swings
the pendulum farther in either case.

Thus, more than half the total amount of the de-
posits, etc., has been loaned to the State Govern-
ment. In the past 10 years the deposits, etc., have
increased by £9,855,974 ; and no less than
£6,386,572 of that money has been borrowed by
the State. No wonder the State Governments hang
on to this easy Cohen like grim Death. It is the
only Cohen from whom they can borrow practically
without asking his leave. Combining Government
stock and debentures with other loans, either guar-
anteed by the State or raised by some public author-
ity (such as the Melbourne Board of Works), it will
be found that 70 p.c. of the entire assets are repre-
sented by those items.

The bank has been allowing p.c. on deposits up
to £lOO and 3 p.c. on anything in excess of £lOO
up to £250. That limit, which had been unaltered
for 32 years, was shot up to £350 on the appear-
ance of the Commonwealth Bank. The rate actually
averaged by depositors was £2 19s. Bd. (nearly).
' This bank consists really of two departments—the
Savings Bank proper (to which these figures refer)
and the Credit Foncier, or Advances to Settlers De-
partment. The latter raises money by issuing de-
bentures ; what the public does not take the Savings
Bank absorbs. Of £2,322,242 outstanding on June
30, the department owed the public £976,092 and
the Savings Bank £1,346,150. In regard to neither
this nor any other part of the business is a large
profit aimed at; it is enough if the margin makes a
respectable addition to the reserves, which are being
built up to meet contingencies. So far as one can
see, these risks are not heavy. Indeed, they are not
as heavy as they should be, because the bank lends
such a ridiculously small amount on mortgage—to
assist people to build homes of their own and for
similar good purposes.

“Debentures” is too vague. The rest look all right,

mortgages are a desirable security for a fire office.
The interest earnings are certainly admirable. Last
year they were £20,070, or over 20 p.c. on the
capital; so that in order to pay even a 25 p.c. div.
it wasn’t necessary for the directors to take as much
as £5OOO out of the underwriting profit: all the
rest went to strengthen the co.’s position, and make
it dead-surer than ever that the policy-holder won’t
find the till empty, though an extra-sized disaster
should get in just ahead of him.

That 12 p.c. div. is a lot better than it looks in
the last line. You need to read the other lines,
“Such-and-such Reserves transferred to Capital.” In
1900 the paid capital was £37,500. It is now
£200,000, though the shareholders haven’t put a
penny more into the business. On the £37,500 paid
in 1900, the present div. is, therefore, 64 p.c., in-
stead of 12 p.c. A co. is quite entitled to do this
sort of thing. Within certain limits it is even
justified in doing it. If a co. made £50,000 profit,
nobody would throw a brick, supposing it divided
£35,000. And having divided the £35,000, no-
body would be entitled to throw the brick if it pre-
sently got in £20,000 more capital, assuming that
it wanted the money. There is no essential dif-
ference between that process and the capitalisation
of reserves. In the one case you divide the profits
and then call them in again ; in the other you don’t
let them out of your custody—you transfer them to
capital straight away. No doubt this system of
capitalising profits often has a bad smell about it
somewhere. But it can be perfectly legitimate.
In this case there seem to have been two main
purposes—(l) to wipe out an unpleasant liability on
the shares, and (2) to make the dividend look less
offensively big. It would certainly have been a safer
and more proper thing at one time if a little capital
had been got from the shareholders, because the
business grew so fast that the risks were out of all
proportion to the funds. Still the co. pulled through,
though even now it is in no extra-strong position.
Its net premiums last year were £200,694; its
capital and reserves are £328,175. Funds are, there-
fore, equal to 163 p.c. of the year’s premiums; and
though that indicates average strength, it doesn’t
put it in the front rank of Australasian offices, from
this one point of view. From other points of view
there is no question about where it stands. But its
business has grown, and still grows so fast (and
business means, of course, risks) that it needs to
also make its reserves grew fast. And that can only
be done by keeping a tight rein on dividends.

It promised to be a rotten year for Australian fire
offices ; but things improved, and the results are far
better than was at one time expected. Still, they
don’t compare well with those of the previous two
years ; and the outlook isn’t charming. It never is
for a fire and marine insurance office when stocks
are hard to shift and other slumpy conditions pre-
vail. Only one other point calls for notice. “In-
terest, fees, etc.,” brought in £13,082. That means
only 54 p.c. of the dividend. The underwriting
profit had to supply the rest. The dividend is too
big ; anyway, it takes too much of the underwriting
profit to pay it. But if the reserves are not grow-
ing as quickly as they should, what there is of them
is very good ; for no word of complaint can be made
about the quality of the assets, on the whole.

When these accounts come up for their post-mortem
it is always the same. The operator has either to
make a wild guess at figures, or hold them over for
12 months. And even at the end of 12 months he
can’t fill in the blanks satisfactorily. Twelve months
ago appropriations were reported:—6 p.c. on
pref. capital; 9J p.c. on ordinary capital (there
was no telling how many months some of this capital
had been paid up) ; 4i p.c. bonus on shareholders’ pur-
chases of merchandise; and a refund of 30 p.c. of
certain commissions. It was impossible to figure
the items out; and all the directors do now is to'
lump the lot together. The same this year: certain
appropriations are made—practically the'Same aS last
year, except that the ordinary shareholders get 1 p.c.
less but not for 12 months will it be known what
amount has been absorbed. The management gives
an assurance that the balance at profit and loss is
sufficient to meet the appropriations; that being so,
the reserves will probably work out at about
£93,000.
In 10 years the co.’s principal figures have moved
this way:—
Liabilities.
1901. 1910. 1911. 1912.
Capital . . £60,988 £227,721 £239,272 £308,382
Deposits . . 74,199 183,901 208,423 206,391
Other liabilities 81,611 142,166 192,556 213,390
Assets.
Advances . . 99,523 238,110 285,456 302,806
Stock .. .. 81,066 151,631 173,701 175,207
Freeholds, etc. 33,824 87,274 112.146 120,908
Book debts . . 30,883 103,497 123,630 171,861
Cash .. .. 11,947 62,231 52,139 74,844
This shows swift going. In 1901 the co. owed
£155,810 ; the debt is now £419,781. Meantime,
however, the shareholders’ funds (capital and re-
serves) have increased from £94,110 to (say)
£401,382 ; so, other things being equal, the de-
positors and other creditors are far better pro-
tected now than they were 11 years ago. The share-
holders’ future depends almost entirely upon the
management. There is no doubt about the latter’s
tremendous energy and enthusiasm; and, so far as
one can judge, there doesn’t seem much doubt that
it adds prudence to its other virtues. The thing
rests on a good basis, anyhow. There probably isn’t
anybody who stands to gain more from co-operation
than the farmer. He is rooked in a wholesale manner
—often because he hasn’t had a business training;
sometimes because he lives away out, and isn’t in
touch with the markets or with others in his own
bus ness; very often because he can’t pav till he
realises his next harvest, and is therefore at the
shark’s mercy. The real, live, economically-managed
co-operative concern which comes to his rescue conse-
quently deserves to be a success. And seeing that
this co.’s turnover has grown from £15.234 in 1882
to £2,961,688 in 1911, it looks as though it is
getting what it deserves.

Reserves wiped out; new co. formed.
Sept., 1912.. 36,828 Nil 22,254 14,574
Six months ago this paper repeated what it had
said many times previously—that the position was
far less satisfactory than the accounts suggested. A
profit of £16,391, a dividend of £9OOO and reserves
of £36,124 didn’t look like trouble; yet within
two or three months the directors were driven to a
reconstruction of which many of the shareholders
don t know much. There were 180,000 shares in the
010. co. paid to £1; there are 180,000 shares
in the new eo., but they carry a liability of 75., not-
withstanding that one call has already been made.
The man who dabbles in mining has an idea that
when his £1 shares are 20s. paid there’s an end to
his troubles. No more calls can be made, anyhow.
It is quite true; but it doesn’t take much ingenuity
to sell out to a new co., wind up the old one, and
thus dragoon the shareholder into a new liability.
Of course, a great improvement in the mine was
contemporaneous with the reconstruction. This invari-
ably happens. Often, no doubt, the improvement is
real enough ; it is the hook on which the call for
more capital is hung. There was an improvement in
this case. The ore hadn’t been so much dipping out
of the mine as making a bee-line across the border.
This body began to turn downwards a little, which
means that the ore will remain in the mine a while
longer than had been expected. But how much the
change is worth it is impossible to say.
Most ridiculous ore estimates have been put forward
from this mine. New estimates, it is alleged, “show
that there are 263,000 tons available for stoping.”
That ought to mean close-up £200,000 of profit if
the stuff were as good as that (say) of the Sulphide
Corporation and the costs as low. But the man who
goes nap on that assumption is very liable to be
cleaned out. In the 18 months up to March, 1912,
the mine was drawn upon to the extent of verv
nearly 200,000 tons of ore. All that the share-
holders got was £45,000, and the liquid assets were
less at the end of that period than at the beginning.
Certainly some plant was provided. But on this
co.’s ore, there never will be a time (certainly not
until much more is known about the treatment of
sulphides) when money will not have to be spent on
plant.
The directors, knowing that their own ore would
last them only a couple of years or so, decided to go
into the business of buying and treating ore and
products. _ It was to finance purchases that the re-
construction became imperative. There may be
money in it, or there may not. The co. has never
made such a success of its own surface operations as
to justify us in being sanguine. Meantime the co.
owes £73,520, much of it borrowed on usurious
terms, and much of it repayable very soon. The
litigation in which the) co. is now engaged will not
help matters. Equity proceedings are never cheap,
and the present trouble promises to be less cheap
than usual.
* * * *
Anderson’s Tanneries, Limited, has been
registered in Sydney, with a nominal capital of
£75,000, to take over the old-established tannery
business of Anderson and Anderson, Botany. The
new co. has Rowland J. Anderson as chairman of
the Board; R. J. Carter is tannery manager. The
business is now confined to the production of sole
leather, but will be extended to include the manu-
facture of belting and other leathers.
* * * *
Broken Hill Junction and Junction North
are bringing an old row to an issue, having now a
little money to play with. Incidentally, an important
point should be settled. Under the N.S.W. law one
mine is entitled to ventilation through another mine;
the inspectors can even order a connection to be
made with another mine ! s workings for the purposes'
of ventilation and escape. The Junction North has
only one shaft; and, it is alleged (and it isn’t likely
that this will .be disputed), it uses that shaft as a
downcast, which means that, partly by mechanical
means, it turns all its foul air (if it has any) into
the Junction. The point is whether a co. is en-
titled to use any mechanical means to pass its foul
air on to a neighbor. It is entitled to “ventilation”
through a neighbor’s workings. The courts will have
to say what “ventilation” means. The matter has
been pending, for 12 months or so, and settlement
has been found hopeless. .J. R. Edwards has been
advising the Junction North; W. A. Freeman the
Junction. Th£ case will come on next week.

Great Qobar, during the last three months,
treated 109(862 tons of ore for 1893 tons blister cop-
per,. containing, approximately, 10,800 oz. gold and
60,3930 z. silver. These products are valued at
£206,342, and expenditure is reckoned at £123,980.
For the previous three months 100,345 tons were
smelted for 2058 tons blister copper, carrying
14,3970 z. gold and 72,1560 z. silver. The grade "dur-
ing the September quarter fell off considerably;
although more ore was treated, the yield of blister
declined 165 tons. The gross profit "for six months
has been estimated at £150,000. Debenture-holders
claim a large share of this; still there is a better
prospect of a dividend than for years past. A good
many thousands of tons of copper have been taken
out since the last div. was paid in 1907. The yields
run: 1906-7, 6115 tons.; 1907-8, 4726 tons; 1908-9
5472 tons; 1909-11 (18 months), 9652 tons.
Re the Ardlethan (N.S.W.) tinfield and
the Carpathia (B. 7/11/ , 12). Logan Jack inspected
the latter last month. He found a bunch of ore ex-
posed 100 ft. long by 20ft. broad. He could not say
how deep it was; but assuming that it would go
down only 10ft., there would be 2500 tons of ore.
Allowing one-half to be mullock, and the other half
worth 33.3 p.c. tin, the value would be £93,750.
‘There is,” said Dr. Jack, “margin enough for profit
even if experience should prove the uncertain factors
in the calculation—viz., depth and grade—to have
been greatly over-estimated.” He concluded:
‘Although little work has been done on it, and there-
fore no estimate of its value can be made with anv
degree of confidence, in my opinion the new-born
Carpathia promises to become a great mine.” That
is one opinion. The other opinion, expressed by The
Bulletin, is that,_ “after the large quantity of tin
still lying _ around is gathered up, the owners will
have to bring themselves down to common earth and
get to work on a narrow lode of probably very
medium value.” Time only can tell which is right.
Meantime there is no difference of opinion as to the
richness of the ore in sight.
* * * *
The copper smelters of Australia are
having’ a bad time just now. The average politician
reckons that with copper at its present price, the
smelters must be making a fortune. Not so. The
smelter has to pay the high price for the metal,
consequently he requires more capital to carry the
same tonnage. His metallurgical losses are pretty
near a constant in percentage, so with high-price
metal he has high-value metallurgical losses. Con-
trary to the experience of the past, present high
price of metals has not induced greater production
amongst the smaller fry. With the relatively high
wages, and relatively high cost of coal and coke, the
local smelter is an easy prey to the foreigner. Dur-
mg the past fortnight a further 30 hands were
knocked off at the E. and A. Copper Co.’s works at
Newcastle. Before the foreign ore buyer got to work
m Australia, the E. and A. Co.’s works kept a dozen
furnaces going. Now they cannot keep one in blast.
Jhe end of Australian copper smelting is fast ap-
proaching under existing conditions ; and when that
happens the present Federal Government will cer-
tainly be hauled over the coals by some of its own
supporters for allowing an industry, essential to the
national life, to be knocked out without extending
to it even the shadow of protection.

Harden (N.S.W.) is being given a chance
to sa ye its bacon. Boring has been commenced from
the shaft where the No. 6 level would be if it were
worth driving. Unfortunately, where first pierced,
the reef hasn t improved upon its bad behavior at
the level above. Other bores will be put in ; and if
they don t strike any better hick the rest of the
operations at Harden will consist of cleaning things
up in preparation for the funeral.

Maoriland has given birth to some re-
markably virile financial institutions. The outside
world knows a few of them; but, from its very
nature, one of the most virile of the lot is less
familiar outside the Dominion. This is the N.Z.
Farmers’ Co-operative Association of Canterbury,
Ltd., which, from very small beginnings 30 years
ago, has now a volume of business exceeding
£ 3,000,000 a year.

The jays who are always wanting to have
a bit of a flutter in the mining market thought tn y
saw a great chance lately. There was the isroKe
Hill Junction, a mine, they told themselves, on the
main lode, alongside big dividend-payers ; yet share
were going for a few shillings. They ran the market
up, got infatuated, and in scores of cases woman t
take their profit. Now, when the market sags, they
write to the papers about the absurdity of it, ana
actually get some of their letters printed. One
“Shareholder” figured it out the other day that the
reserves (worth in the mine at least 18s. to 2us. per
ton) couldn’t be put down at less than £650,000,
and a few extras brought the total to £750,000,
vet the market valued the whole thing at £ IOO, UUU.
And the market is most likely right. The Junction
has been the rogue mine of the Barrier for nearly 25
years. Eighteen years ago it looked as though it
would go on coining money. Then the bottom
dropped out of it suddenly, the sky-rocketty divi-
dends stopped, and there has been nothing but re-
constructions and calls ever since. The Junction can
no more be likened to the B. H. South or the B. H.
North, or the other big Broken Hill mines than a
dog’s leg can be likened to a yard-stick. The Junc-
tion is at an elbow of the lode. There is no regu-
larity, no continuity thereabouts. Occasional rich
bodies occur —and disappear. The stuff is difficult
and costly to get out; and usually it is difficult and
costly to treat. J. C. Moulden tried his teeth on it
years ago. He has since proved himself a ratthng
good man ; but it was too hard a nut for him. Not
that it may not pay a dividend. It may pay naif a
dozen. But the div. or two are pretty certain to be
followed by calls and reconstruction. It is a
“stringer,” and no man who wants good sleep should
have it on his mind.
* * * *
Morning Star, Magnet (W.A.), has given
things best. For some time it has not made ends meet;
and as there are no signs of improvement, mining
and milling operations have ceased. In a recent re-
port reserves were given at 20,950 tons of 445. 6d.
ore ; but, as usual, mill returns have not come up
to the estimate. As a last resource the co. told the
Government that unless it guaranteed to make good
any loss up to £250 a month, the show would be
closed down and 50-odd, mostly married, men thrown
out of work. The audacity of the proposal nearly
staggered Scaddan and Co.
* * * *
Of the May Queen, beyond Wiluna
(W.A.), which a venturesome English co. was re-
cently induced to take on, a Government field geolo-
gist speaks thus: “Most of the work done is along
a series of quartz lenses which have been exploited
by means of shafts, costeens, pot-holes and open cuts.
... .In nearly all the shafts and in many of the open
cuts, pot-holes, etc., these lenses pinch out at depths
ranging from a few feet to 60ft. from the surface...
So far little endeavor has been made to ascertain
whether the quartz makes again at a lower depth.
In some cases sinking has been continued a few
feet beyond where the pinching'Occurs, but the coun-
try rock is hard and expensive to work. . . .With
further sinking there is a probability of the down-
ward continuation of the quartz being picked up,
especially the main lens.” Fortified with this ray
of hope, the co. hangs on.
Gwalia South held a semi-final meeting
at Kalgoorlie the other day, when the liquidator in-
timated that £18,859 had been returned, to share-
holders and £574 debited to expenses. A credit
balance of £1877 remains, and the chairman opined
that after allowing for all remaining liabilities
there would be a further distribution of at least 44d.
per share. A final meeting will be held shortly.

broken from the Nos. 2 and 4 levels, where, as this
paper has said already, the shoot is short and not
of the class that a co. with tens of thousands of
shares is likely to thrive on.

Apparently the Nor’-West Syndicate knows
a bit about the art of window-dressing. Telegrams
have passed between the manager and the Melbourne
directors concerning the great value of the co.’s
Pilbarra (W.A.) show, and the opinion that it will
yet prove to be one of the biggest mines in the
West has been put into print. Values are stated
to range from 12 to 23 p.c. copper and ldwt. to
21dwt. gold for a width of 20ft., while gold, silver
and copper galore are alleged to have been got from
ore taken from 320 acres of ground adjoining.
Shares have soared, the creation of several thousand
more has been decided on, and all is merry and
bright. But there is this to be said: the ore in the
far-distant locality where the co. has taken root, in-
variably occurs in short shoots, which, although at
times high in gold, copper and other values, are not
to be relied on. Some independent report on the
property is, therefore, wanted before the public can
with safety take a risk.

Sweet William Consolidated has sunk 100 ft.
on its Paynes’ Find (W.A.) show, and all hands and
the cook are now gouging out 1000 tons, estimated
to return from 20dwt. to 30dwt. Nothing is said
about the look of the reef in the bottom; but as
appearances are against the field being permanent at
depth, the co. probably considers that it would be
better to wait a while before expressing an opinion.
Meantime it has £9OO cash and the return from the
ore awaiting treatment.
Local enthusiasts have formed a no-lia-
bility co. to bore Frasers’ line of reef at Southern
Cross, and are getting substantial State assistance.
Some time ago 8., M. and Co. spent some thousands of
pounds in putting down a series of drill holes in
Frasers’ main workings, but as assays were poor they
rubbed the mine off their list of options. Seeing
that the show is on a field notorious for disappoint-
ments, the syndicate is commended for its pluck.

A peculiar case cropped up at the
Warden’s Court at Bonnievale (W.A.), the other
day, when a party of tributors applied for an order
to compel the Westralia and East Extension Co. to
remove its plant, consisting of 60 head of stamps,
Berdan plans, etc. Tributors claimed that the plant
was on the hanging-wall side, and liable to crush in
on the workings; also, that it was in the way of
their sinking a shaft to work the reef they were on.
The co. pleaded that it had no use for the plant
elsewhere at the present time, hut was iooking rou
Mines Inspector Crabb reported that there was no
danger of a crush, and the Warden (after he had
examined the mine in company with the inspector
and the petitioners) decided to endorse the inspectors
recommendation, which was that the co. g .
till February, 1914, to remove the plant. Judging
by the result of the last crushing, the tnbutors
won’t be there by that date. * #
Great Fitzroy (Q.) has made a 6d. call
to pay some of the expenses at its new undertaking
in Papua. Later on, if the Laloki mme turns out a
big thing, it will require a railway. The idea is
to build the line to Sapphire Creek, so that it may
serve some coffee and hemp plantations, but tne
copper country up the range from the Port is the
main attraction. Most of the shows in this belt
have an oxidised capping, with rich makes of ore
near the surface. So far there doesnt appear to be
any big copper mine thereabouts to blend with the
pyritic ore from the Laloki. Assays show that the
average metal values are low, with high sulphur
and iron contents. # #
The 520-share co. which has been work-

long time to forget what happened in connection with
that other N.Q. tin proposition,* the Vulcan.*
For the half-year Mills’ United, Charters
Towers, crushed 10,388 tons for £33,388, at the rate

in ore reserves, and the last crushing reported—7B2
tons for '£l993—shows that the crushing stuff is
not getting any richer. The co. is hi a strong
position financially ; it has cash balances of £35,259,
besides its interest in the Burdekin mill, valued at
£9186. Last year’s profit amounted to £9392.
With these resources it is possible that a dividend
may be paid before the end of the year, particularly
if there is a lucky development in No. 15.
The sale of 50.000 shares has given
Hampden Cloncurry more financial stability, and the
co. intends to push on with the development of its
mines, which are widely scattered. The Answer, re-
cently secured under option, is only 12 miles S.E. of
Mount Elliott. Before the big rise in copper Elliott
collared the Hampden Consols; now Hampden
snatches the Answer from under Elliott’s nose. Also,
Hampden directors are considering the purchase of
the MacGregor, seven miles further out. _ Neither of
these mines can be considered high-grade in the Clon-
curry sense, but with a railway they may make a
profit—some day. It will, it is said, take two years
to complete the Mount Cuthbert line (two sections) ;
and judging by that, the MacGregor line is a long
way 'off. * # .
Golden Age, Bendigo, isn’t full of the
precious metal yet, though it has three chances.
There seems to be a dread suspicion somewhere that
the local sharebroking element doesn’t want to hustle
things up at the Johnson’s shaft, though the press is
forcing matters in that direction. If this is the
case, Bendigo is only killing itself in the eyes of the
outsider, because, having three points of attack,
Golden Age has more attention and a better chance
than most of the shows of the field. It has the hope
of getting gold from the same make the Pike is
working on; then there is the Derby line and the
Golden Gully line. Still Golden Age seems to be
typical of modern Bendigo, which makes a grab for
the local threepenny-bit, and misses the outsider’s
nimble ninepence.

Central Cathcart (Vic.) has been the
stable tip of the alluvial crowd for the past half-
year. Last week the stock showed that it wasn tas
firm on its foundations as it might be. The hint of
this came from the Cathcart camp rather than from
the Central itself. Up the lead towards the Cathcart,
the co. has had a long drive, and it was a monte, if
good gold was got there, that shares would buck.
But the good gold has not materialised. So far
drives to the south-west across the lead have shown
wash of more or less low quality. Hence the gentle
scuttle which has scraped 10s. a share off the bar-
nacles that are clinging to the Central bottom. Of
course a poor strike up the lead towards the Cath-
cart boundary is a double-barrelled gun. If it hits
Centrals hard, Cathcarts must be hit harder. So, with
the banks sitting tight, and the need for a “bear
deal to help some of the “bulls” in the Langi Logan
stocks, the outlook for Central Cathcart isn t full
of inspiration.

The extraction of zinc from slag containing up to
20 p.c. zinc has become tempting with spelter at £26
per ton. But it isn’t as easy as losing money. As
with all zinc products, only a proportion of the
metal would be recoverable. Take a 20 p.c. zinc
slag to start with. Allow 75 p.c. recovery, and
calculate the value of the 15 units of spelter at £2O
per ton. The return spells £3 per ton of slag. It
would be quite easy to use up that much in labor,
fuel and other expenses.

Another Australian co. to probe the Malay
Peninsula for tin was registered in Sydney last week
—the Austral-Malay Tin Trust, Ltd: This is the out-
come of one of H. E. Pratten’s visits to those parts.
(Tin and jam seem to run together. It was the
Jones jam people who promoted, the Tongkah, which
was first named the IXL—the trade name of Jones s
jams. Pratten got interested in the business when
he was a partner in Taylor’s jam factory.) This
trust is formed to take over the options secured by
Pratten on behalf of the Malaysia syndicate—a small
concern in 15 shares of £lOO each. The syndicate
gets 15,000 shares paid to 12s. and the public pays
for 5000. Of five areas under offer the most im-
portant appears to be one of 400 acres or so in
Taiping. The greater part of this has been bored,
and the results show i.4lb. of tin per yard. The
ground is also said to be very suitable for bucket
dredging. Horton, a Sydney engineer of high stand-
ing, is on his way north now, in company with
Pratten; and if there isn’t any fly waiting for them
to fish out of the ointment, there is a lot of money
in the proposition.
* * * *
Tongkah Compound is the choicest titbit
that Australia has got hold of for many a day. Not
a good round of beef; for, on present showing, the
thing can’t last more than about three or four
years further, and it has been going only one. While
it lasts, however, and while the tin market booms,
there is about 16s. of profit in about every £1 worth of
tin recovered. It takes £6OOO to pay a 2s. div., and on
recent figures almost that much can be paid regularly.
The co. has a fine dredge, and it naturally wants to
get work for it to do when the present area is ex-
hausted. The co. has considered two or three offers,
and has done a lot of test boring. One option has
been turned down definitely; another, over a block
of 30 acres, will no doubt be exercised if a transfer
can be got. The money for this ground is already in
hand. It isn’t expected that there will be very fat
dividends in the new area; but it will at any rate
keep the dredge profitably employed for (say) three
years after the end comes at the wonderful Com-
pound patch.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the

ploying such appliances.' W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*
The conditions under which Maoriland farmers and
settlers struggled 30 years ago, or niorc were rTiQst
unsatisfactory. The trade was mostly in the hands of
large and small district storekeepers, many of whom
were grasping in their methods. Stores, etc.,
supplied at exceedingly high rates, and, on the other
hand, the produce—chiefly butter, cheese and gra
which they received from the farmers in exchange,
by way of payment on the barter principle, was
allowed for at minimum prices. From 4d. to oct. tor
butter was considered a good price, and sometimes
settlers had to be content with a great deal less. As
far as wheat and oats were concerned, the rates were
usually fixed by the merchants and storekeepers, and
there was practically no option on the growers part
but to accept the rate offered and look pleasant. As
a rule, accounts were squared off at harvest time,
and those who were not lucky enough to be able to
“clean the slate,” arranged for the balance by a
promissory note or some other form of collateral
security. Very questionable practices were also in-
dulged' in by many of the traders in those times.
Seeds were both expensive and unreliable; and
wherever there was a chance to get rich by giving
the farmer poor value, it seems to have been taken.
It was in 1881 that the prospectus of the co. was
issued —a modest prospectus, yet stating clearly and
forcibly the advantages to be gained from co-operative
effort. The interests of the primary producer were
to be served by:—
“Offering facilities to the farmer for the storage
and shipment of his produce to the Home or other
markets, and the appointment of agents in different
localities for the furtherance of this object. The
grading and classification of shipments of produce
in order to facilitate their sale at the most advan-
tageous prices. . .
“Effecting advances to members on any securities,
real and personal.
“Acting as general merchants in procuring for sale
to the shareholders, cornsacks, woolpacks, agricul-
tural implements, seeds and other articles.
“Undertaking the indenting of goods, and acting as
agents generally in all branches of business for the
shareholders.
“Conducting the sales of the society on a cash
“The benefits derivable from the Association will
be confined to shareholders.”
Private Enterprise mostly laughed at the idea:
“It is a very small affair. And it will soon burst,
in any case, as most of the farmers are under the
thumb of their local merchant.” It was true enough
in many cases that the farmer was under the mer-
chant’s thumb. But since that meant that the
farmer was being squeezed in a very painful manner,
the oromoters decided that the co. must live. They
decided not to risk the future by biting off more
than they could chew in the present. There was no
money for stocks at the start; so the directors
simply made arrangements to buy the best stuff at
the best price, and the members got the advantage of
this wholesale dealing. They liked it so much that
others came in. Stores came next. Then a London
buying and selling agent was appointed; then one
in Sydney; then agencies were established through-
out Maoriland, wherever there seemed good chances
of getting an outlet for the members’ produce.
The business, thus established, grew, until the co.
now ranks as the largest organisation of its kind in the
Southern Hemisphere. It began with a staff of two —a
secretary and a boy. To-day the permanent staff num-
bers 650. The freehold stores and offices in Cashel-
street (Christchurch) erected in 1882, occupied a
frontage on Cashel-street and Bedford-row of 66ft.
These premises have been steadily extended, until
now they occupy a frontage of 327 ft. on Cashel-street,
264 ft. on Madras-street, and 457 ft. on Bedford-row,
representing a total floor space of 135,744 superficial
feet. In these premises nearly 400 employees supply
shareholders and others with their requirements
from the retail departments, which stock almost
every conceivable article.
The activities of the co. have been extended fur-
ther and further as the years have sped by. There
is a Live Stock department, another devoted to Land
and Estates, another to motor-cars; the co. has its
bacon factory, its twine works, its manure works;
it lays itself out to supply farmers with every agri-
cultural implement. Or, rather, farmers are laying
themselves out to supply themselves with every im-
plement. For the co-operative character of the
undertaking is never lost sight of. The object is to
let the farmer keep in his own pocket the thumping
profit which otherwise would go to the middleman.
The Association also does a very considerable
financial business. Members’ surplus moneys are re-
ceived on deposit at satisfactory rates of interest,
and advances are made to assist farmers in acquiring
and stocking suitable properties. Some thousands of
current accounts, chiefly with farmers, are in opera-
tion upon banking principles at the Christchurch
house and the branches.
The fact that the turnover is now over £3,000,000
per annum is proof enough that this great co-opera-
tive association is widely appreciated. Yet the man-
agement believes that it is still not appreciated as
widely as it should be. The younger members do
not realise the bitterness of the struggles of the
pioneers of the movement; and thousands of others
do not realise what a potent factor the co. has been
in bringing about the present improved conditions by
which the whole producing industry benefits. But
the propaganda goes on. Year by year the advan-
tages of co-operation become obvious to a wider circle
of producers. And the wider the circle the greater
the advantages. For 1911 the co. paid in dividends
and bonuses to shareholders no less than £36,642.
Every penny earned belongs to the co-operating
shareholders, who are the farmers and graziers them-
selves. They get their work done well and cheaply,
in the first place; then, whatever profit remains,
comes back to them. Some, of course, is retained
to strengthen the business—-to ensure a still better
service in the future. There may be farmers and
graziers in Maoriland for whom the co. can do no-
thing. But no farmer or grazier can afford to lazily
assume that it is so. He ought at the least to com-
municate with the co., and put the matter beyond
doubt. And those who handle the products of the
land are also very probably missing something if
they have not made themselves familiar with what
this great and rapidly-growing institution can offer
them.
The financial position of the co. is now unassail-
able. The shareholders’ capital has been gradually
increased, until there is £308,382 in the business.
In addition, the reserve fund, made partly out of the
business and partly out of share premiums, exceeds
£90,000. As money has been needed to extend
operations, shareholders who have risen to affluence
through the operations of the co. have left sums on
fixed deposit. What they think of the co.’s stability
(and they have the best of inside information) may be
judged by the fact that of £206,391 of fixed de-
posits, no less than £118,453 is fixed for five years.
This money and some other smaller amounts find
profitable use in the business. As already men-
tioned, large stocks are carried ; but, in addition,
the financing of customers calls for a good deal of
capital. Thus, “Advances against stock, grain, etc.,
and produce shipped,” amounted on July 31 last
to £302,806. In the days before this co. brought
the advantages of co-operation to the farmer, the
latter, instead of getting an advance in this way,
frequently had to put himself at the mercy of some
trader, who bled him white. Now he can deal with
a company which has exactly the same interests as
he has himself —a company which to all intents and
purposes is himself.*

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, November 18, 1912.
My Dear Arini, —
The “Twilight House” League is prepar-
ing for a meeting at the Town Hall on Nov-
ember 28. Sydney’s little Mayoress has lent
her official nest for the occasion. A large,
typewritten sheet containing the skeleton
outline of the proposed home was pushed my
way the other morning. It glides over the
same old sea of trouble, the cheap-and-nasty
lodging-house. The Twilight League is up
against one of the most tearful problems of
the day. How can the elderly educated
woman, Who is past work (usually she’s a
broken-down teacher) find a decent shelter
at a moderate price? Hostels in Germany
and other low-wage countries —England, for
instance —have a fair chance of meeting the
trouble half-way. I think there used to be a
dreary place of sorts called “Home for De-
cayed Gentlewomen” somewhere on the edge
of one of London’s cheaper suburbs. A
Twilight Home sounds easier to live in, but
the price of a roof at the present moment
throws a steep wall across the track of the
league. Still, the object is well worth a good
hard struggle to climb the obstacle.
Cronulla made elaborate preparations to
sun itself in the vice-regal smile on Satur-
day afternoon. Every stitch of available
bunting had been dragged into the open air,
and most of it fluttered across the street
that faces the Pacific surf. On one side is
the local church, with a very early Norman
tower and a bell that has to work overtime
to get the tripper out of the breakers. On
the other is the original School of Arts, an
unlovely weatherboard barn. Beside it is a
half-grown brick building, the new School
of Arts whereon Governor Chelmsford laid
a slab of marble.
. When the Chelmsford car stirred the dust
in front of the dais, Downes, M.L.A., hurried
forward to greet it. Hard on his heels was
W. Hyndman, who is something in the
shire council. He gripped the usual volume
ot illuminated welcome, and handed it over
to his Ex. It was followed by a decorative
trowel from the School of Arts committee.
A contingent of fine strapping cadets lined
the road, and a few irresponsible little Boy
Shouts got in the way.
Lady Chelmsford, with Jove’s thunder on
her brow, frowned on the proceedings from
a patch of carpet on the dais. A few of the
local nobility and gentry, who had rushed
the glory of sitting near the vice-regal hat,
gradually edged to the lee side of her
Haughtiness, and looked as cheerful as if an
ants’ nest had found them. The small bonny
boy who was lifted over the collars of the
crowd to hand the inevitable bouquet (which
happened to be a basket) retired from the
Presence with a whipped expression. The
basket rolled over on the carpet, and might
have stayed there if a male infant Thesiger
hadn’t picked it up and stood it on its right
end at the feet of his haughty mamma.
Something had gone wrong with the vice-
regal works.
The dust of pre-election battle is begin-
ning to fall on suburban tea-cakes. Mrs.
Macarthur Onslow is already praying aloud
to the “Liberal” gods to swoop down and
save us. If the lady would urge her party
to put Brains into the House, it might be
awkward for many of the sitting “Liberal”
members, but it would be more to the point.
After they had been informed by Mrs. Ons-
low that she was the mother of three chil-
dren, and proud of it (presumably as a
reason tor voting for “sober Liberals,”
though the argument is somewhat obscure),
the Kose Bay ladies retreated in good order
on the Teapot.
Kirkby Lunn’s farewell to Sydney came
along when the tired tail of the concert sea-
son was almost at rest. But this singer and
her song would be welcome in any kind of
weather. Saturday night’s programme was
one of the best the velvet voice has exploited
in this town. German lilts, which she sang
with all her soul and breathing apparatus,
notably the compositions of Brahms and
Franz, were gems of vocal art. Her inter-
pretation of Liddle’s much-hacked “Abide
With Me” was even more: it stood a mile
° r , so above anything attempted here since
Ada Grossley sang it, with the organ, in the
same place. The Lunn voice needs no wind
instrument to support its firm, round notes,
other than the wind pipes under the satin
corsage. She looks the successful singer.
Sleek dark locks pushed into soft curls all
over hei cranium, and a wine-colored gown
that shimmered under a tunic of transparent
glitter, lifted her into the A 1 class on Satur-
day night.
Pianist William Murdoch, with the Lunn
concert co., smites the keyboard in the sweat
ot his brow. He is so tormented in agitato
moments by a loose lock of hair that waggles
on his perspiring forehead that he might
exorcise it, to his own comfort, with any
sharp weapon he can find on the premises.
His touch is firm and sure, with no inter-
ludes of bang. Murdoch is particularly
handy with his fingers, in the tricky music
of Liszt and Debussy. As an accompanist
however, he has a few points to pick up from
Laurance Phillip, who alwavs keeps the piano
subordinate to the voice. The very youthful-
looking Andre de Ribaupierre is a pleasing
fiddler, with the bloom of young enthusiasm
on his sunburnt cheek.
The Shakspeare memorial committee is
getting into its ter-centenary stride. Last
week’s meeting in the Town Hall produced
a flood of suggestions which were dammed
by the president of the hour, Chief Justice
Cullen. He treated the arguments like pri-
soners in the dock, and administered the last
rites of the law with impartial justice. The
proposed statue stood firm, though Judge
Backhouse tried to annihilate it —“and
smiled and smiled, and murdered while
he smiled.” Eadith Walker the Wealthy
wants a clean city before a Shakspeare
marble is given a chance to look down upon
the local Judge Backhouse de-
mands good drying weather, and lamented
the misfortune of a statue doomed to
stand in a deluge of rain without an um-
brella. The City Council -might take the
hint and supply Captain Cook’s effigy with
goloshes and a gamp. After picturing the
miseries of Captain Cook standing in Hyde
Park and looking for Australia in all
weathers, the cherubic Backhouse had to
scurry away; and the 17 or thereabouts who
were, left behind voted solid for a statue.
Talking of Cook’s statue, Sydney Daily
Telegraph published a picture of it the
other day under the heading, “The Man Who
Put Australia on the Map.” That is the
second time within 12 months the same
paper has made an estimable navigator
appear guilty of false pretences.
Governor Chelmsford will hold the fort at
another Shakspeare greeting this week. For
this outbreak there will be a specially-
selected assortment of brains from the
Teachers Training College. Fort-street
school and one or two other founts of learn-
ing have been asked to ransack their prem-
ises for youngsters who prefer Shakspeare
to green apples and marbles.

the following advt.:—
Mrs. T. H. Kelly requires the following servants
to enter for duties... .Will interview applicants, etc.
Then the list of minions required is men-

straight off.
rr' M l r A Justic . e Isaacs has been sitting in the
