A fashionable film of Limerick lace floated
over the satin shimmer of the bridal robe
by Miss Beth Weedon, of Hunter’s
Hill. The groom was Dr. W. Martin, who
belongs to a Goulburn family. Four brides-
maidens followed the turtle-doves to the
altar rails. Sweet peas, which are prevalent
at these affairs, being as much the fashion
of the moment as the formerly-snubbed red
geranium, were sprinkled through the bridal
bouquets. A strong contingent of the Pill
and Porous-Plaster brigade attended the
orange-blossoming.
The Women’s Handicrafts Society had a
good show in St.. James’s Hall last week.
Leather work, China painting, wood-carving
and needle-work are the main planks of
this youthful society’s bold bid for cash.
Mary Makinson carves the Queensland
black-bean timber (a wood much in request
for the Dryberra capital just now) till it
bursts into bud all over. Ella Richards,
strong in the knack of firing the crockery
she paints; Miss Bozon, another China-
painter; and Mrs. Munday and her pottery
were stalled side by side. Cleone Cracknell’s
enamel work —I fancy she caught her trick
ot color and design in Paris—leaps to the
front in every local show. A Madson blouse
is a dream of fine stitches of eye-aching
craftsmanship, embroidered with fairy-films
of_ lattice-insertion. Leather, handled with
skill by Mrs. Sanson, breaks out in bush-
scenery Miss Bamberger is another leather-
lady who gets good effects in repousse work.
Maisie Edwards, just emerging from the
flapper age, though the pigtail remains,
showed the best stencil design at the
Women’s Handicrafts. The original, some
member of the large bush-lily family, was
painted by Mrs. Ellis Rowan, and is shrined
at the Technical College. The girl, with
a good notion of form and color, adapted the
bloom, and dragged it into a suitable shape
tor a big table-cover. As stencilling of the
average quality is one of the minor crimes
of the century, the success of this effort is
worth three cheers. Emily Leist, a sister of
red Leist, finds an outlet for her artistic
bumps m beaten copper and other Arts, and
Crafts work. She sticks to Australian sub-
jects for her designs, and is getting consider-
ably in front of the ruck.
Strathfield recently blazed with revelry
in the form of the afternoon bun, accom-
panied by musical items. Mrs. A. G. Ral-
ston gave two tea-spreads, and trimmed
them both with harmony. In spite of search-
ing inquiries, suburban dames who were
bidden to the 50-guest frivol, can’t find out
whether they were amongst the social goats,
while the sheep browsed at the other bun-
and-song affair. Most likely the lamb
munched cream puffs side by side with the
kid, and Mamma Mutton bleated with
pleasing condescension in the ear of Mrs.
William Goat. The splitting of social
straws, which suburbia evolves out of its
own imagination, is fiercely reminiscent of
Dickens’s skit on Dockyard Society.
An exhausted concert season will fall
down dead after the Phil., the Oswald Ander-
son song recital, and Mirrie Solomon’s even-
ing with her own compositions. Next month,
I suppose, the average concert manager will
take up his residence in the surf, and only
the wail of the breaking-up school-fiddle
will be heard in the land.
H. N. Southwell has been presented with
a very fat and solid silver-topped inkpot
from the organisers of the recent School of
Industry entertainment. It was tied up
with white satin ribbon, and adds one more
to the large and growing family of presenta-
tion inkpots which pursue Southwell re-
lentlessly. . Mrs. Ashton, Mrs. Garland and
Miss Cecilia Stephen affixed their signa-
tures to the card introducing the gift, which
looks like the grandfather of all other ink-
pots.
Mrs. Arthur Styan, who used to be the
long and handsome Clarice Masters, of Mel-
bourne, sat for Florence Rodway’s study of
a profile. It was called “The Cameo,” and
exhibited at the recent women painters’
show. Mrs. R. M. Sly bought it.
The Faithfull family (N.S.W.) have
acquired another golden streak in their cash-
box. The late Edgar R. Deane, once upon
a time of Springfield, in the Goulburn dis-
trict, recently passed out in the South of
England. His will appoints Dr. Faithfull,
of Sydney, “sole executor and trustee” of his
estate. This cuts out at £26,000 or so, and
is to be divided in equal slices amongst
every member of the Faithfull tribe who
happens to be above the earth at the pre-
sent time. To them that hath shall be
given, and from them that can’t spare even
a bootlace, that little is likely to be bor-
rowed.
One toiler named Walker, who lives up
Cowra way, seems to be the kind of brother
every sister would like to order for herself.
He won the diamond tiara in the recent Eight-
Hour art-union racket, and the sister will
have the right to wear it, or sell it, or hide
it away in a stocking, or bury it in a bank
vault. If Mr. J. W. Walker doesn’t sud-
denly repent, he means to give the bauble,
said to be worth £750, to the sister who
keeps house for him and brother No. 2.
Brothers who don’t want to pass the dia-
monds to someone else’s sister ought one
day to be preserved in Mr. Holman’s Gov-
ernment House museum of curios.
Minister Carmichael wasn’t swift enough
to catch the main body of the ex-residence
of Governors in Macquarie-street. But he
is reported to have snatched the old stables
for the Conservatorium that he has been
dreaming about through the winter nights.
A Sydney-sider meandering through the
Cup riots happened to see the two musical
Dearns, the clever little Lottie and her slim,
willowy sister. They were both feeling
mighty homesick, and were hugging then-
grief on board an outward-bound German
boat, then alongside a Melbourne wharf.
There is some use, by the way, for the
stacks of gold keys a Minister for Anything
accumulates during his term of oflice. Lottie
Dearn’s farewell tears, after trickling down
her cheek, splashed on an uncommon brooch
made out of a gold Yale key. “It looked as
if it had once torn open some official latch
to the tune of popping corks,” says Miss
Port Jackson, who claims to be an observant
minx.
Rosina Buckmann was one of John McCor-
mack’s concert aviary that recently toured
Ireland and the English provinces.
The Sydney Philharmonic Society wrestles
with the music of “Samson and Delilah” on
November 28. Etta Gibson, who used to
sing in the Carl Rosa opera co., will make
her first appearance here, as Delilah.
Samson (Walter Whyte) wears his hair
short and takes no risks. Andrew Black
has the billet of High Priest. The same old
Town Hall is the place.
The Archibald Firths, who are honey-
mooning in these parts, are like to skip off
to England early in the coming year. He
is a Yorkshireman, and she is a daughter
of Dr. and Mrs. Miles, of Rydalmere, who
are very keen about Culture, with the
largest C the linotype can spare. It is still
mentioned by those who were at the wed-
ding that the lace on the bridal gown cost
at least £75 and a possible elevenpence-three-
farthings.
The South Sydney Hospital is promoting
a charity fete to be held in the Town Hall
from December 4 to 11. The hospital is a
young and poor relation of the richer shel-
ters for the sick in this town. It examines
the broken bones and other misfortunes of
Waterloo, Redfern and Botany to the best
of its limited financial ability. Secretary
Cobham Stewart, who used to sad the
stormy brine in the Powerful, is burning
the midnight gas in search of new notions
for the f6te.

~S y d ney bride and ’groom hurried to
Melbourne for the honeymoon about three
weeks before the Cup met the second Flood.
But the moon was mostly measles, which
the bride carelessly caught soon after the
trousseau had been unfurled in a fashion-
able hotel. So she had to be bundled off to
a hospital, while he drifted round for 10
days or so, reflecting that the moon was
merely green cheese. Then the measles
which were slight, fled, and the bride sped
back, to be told that she hadn’t done her
three weeks’ solitary confinement, which are
the lot of measles here below. A smart tav-
ern doesn’t take many risks, so the front
door closed with a polite bang. Back went
the bride to her hospital, and the ’groom
sauntered through a desert of Horse at
Flemington. Whether Mrs. Bride slipped
out to join him disguised under a large
macintosh, no Cupper has been mean enough
to tell. &
Pale, lemon-colored kid gloves are appar-
ently the thing for young bloods on and off
the stage. The beauteous Higginson, the
Kings Messenger in the present brisk sur-
vival of “The Quaker Girl,’’ is delicately
striped as to his waistcoat, and raw mustard
as to his gloves, when he bounds into the
first act. I notice a larger infusion of milk-
ana-water in his once ardent stage love
scenes. It is much too soon for Higginson
to lean back and contemplate dipping into
ink with reminiscences, such as “Girls Who
Admired Me,” or something of the kind.
Limerick lace drapes the bridal plumage of
every girl who has Right-minded parents this
season. One father of six daughters re-
cently asked the third bride from his hearth
whether one consignment wouldn’t do the
lot. He was severely reminded by mamma
that trousseaux were not his business till the
bills came in.
George Upward, the sculling enthusiast,
who is mostly in Sydney when he isn’t work-
ing for his soup as clerk of the Senate in
Melbourne, is more or less laid up with ill-
health just now. Which is extra-bad luck,
for he but recently returned to Australia
after accompanying the rowing team across
the globe The Upward who scene-paints
tor J.C.W., Ltd., is a son of this sporting
Upward, by the way.
A well-known Federal politician asked a
Sydney man to give him a tip for the recent
Steeplechase at Flemington. “Why not put
your cash on Statecraft?” said the hurried
acquaintance by way of cracking a small
joke. The advice was taken seriously, and
the cash went the way most cash goes at a
race meeting, for Statecraft had the bad
luck to fall at the first fence.
Jack London and his wife are camped for
a time on their own ranch, Glen Ellen,
County Sonoma (U.S.). Pianist Laurence
Godfrey Smith, of this town, was staying
with the roving pair when the last mail left
b risco. A postcard shows a large area of
foliage on the banks of Wild Water, Wake-
Robin Lodge _ (isn’t this descriptive place-
painting?), with an informative scribble in
the corner. “Here the ‘Sea Wolf’ was
written. ’ The author didn’t have to balance
the ink-pot on his knee, though, for a camp
table occupies the middle of the view.
Angus and Robertson publish “Stories of
Old Sydney,” by Charles Bertie, of the
Mitchell Library; illustrations by Syd-
ne.y Ure Smith. The windmills and
crinolines, which belonged to the same
period, make a . breezy duet through
many pages of briskly written letterpress,
ihe crinoline waggles in front of most of
the elderly buildings, and looks as if a full-
grown north-easter was whistling through
its frills, while the lady inside was admiring
the harbor from the edge of Dawes Point.
How these huge wire cages came out from
England, in the first blush of the 19th cen-
tury, and whether heavyweight wharf lump-
ers were employed to jump on, them before
they were squeezed into the ship’s hold,
Bertie does not say.

“B. & B.”—The Cycle of
Greatest Popularity.
This fact is significant: —More riders in Australia own
“B & B.” Cycles than any other single make. Just
think —we have sold over ten thousand machines, and
every purchaser says they are unsurpassed at any price
for easy-running, riding comfort, and lightness.
Every machine is built by expert mechanics, who receive the
highest wages. The materials are the best weldless steel-tubing
and B.S.A. and British parts. Finish is three coats of stove
enamel in any preferred color, and nickel parts heavily plated
on copper. Prices from £B/10/- to £lB. Call and see models
or write for free Illustrated Catalogue—an interesting book
giving illustrations and full descriptions of every model we
make.
Proof of “B. & B.” Superiority.
“In 1900 I purchased one of your bicycles
in W. Australia, where, in four years, I
covered some 12,528 miles. I took it with
me to S. Australia, where it did 36 trips
from Hahndorf to Adelaide, 18 miles each
way. I recommend these machines before
any I know.”
Mr. A. MOSER, Hahndorf.
“My ‘B. & B.’ machine has been of splen-
did service, running well all the time over
some very rough, rocky and broken country,
and in all weathers. It looks, and is, as
good as new.”
Mr. W. W. KING, Arcadia.
“The ‘B. & B.’ Cycle I purchased about 18
months ago has given every satisfaction. I
have travelled 4000 miles on it, and, as I
weigh 14st. and always carry a heavy load, I
consider this a splendid record. The bike is
as good as new.”
Mr. E. J. BOWMAN, Moree.
“I have travelled over all sorts of roads,
both rough and smooth, but the bike is as
good as when it first came out of the shop.
I recommend the ‘B. & B.’ Cycle to all people
who have to ride on country roads.”
Mr. HANSLOW, Cudgebegong.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, November 18, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Saturday’s march of 18,000 cadets was
the most striking spectacle Melbourne has
had since it rocked the cradle of the |Com-
monwealth. Many of the warriors were
almost eclipsed by their hats; but the
100,000 or so able-bodied lookers-on seemed
tremendously pleased with the military
baby-show. Though the jaunty-looking
youngsters stepped out briskly to the music,
the procession was about an hour and a
quarter passing the saluting point. Shortly
before the head of the long, brown column
reached the top of Collins-street, G.-G. Den-
man, Governor Fuller and a number of med-
alled and mounted uniforms took up their
positions in front of the crowded steps of
Federal Parliament House. Lady Denman,
Lady Fuller and several vice-regal guests sat
in open landaus well back from the heels of
the war horses, watching and listening to
the tramp-tramp of young feet, and shud-
dering, maybe, at the bayonets that shone
like slanting rain above the ranks. Prime
Minister Fisher and Defence Minister
Pearce were among the thoughtful Parlia-
mentarians who watched the pageant of
youth go by. All these youngsters are
proudly written of as “men” in the parade
reports, though a fearful proportion of them
seem to be little more than two short legs
and a large piece of felt. But for the chin-
strap, I am sure a lot of them would fall
out of their hats and be arrested by kindly
policemen as neglected children.
G.-G. Denman rounded up a picture party,
at the Majestic Theatre, to watch the
screened ghost of himself turn the first sod of
the Transcontinental. Little Thomas and
Judith eagerly watched their shadowy pa
get out of a train and stand in the wind
shaking hands with a number of dumb
strangers. Then they saw him lacerate the
face of the patient earth with a shovel, and
trundle a common barrow along a plank,
while the wind plucked hilariously at his
coat-tails. After he had trundled his noise-
less barrow back, they witnessed the presen-
tation of a small gold shovel, amid a great
amount of soundless enthusiasm.
As soon as the race-time revels were over,
the Denman lady, accompanied by the Bart-
telot pair and Major Quilter, set out on a
riding tour to Walhalla, the mining town
that runs for two miles alongside the great
hills. The going is rough thereabouts; but
her Ex. and the Barttelot dame lare good
enough riders to rather enjoy that sort of
thing. The party camped and took pot-luck
during their travels.
The Denmans’ dance raised quite a breeze
amongst Federal Laborites when Prime Min-
ister Fisher, in his private room, hinted at
adjournment. The party considered that its
labors in the House should not be inter-
rupted by a frivol given primarily for the
Most Expensive. But Fisher had promised
his wife that they would go; and at last
persuaded his people to shut up shop. All
his Ministers, except O’Malley and P.M.G.
Frazer (otherwise engaged in hospital), put
in an appearance with their womenfolk.
Mayoress Hennessy, gowned in shot silk
and brocaded extras, made an impressive
hostess at the Town Hall last week, when
she entertained the members of the Congress
of the Council of Women, now pervading
this city. About 500 members of the
C.N.C.W.V. (stop here for refreshments)
turned up to shake the hand of the new
civic hostess. Running fights are now pro-
ceeding amongst the various female leagues;
so the average visitor found it safer to in-
dulge in gritty, non-committal small-talk
than to wade in with political maxims and
moral reflections, the air being more or less
electric. The reception, which was purely
social, began at the head of the stairs in the
blue-and-gold suite of rooms —a restful place
full of cushioned corners and shadows. The
guests spread out like grazing sheep through
the corridors, and drifted by degrees into the
big council room, where food was available.
Gaud Mayor Hennessy, brisk and alert, per-
vaded the party, and exhibited an excellent
afternoon-tea manner. The Very Best
People panted up the marble stairs in a
continuous stream all the afternoon, and,
once fairly inside, attentive maids skimmed
towards them with plates of strawberries
for their refreshment. In the big hall,
organist Price played through a special pro-
gramme on the big instrument that has
never been degraded with a monkey on a
chain.
More than one Excellency turned out for
the Fred Paynes’ Cup-week dance at Mari-
timo. Carpets were laid on the wet lawns
among the dripping rhododendrons, and the
only complaint was lodged next day by the
stipendiary carpet-cleaner. Mrs. Fred Payne,
one of the smartest frockers in the social
firing line, received the guests at the stair-
head among heaps of azaleas. She was
gowned in many silken layers of petunia
color, and flowers smothered every part of
the house, even unto the pink parasols that
kept the champagne’s head cool in the supper
room.
The aged Baroness de Pury died the other
day at Yerinburg, in the hills near Lilydale,
where she had been rusticating and growing
roses since the death of her husband. She
left two sons, the elder of whom is encum-
bered with the title dropped by his late
father. The Baron dodges about this coun-
try disguised as plain “Mister.”
Burnham, the long, lean Denham aide who
wields a magic golf stick, is a good sport.
Said that the little G.-G. really dragged him
from India for his play. The talented young
man is fearfully wealthy, and is closely re-
lated to a brewery, about the best sort of
relation one could possibly have about the
premises. Help Nutting has a father who
bottles things to drink; and big Major Quil-
ter is the son of the late baronet Cuthbert
Quilter, whose name in England spelt “tele-
phone” in letters of gold.
Mrs. Norman Bayles gave a strawberry
tea at the Botanic Gardens one morning
in Cup week, and got damp, dejected
weather for her reward. A carefully-
selected bunch of Toorakians huddled under
the verandahs of the kiosk, sadly absorb-
ing refreshments, and envying the waterproof
swans gliding on the lake. Mrs. Kathleen
Watson-Dearden was luckier, and scored a
sunny day for her grill party, when she
lunched a select few and showed them the
azaleas. Mrs. Guilfoyle, wife of the late
curator, every year gave an azalea party
just when the blooms were at their best.
The flowers crush and crowd together down
a lawn border, and a fine cluster of cherry
color overhangs the lake, which faithfully
reflects it.
A most exclusive male club is said to have
blackballed a candidate who lately came to
be a husband and a squatter in our midst.
His rejection by these superior clubites is a
mystery to most people, for the bride-to-be
is one of the very smartest society misses,
and her chosen was regarded as being Good
Enough. When the result came out, the
new Australian had hardly breath to call a
cab to convey his luggage to a more hospit-
able abode ; for, as an hon. member, he had
been camping on the premises.
A Sydney girl who drives her own car,
and brought it to Melbourne for race time,
was held up by the Law, which ass insisted
that she should qualify for her license locally
by taking the usual policeman for the usual
drive round town. She agreed, and
the Department, to her surprise, sup-
plied a plainclothes man for the plea-
sant job. In Sydney, it appears, the
official observer takes his seat in uniform,
as though he had the candidate in custody.
It. attracts attention, of course, but spiteful
friends can’t go round saying that Blanche'
was seen driving round town with such a
badly-dressed man.
“Rubber” Edwards and his wife cut out
the rush of Cup-week in Melbourne to motor
in Tasmania. The big grey car has been
making even the much-petrolled Apple
Island stare. The Edwards family run
three private cars —one for papa, one for
mamma, and another for the baby Ed-
wardses and nursie. But the big grey is
their particular joy. It is Australian-made
in every detail, and its timbers were grown
on the soil. As big as a house, it takes two
flunkies to uphold its dignity and minister
suitably to the comfort of the fair-haired
Lady of the Car, who completes the vision.
The dancing man having practically van-
ished from the ballrooms of the earth, the
dancing boy is doing his feeble best to fill
the other’s pumps. One hospitable matron
lately raked the ’Varsity for a squad of stu-
dents for her dance. She was regretfully
told that the coming examinations stood in
the way ; Then she despairfully applied to
a dominie friend for a list of newly-emanci-
pated scholars, and managed in that way
to secure a number of well-grown infants.
It was a sight to see these dear little men
make their entrance on the eventful night
rigged out in the dinner jacket of youth.
What else can be done I don’t know —unless
the Boy Scouts are called out.
The Hordern bridal car has departed and
left a blank in this city. The travelling
showcase was enamelled dove grey, and com-
pletely glassed in, with grey linen blinds on
silver rollers, for the exclusion of daylight
and prying glances. The impressive vehicle
turned up with the dignity and regularity of
a mailboat, to take its place in the four
o’clock tea line in Collins-street.
Mrs. Pinschoffs reception, at Studley Tark,
was blessed with a day of blue- and gold. It
was mainly a gathering of musical souls.
Kirkby Lunn, dressed in chalky white, looked
in. Willie Murdoch, who accompanied her,
wore his most soulful expression. A bunch
of fresh young voices, cultivated in the Pins-
chof reserve, caused a tuneful diversion, and
daughter Carmen Harris’s best top-note rose
to the occasion. Mrs. Albert Miller and
Mrs. Hodges were on hand. Dame Best,
whose twins were on view outside, wore a
gratified expression and some nice clothes.
Mrs. Merrill was garbed quaintly in a black-
and-gold zebra outfit. Elizabeth and Louise
Pinschof made themselves useful by calling
out the names of the guests as the motors
flashed up to the gate.
Miss Pope, of Sydney, and her sister
petrolled off home last week, by way of the
dusty Sydney-road. The two girls motored
over to the Cup by themselves ; and though
a breakdown made them miss the big race,
they had the satisfaction of parading their
new clothes on Oaks and Steeplechase days,
When most of the Cup finery was looking
very tired.
The Harry Emmertons, who have tennis
champion Norman Brookes for son-in-law,
filled the family residence with flowers for
their annual Cup revel. Mamma, expen-
ively upholstered in putty-hued satin, was
assisted by daughter Mabel, wearing a bil-
lowy garment like an evening cloud. A
good musical programme was much dis-
turbed by Toorak chatterers. An imported
fiddler struggled hard against the shrill
crescendo of talk, and Maggie Sterling and
daughter Mary lifted up their voices in
song. The Grey-Egerton dame, in a florid
frock with pamers thereto, loudly bewailed
the behavior of her native climate, which
had sadly disappointed her British baronet,
and almost decided him to ask for a Royal
Commission. Norman Bayles, Dame Cres-
well, the Russell Clarkes, the Power family
and a Higgins host gave the music a sym-
pathetic hearing.
A girl who married into the squattocracy
from a smart chiffon store has been enjoying
herself thoroughly during the Cup revels.
The local peerage resents her presence with
sniffs and other evidences of high birth. She
gets back this way. While watching the
sweep of frocks over a ballroom floor, a
neighboring matron murmured enviously:
“What a lovely gown Mrs. Yeridear has
on !” The bride smiled a Mona Lisa smile.
“Yes,” she says. “But whenever we sent her
a hat, the special messenger boy was told to
hold the box until he got the money. Poor
child ! he generally had to put resin on his
hands in order to stick to it!”
Mrs. Langlands Jack, who has the habit
of annually transplanting a small member of
the family to learn English, hurried back to
Melbourne in time to air some fine clothes at
the Cup. Though the home is now depleted
of small fry through the transplanting
habit above mentioned, mother’s occupation
as a tripper is not finished, a yearly voyage
still being necessary in order that the
youngsters may not lack supervision and
companionship during the holidays. Isn’t
it strange that, though we import headmast-
ers from England, there is no tuition worth
tuppence in Australia?
Cricketer Ilazlitt is returning this week to
Australia with his wife and baby daughter,
Peggy. Grandmamma Summerhayes is wait-
ing to welcome them and borrow Peggy from
her young mother, who is a daughter of E.
H. Major, of the J. C. Williamson staff.
By the way, Gertrude Summerhayes, back
in Melbourne after an exhaustive tour of
Queensland and N.S.W., is booked for an-
other northern trip next winter, with harpist
Walter Barker. Barker’s hereditary touch
on the strings sounds ever like a gentle
satire on his family name. His father was
minstrel at the Court of “the Old Queen.”
Patrons of the Savoy trembled in their
private supper rooms, one night last week,
and let the devilled oysters get cold. A fire
had got loose a few doors away, and threat-
ened to wipe up the vicinity. Flames licked
greedily out across the street, and it only
wanted a puff of wind from the wrong quar-
ter to make the blaze of a century. Richard-
son’s Cafe, alongside, began to disgorge
excited humans in evening undress, and the
alarm at last reached the Savoy. Out came
the supperers pell mell. In the confusion, a
prominent citizen was discovered gallantly
escorting the wife of somebody else. A num-
ber of less important citizens were not so
much discovered.
The battling Swastika Club is defunct;
but some of the old members are working
over the remains and trying to make a Vic-
toria Club for Women rise from its ashes.
Mrs. Staughton will be at the head of the
new organisation, and Mrs. Davidson and
Mrs. Barlow will be first and second mates.
The opening of the new Club will happen
on Thursday, in the old Swastika rooms.
The indefatigable Mrs. Rumor is tacking
orange blossoms over the names of a
wealthy political widower and a mischievous
young Australienne who has just returned
from a trip to England.
Barrister Ah Ket and Miss Bullock, of
Kew, were married on Saturday last, by
Methodist minister Flockhart, in the church
where the Australian girl had sat piously
every Sunday from her early pigtail days.
Mr. Chia and his bride-elect, Ume Russell,
were among the guests, and there was a
large Mongolian round-up at the station to
wave the departing pair good-bye. Ah Ket
is taking his wife to China for a trip. He
has been selected by the Chinese merchants
here to represent Victoria at a conference
of oversea delegates which is to meet the
National Parliament of the new Republic.
After the business is over, the little Kew
girl will tour as much of China as it may
be thought desirable to show her ; and will
then return to Melbourne. Mrs. Bullock
wept over her departing daughter from the
depths of a becoming bonnet.
Mr. and Mrs. Kirkby (he supplies Aus-
tralian-bred horses for military needs in
India) got back from their travels in time
to motor out to Flemington. They are stay-
ing at the George, waiting for tenants to quit
their fine Toorak house. Joyful friends gave
them a welcome-home dinner after the
Steeplechase.
Stanley Halkyard, one of the clever South
Yarra brothers who have captured over
£l3OO in scholarships, is off to fill the aching
voids of Hobart. Before leaving for his new
dentist’s shop, he wedded Cora Doris, of
the Wrigley clan, and took her to Sydney
for a honeymoon. Uncle Captain Black
came over from the Flyspeck to play the part
of father at the St. Paul’s altar rails, the
rightful person being absent in Queensland
on a health trip. Mrs. Wrigley breakfasted
the party at Paris Cafe.
Guests have discovered the perpetual-
motion secret of the Fairbairns’ race-week
dance. The expensive and beautiful hop,
which went gaily until the milk-o’s yell was
heard without, was kept going by fresh re-
lays of musicians. When one band was done
up, another lot slipped into place behind the
potted palms, and scraped and twanged vig-
orously while the off shift crept away to
pour beer down the cornet and feed the
violin on bread and cheese.
Mrs. Kirkby Lunn, saying good-bye at a
matinee at the Town Hall, wore a frock of
blue brocade, with golden iris embroidered
over its expansive front breadth, and blue
veiling dimming the general effect. She
curtseyed reverently to a vice-regal lady
hidden under an ostrich wing or two, and
just bowed to that part of the audience
which spread its race-time chiffons in the re-
served seats. Her dem'ure salutations never
seem to extend further than the front
benches. There is none of the expansive
Clara Butt smile and the scattered kisses
which made the large contralto so popular
with the crowd.
Percy Hunter, of N.S.W. Immigration
Bureau and the earth generally, is one of
those who suggest the establishment of a
tourist bureau in London for the Australian
education of the moneyed classes. These
people are possessed of libraries of ignor-
ance concerning this wide country, and the
consequence is that they have to discover it
for themselves, or curse it at long-range on
the strength of the slanders retailed by trav-
elling liars and fools. And the number of
liars and fools that are loose and ready to
talk or write is tremendous.
Mrs. Rumor asserts that one of the Very
Best spinsters contemplates following up
amateur stage successes by facing the foot-
lights in England. Wearied of the social
treadmill as worked in Melbourne, she will
hide her attractions behind the wigs and
bonnets of elderly characters whenever she
can get the chance. She has no ambition
to sweep the boards as Juliet or Rosalind.
She is simply mad to act for acting’s sake.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Federal Exhibition of the Society of Arts was
opened on Thursday evening with a conversazione—a
favorite weapon or tool for such enterprises. Mr.
John White, president of the society, conversed with
each guest separately, and then there was music, and
conversation became general. The only silent
moments of a thoroughly pleasant and chatty even-
ing were during supper.
A.D.C. Walker arrived on Thursday. He is a
Grenadier Guardsman —which means, my dear,
amongst other things, that he has the right to wear
an immense busby on his head and a red and blue
ribbon on his straw hat. And his front name is
Cecil. What could one wish for more?
Will Ashton is off to England shortly. The con-
trollers of the Art Gallery have asked him to mark
and inwardly digest the appearance of similar struc-
tures in foreign parts.
At a meeting of the Public Library Board, on
Friday, W. J. Sowden, of the Register, was re-
elected president. This has happened to him four
times running, and < the Prophets only know when it
is going to stop.
Adelaide is threatened with a whisky-and-soda
strike. The hotels talk of charging 9d. for the
hitherto-sixpenny drink, whereupon the man who
wants whisky will probably fill up with water in-
stead of carbonic acid gas, and the factories—which
have increased and multiplied of late—will be left to
cater for the soft-drinks trade.
Headmaster Chippie, of Prince Alfred College, is
leaving at the ehd of 1914. By that time he will
have been in charge for 39 years, have added £40,000
worth of buildings to the establishment, and
whacked about a million small boys. A fine record.
The Tourist Bureau has built an Alpine tea-chalet
at Waterfall Gully. The Governor opened it prac-
tically single-handed on Saturday. So much for
the sneering traitors who argue that the imported
State Governor arrangement is of no value to the
people. The only help he got was from Commissioner
of Crown Lands Young, ex-Commissioner Crawford
Vaughan and a few others.
On the same day the Marryatville bowling club
was similarly treated. Mayor and Mayoress Prosser
acted as host and hostess—it is a confirmed habit
of theirs. But they say definitely that this is their
last year of civic greatness.
Mr. and Mrs. Ellis are going to Melbourne, he
having been appointed one of the stipendiary stewards
of the racing clubs in Victoria. The Stock Exchange
farewelled him affectionately on Tuesday morning.
He was one of the original 50 members, and has been
chairman of its club since that infant was born.
The Dick Hawkers are back from the Cup. Miss
Hilda Fisher is staying on in Melbourne for the
tennis tournaments, and her brother, Guy Fisher,
has gone over for the same strenuous festivities.
Mr. Frank Toms, of Kapunda, has been seriously
ill of pneumonia, but is reported better.
The Adelaide Polo Club is training hard, and, in
the intervals, seething with excitement. The G-G
and his horsey collection of A.D.C.’s will be here be-
fore Christmas, and will play against an Adelaide
polo club team and one representing the associated
clubs. The matches will be on December 19 and 21.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

POLITICAL
POINTS
From the indignant comments of the Tory
press on the amended Referendum questions,
one may assume that the Fusion party has
abandoned all hope of getting the Labor
party out. The object of the Referendum,
as the Argus, for example, professes to see
it, is “to arm the Caucus” with further
powers to ruin the country. The proposed
alterations in the Constitution will enable
the Federal Government to do this, that and
the other misdeed, and the Government will
always be the Caucus. The possibility of
the Deacook party obtaining permission to
do good is not even suggested. Which seems
to be a straight Tory tip for the next Fed-
eral elections, not to mention all the elec-
tions thereafter.
UNNATURAL HISTORY.
GRAFTON.—The pumpkin beetle is creating great
havoc. —News item.
The pumpkin beetle by no means confines it-
self to pumpkins. It has been known to swallow
match Jactories and other flourishing industries.
It is attracted by a spineless tariff. —Agricul-
TRUAL EDITOR.
“Nulla” : The N.S.W. Government’s plat-
form has been denuded of the plank dealing
with the abolition of imported State Gov-
ernors. It has also lost the timber dealing
with the limitation of borrowing; likewise
the fragment which stands for the abolition
of the Upper House. In fact, is the dashed
thing a platform or a step-ladder nowadays?
“Homo” : Have no desire to throw a brick
at the modus opcrandi of the Maternity
grant, but there seems to be something lack-
ing. The claimant has to make a solemn
declaration that she is not an Asiatic, or an
Aboriginality, or other such contraband
goods; but the absence of any query as to
the brand of male partner involved natur-
ally stirs me up to reflect on a possible
piebald aspect of the transaction. White
motherhood does not necessarily prevent a
White Australia being streaked and mottled
with nigger and Chow, in a manner opposed
to the settled policy of the Commonwealth.
“Dismal Dick” Butler, S. Australian Min-
ister of Woi’ks, now calls himself “Cheerful
Richard,” in explanation of his lavish pro-
posals of water schemes that will not return
i per cent., and railways which the authori-
ties prophesy will result in heavy annual
losses. By way of change, 85-year-old Bur-
goyne last week referred to him as “Reckless
Richard.” But, whatever they call him, he
is as “touchy” as .ever; and should he ever
become Premier there would be a civil war
in a week.
The Balkan eruption and the consequent
rejoicings, arguments and threats over the
captured seaports start the casual philo-
sopher wondering whether N. S. Wales
would have valued Twofold Bay if that pos-
session had been gained at the end of the
bayonet, or whether Victoria would have put
its helmet on a rifle barrel and cheered if
Portland had only been won at the cost of
thousands of lives and millions of money.
The delight of these bottled-up Europeans at
gaining an outlet for trade is rather amaz-
ing to the Small Australian, who has been
hard put to it of recent years to keep his
closed ports corked up with newspapers.
Some people would gladly make the Allies
a present of the unused harbors, if those
anxieties could be separated from Australia
and towed over the horizon.
Some notion of the cost of Parliamentary
trimmings in the land of the Ancient Bri-
ton can be obtained from Speaker Willis’s
statement that 49 rooms are set apart for
the Speaker of the Commons and 24 for
the Sergeant-at-Arms. Yet, in that wonder-
ful home of Party Government and general
muddle, there is insufficient accommodation
for the members within the chamber itself.
Also, in parts of London there are half a
dozen families —families which in their
miserable way help to support Mr. Speaker
and his flummery—who make shift with a
single room. There is plenty of tragedy
mixed with Bull’s comedy of government.
Premier McGowen (N.S.W.) says that
when the name of Sir Gerald Strickland was
submitted, the Ministry approved at once.
McGowen seems to have a shocking memory.
He and his Ministry are pledged to carry
out the Labor platform and crystallise it
into legal enactment. One of the planks
of the platform is the abolition of State
Governors. The retirement of Chelmsford
gave the Labor Ministry an excellent oppor-
tunity to stand solidly on this plank and in-
timate to the Imperial authority that it
need not go to the trouble of hunting up a
successor to Chelmsford, as the State Chief
Justice would apply the rubber stamp for a
few extra bob per week. But the amazing
Ministry clean forgot the Labor policy it
was sent into power to give effect to, in its
appalling eagerness to have an imported
Governor of its very own on the premises.
The absurdity of Party Government was
recently instanced when J. Vigor Brown
rose in Maoriland Parliament and asked
whether a motion of his which had been for
some time on the order
paper would be allowed to
come on. Massey, the Pre-
mier, replied that he could
not allow the matter to be
discussed unless it had the
endorsement of the leader of
the Opposition. Vigor
Brown replied that his
trouble was that he didn’t
know who was the leader,
and couldn’t find anything
that looked like one. The
only way out of the difficulty
would appear to be for each
man opposed to the Massey
crowd to constitute himself
a party, and solemnly inform
the Premier that he had
been elected leader of it. By way of
precedent, Massey may recall that he him-
self once led a party which consisted mostly
of Massey and some empty benches.
Melbourne Age ties a bow on the tail of
the Sosh tiger :
For good or ill, we have irretrievably committed
ourselves to the principle of exclusive State owner-
ship and control of railway undertakings. There are
critics to be found who hold that we have acted
foolishly; but the vast bulk of the population is
firmly convinced that we never acted more wisely
that when we chose our course and burnt our boats
against retreat. And we have the best of reasons,
both social and economic, for so believing.
In fact, the Age might have added, it has
the best of reasons for admitting that the
tiger’s skin, which it formerly helped to
fasten on to Socialism, was a menn decep-
tion to hide a domesticated and highly-
esteemed animal, so tame that it will browse
out of the hand.
The Bendigo (Vic.) Methodist Council is
doing a characteristic rail-straddle. In
Synod the other day it passed a motion
“recognising that it is the duty of the Gov-
ernment to defend the Commonwealth, but
profoundly regretting that boys are com-
pelled to attend drill.” The Bulletin
would go so far as to second the motion if
the Synod would propose to profoundly regret
that only boys are compelled to attend drill.
In the absence of anything so definite, it
looks as though the Methodist Synod expects
Fisher to defend Australia by throwing two
Hallelujahs and one Amen at the advancing
foe.
Party Government reaches about the high-
watermark of insanity. The other day in
England Asquith’s crowd was defeated on
a snap vote on a clause of the Home Rule
Bill. The Conservative Opposition instantly
broke into yells of “Resign ! Resign!” and
the House was actually adjourned in order
that the Ministry might consider the grave
constitutional crisis. It didn’t resign; but
the mere fact that it thought of letting all
the big legislation it has in hand go over-
board because a few of its supporters were
for a moment asleep, or tired, or drunk, or
something is an indication of the mad way
in which the party game is played.
Premier Watt finds his Country Roads
Bill hard to travel. It was a well-meaning
measure calculated to improve the existing
highways of Victoria and create new ones
where they would do most good. But the
Country Party couldn’t sit still and see
£2,000,000 of money spent in road-making
under a non-political Board. McLeod, Pea-
cock and other representatives of Victoria’s
selfish, short-sighted shireocracy therefore
joined with the Labor party and carried
a proposal to change the Board of independ-
ents into a trio of public servants, who will
have the “confidence” of the shire councils.
The Minister of Lands put it that “an in-
dependent Board was desirable; otherwise
there would be appeals to the Minister five
times out of six, and the Minister would be
forced to decide without personal knowledge
of the facts.” He might as well have ad-
mitted candidly that he would be button-
holed all the time by country members. The
log-rollers carried the amendment by 31 to
22, and the Country Roads Bill is in a fair
way to be a failure if Watt doesn’t
abandon it altogether.
UNSEEMLY ANTICS.
This unfortunate state of affairs has caused great
concern to Liberals everywhere. . . .There is a small
section who have even been encouraging the Labor
party. -ARGUIS.
“’ Ere , you in front! Just stop and behave
like th' right-thinkin' Tory party I meant
yer I' be !”
George Greid favors “Pacifica” as the
name for Australia’s Federal capital.
“Pacifica” means peaceful. Our Pie Com-
missioner no doubt thought it meant piece-
ful.
Fitchett, Snowball, Worrall and some
other persons have been doing a huge anti-
Ilome Rule wowse in Melbourne, and cab-
ling “home” to Edward Carson about it.
Orangeism is a strange business. In Ire-
land it is mostly drum-and-fife bands with
unlimited drunk; in Australia it links up
loyalty (to anything but the Common-
wealth) with all the choice elements of ad-
vanced Wowserism. The Orangeman reel-
ing home full of bad whisky and enthusiasm
in County Down or Antrim would hardly
recognise Brother Worrall as the right brand
of Paptist-baiter; but as long as the sec-
tarian business can be kept going in a
country like this, which has no use for it,
anti-Home Rule is a good enough cry when
anti-Mixed Bathing for the moment palls.
The satisfaction of Federal Ministers
over the present condition of Australian
defence is one disquieting fact in connection
with the business. Last week 17,000
paraded in Melbourne, and Prime Minister
Fisher, looking at the raw files as they
sauntered past, declared them equal tf- the
troops of any country. This, on the face of
it, is plain blither; and the man who can
persuade himself that our kid army Is all
that it should be is capable of holding the
view (1) that our mixed railway ""gauges
are all right; (2) that the quickest method
of getting to the Northern Territory is by
walking; (3) that speed is not an essential
of modern warfare, and (4) thayt*nothing
in particular is likely to happen ‘when an
enemy lands on Australian shores." If An-
drew Fisher doesn’t believe these*things he
acts as if he did believe them, and they are
quite in line with the thing-he-said about
that portion of the defence force which he
saw. ...
Part of John Bull’s army went out for an
airing the other day. To prevent it getting
lost, or bushed, or fogged, or bogged with
plunder, or otherwise incommoded, the War
Office thoughtfully provided the officers with
maps. According to these maps the country
was open fields, giving plenty of room for
Balaclava charges. In reality it was occu-
pied by a new suburban town of about 7000
people. All that was the matter was that
the maps were a few years out of date.
THE MISSING INGREDIENT.
The Minister for Home Affairs has promised to
submit to the House a list of suggested names for
the Federal capital.
KINK: “ This is the site, brother. The next
thing is the christening .”
AUSTRALIA: “ Christening? Where you going
to get the water from? ”
The Fatman’s press has
often been horrified at “the
inhumanity of the Labor
unions in denying the right
of the non-unionist to live.”
They haven’t, of course,
done anything of the sort;
they have merely denied
themselves the doubtful
pleasure of working with
the non-unionist. Now it
transpires that the exceed-
ingly Fat gas co. of Mel-
bourne does not take any
coal from the Browns, of
Newcastle, because the ship-
ping-coal combine won’t let
it; also, the Vic. Railway
Commissioners take no coal
from the same Browns be-
cause they (the Commis-
sioners) were told that if
they took any from the
Browns the combine
wouldn’t be suppliers at any price. When
a combine does this sort of thing it is “busi-
ness” ; when a labor union does the same
thing it is “inhumanity.”
Westralia, by knocking £lOOO off the vote
to be spent on Government House, has
struck the first serious blow against the ex-
pensive uselessness of State Governors.
The Labor party out of power has no use
for these ornamental persons, but the men
who take office in Labor Ministries quickly
weaken on the proposal to abolish them. In
Westralia the Scaddan Government has,
however, been defeated by its own sup-
porters at a time when there were not
enough Tories on the premises to help the
Labor Ministers through.
“George Bltiegum”:—
For N.S.W. to spend money sending a Minister to
London to help T. A. Coghlan float a loan is a
wasteful and ridiculous excess. The business can be
done by cable more easily and with less friction.
The fact is that one stockbroking firm of under-
writers, R. Nivison and Co., acts for India, Canada and
, the six Australian States; and the floating or con-
version of any of these loans remains with “Bobby”
Nivison. If terms are right and the market favor-
able, Nivison can - underwrite anything he undertakes.
If they are not, well, he won’t, and a whole team of
Cabinet Ministers could not persuade him to. _ All
such loans are gradually peddled by the underwriters,
for the public very rarely applies for the lot.
Considering that nearly all the row in the
N.S.W. Assembly has been staged by men
like Wood, Perry, Fitzpatrick and Levy, and
that their sole grievance has been a Speaker
who insists on order, the attempt to fasten
on to the Labor Government the “degrada-
tion of Parliament” is scarcely playing even
the' miserable Party game according to the
rules. When Mrs. Onslow addresses .Liberal
women, and tells them of the disgrace of
Parliament, she should in decency add that
her husband’s political friends are the worst
offenders, and that their own husbands’
language isn’t always fit for —well, say, Mrs.
Onslow’s hearing.
STONEWALL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The stonewall which started in the House of Re-
presentatives, over the Estimates, on Wednesday
night, came to an end at four o’clock on Friday
afternoon.— Political news item.
The last suburban trains have gone. There are no
trams in any street."
Past dark and shuttered shops slow cabs roll wearily.
Cab horses' feet
Ring treble knells upon the stones, and heavy wheels
a chorus growl.
The city, like a brooding monk, dreams sombrely
in Night’s dark cowl.
The last, lone drunk, his crayfish gone, resigned and
sodden waits arrest;
Road mud upon his coat-tails dries, and crumbs and
beer-stains deck his vest.
The fretting world is stopped awhile. There comes
no clank of market carts
That roll before the haggard dawn with earth-cold
greens to early marts.
All, all is done. The planet rolls as silently as any
star;
But lighted lamps before the House show where
the Chronic Talkers are.
But what they do is neither work nor play. "Sou
could not class as sin
The endless fuss and noise they make their mad-
men’s victories to win.
’Tis something quite apart from Life, and yet is not
to Death allied,
That keeps them gabbling through the night while
flows and ebbs the careless tide.
The clock to them no warning gives that Earth will
yet of them be rid ;
They hear not through its heartless tick the clods
upon their coffin lid;
But, careless of the race of Time, they talk as if the
stars stood still
To hear their babble, and the sun was halted like a
squad at drill.
The unsolved problems Yesterday laid down at their
accursed door
Lie rotting—and To-morrow speeds towards them
with a hundred more!
The curse of Party Government holds them eternally
in thrall.
A Nation’s way is stopped; Fate only steps across
their mean stonewall.
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

Aged Lady derived great Benefit from
Comstock’s Nerve and Bone Liniment
for Rheumatism.
The powerful, penetrating qualities of
Comstock’s Nerve and Bone Liniment act-
ing through the skin on the delicate nerves
and muscles was never more forcibly illus-
trated than in a letter from a lady describ-
ing the great benefit her aged mother had
received from its use, for Rheumatic Pains.
Miss A. Mantle, of Eastern Terrace, Queens-
town, Otago, N.Z., writes: —-
“I have pleasure in stating that my
mother (Mrs. Mantle) has received great
benefit from your very valuable Liniment for
Rheumatism, after trying many others, and
receiving more pain than benefit in the last
40 years. It is the only one that has really
done good, and she is now telling it to
everyone she meets. My mother has suffered
much for many years, being now 74 years of
age, and is in better health than I have seen
her for years, for which I have to give you
many thanks, and hope to see Comstock’s
Nerve and Bone Liniment continue in its
good work.”
To be obtained from all Chemists and
Stores, or will be sent post paid on receipt
of price, 2s. 6d. per bottle, by The W. H.
Comstock Co., Ltd., 23 Lang Street, Sydney.*
Government Notices.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, Ist November, 1912.
NAVAL FORCES OF THE COM-
MONWEALTH.
Appointment of Assistant Overseer
(temporary), to supervise Dredger
Construction.
APPLICATIONS are invited from per-
sons qualified and desirous of ap-
pointment as Assistant Overseer (tempor-
ary) to supervise dredger construction for a
period of not less than twelve months.
Salary will be based upon applicants’ ex-
perience and credentials, but will not exceed
£3OO per annum.
Applicants must have had practical ex-
perience in the construction of dredgers.
Full particulars regarding the above ap-
pointment may be obtained on application
to the Navy Office, Melbourne, and at the
Naval Staff Offices, Sydney, Brisbane,
Largs Bay (S.A.), Fremantle and Hobart.
Applications must reach the Naval Secre-
tary, Navy Office, Melbourne, not later than
25th November, 1912.
S. A. PETHEBRIDGE,
Secretary, Department of Defence.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, 12th November, 1912.
Appointment of Assistant Examiners of
Stores and Equipment, Grade 11, at
Headquarters, Melbourne.
APPLICATIONS are invited from per-
sons qualified for and desirous of ap-
pointment as Assistant Examiners, Grade
11, for soft goods, military uniforms, includ-
ing hats, caps, badges, etc. (four vacancies).
Salary, £156 per annum.
Application forms and full particulars
may be obtained on application to the Secre-
tary, Department of Defence, Melbourne, or
, to the Commandant, Military District Head-
quarters, Sydney, Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth
and Hobart.
Applications must reach the Secretary,
Department of Defence, Melbourne, not later
than 14th December, 1912.
S. A. PETHEBRIDGE,
Secretary.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
“Looking Backward” : In 1870, Craig, the
owner Of Nimblefoot, a rank outsider for the Mel-
bourne Cup, dreamt that he saw the horse win, the
jockey having a black crepe band on his arm. He
instructed an agent to back Nimblefoot to win
£lO,OOO. Nimblefoot won. Craig died on the same
day, knowing nothing of his victory. Among the
minor bets made bv Craig was one with Slack, a well-
known bookie, who laid him £lOOO to eight nobblers of
brandy against a double with Crovdon, who was
favorite for an Oaks Day race. Croydon won, and
Slack paid the debt to Craig’s widow. Nimblefoot’s
victory in the Cup was the closest on record. He beat
Lapdog by a nose. There are turfites living to-day
to whom Craig related his dream, though he kept his
big bet dark. ♦ * *
Almissa, who won the Moonee Valley and
Williamstown Cups, is a good advt. for the Australian
climate. In England he was a broken-winded horse
—a “roarer,” and a bad one at that. The voyage
out improved him, and a year or two completed the
cure. Almissa these times appears to be longer
winded than the average gee. He breathed himself
into fifth place for the Melbourne Cup.
* * * *
John “Baron,” ruminating over Duke
Foote’s victory in last year’s Williamstown Cup
race, remarked to a pressman that he could never
understand how his horse had been beaten for the
Melbourne Cup six days previously. Well, the handi-
cappers, who saw the horse win the Williamstown
Cup, after failing badly in the two miles race,
classed him as a non-stayer, seemingly. Anyway,
last year he was given 7st. 71b. for the Melbourne
Cup, and they did not add to his weight this year,
notwithstanding his win at Williamstown (11 fur-
longs). Mr. “Baron” Brown talks of Melbourne
Cups as though they are bound to be within the
compass of any horse that runs brilliantly over a
mile and a half. As yet, there has been no proof
that Duke Foote can stay the extra half-mile. He
looked likely to do it on his Metropolitan perform-
ance ; but lie isn’t the first Metropolitan winner that
has been afterwards beaten by the runner-up in a
longer race.
* * * *
Whereas Duke Foote, all going well with
him, will be raced again in the autumn, the chances
are that Uncle Sam will be out of training for nine
months or so. There’s a lot of room for improve-
ment in the big son of United States, if his nervous
disposition will give him a chance to settle down
into a sedate five-year-old. For all his resolute
manner of finishing a race, Uncle Sam is an easily
disconcerted animal. A bump puts him into diffi-
culties and throws him into a sweat. A very heavy
bump knocks him over, almost, for he has no
notion of righting himself. In the Caulfield Cup,
where he was ridden wide, Uncle escaped with one
moderate bump. In the Melbourne Cup he was
bumped a lot in the first half of the race, and in
the C. B. Fisher Plate he got worse still, and was
nearly down.
* » * *
“Pick”: Newspaper reports of the flutter
for the Melbourne Cup say that Piastre was hurried
along in front “without a whip,” and that among the
less speedy gee-gees “whips flew like flails.” In a
subsequent interview a favorite’s rider is alleged to
have owned up that he “rode with the whip, but
nothing would stir him.” In other words, no amount
of savagery vented upon the exhausted brute could
make him win against fleeter opponents. The read-
ing made me sad. Also, it made me tired when I
remembered that at Sydney the previous week a carter
was fined £3 and costs for working a horse with a
sore shoulder. If racing clubs were civilised, or
even if they had half the love for the horse that
they pretend to have, they would everlastingly bar
the whip from the course.
* * * *
Eudorus was the urduckiest of the im-
ported gees (bar Eye-glass, now thoroughly Aus-
tralianised) that ran at the Spring meetings. Hal-
lowmas won a couple of races at Randwick and a
couple of thousand pounds for his second in the Mel-
bourne Cup. Almissa won a pair of Cups. Sand-
bath got home once. But Eudorus, though often near
a victory, was never near enough. This aged im-
portation seemed to have a great chance for the
Williamstown Cup after running Cider to a head in
the scramble for the C. B. Fisher Plate; but it
transpired that he cracked a heel whilst chasing
Cider, which accounted for his absence from the
Williamstown event. Eudorus was improving all the
time at Caulfield and Flemington meetings, and if
he stands up to his work he should win a good race
presently.
* * * *
News comes that Carbine has retired from
the stud, having reached his 28th year. He will still
have his home at Welbeck Abbey, where his Grace
of Portland leads the simple life on rare occasions.
* * * *
The Flemington Stewards’ leniency in the
Shanahan case was followed by unsatisfactory riding
at Caulfield a week later, when in the Armadale Cup
C. Andrews, on Magas, crowded Sampson, on Mousme,
out of first or second place in the run home. This
time the stewards pushed the offending moke out
among the “also rans.” Mousme took second place
and its gilt. Then the stewards considered Andrews,
and that crack light-weight found the tariff had
risen since Flemington. He got “three months,”
and will, therefore, not be available for further en-
gagements till the Y.A.T.C. Autumn Meeting. That’s
the way to meet that sort of thing. Keep trebling
the penalties. Like other people, a jockey is amen-
able to the reasoning of his money-bags. #
The Tote again! Last week, at Belmont
Park, Westralia, Misfire, winner of the last race,
paid £lO6 13s. for a 10s. ticket. The best the
bookmakers were offering was £2O to one.
* * * *
AtLismore (N.S.W.) last week the jockeys
went on strike, but were persuaded by the stipendiary
steward to go on and leave their grievances to be
adjusted later. The trouble was that under the old
scale they received £3 for a winning mount and
£1 for a losing ride. The new scale gives them
10s. all round, and 5 per cent, of the stakes for
winning. As the average country race is under £3O,
it is small wonder they struck. The wonder is they
didn’t kick with both feet.
* * * *
Cue (W.A.) put up a record of a sort at
its recent race meeting. In the amateur-rider event,
all the jockeys declared 4st. overweight!
Talking of stipendiary stewards and their
shortcomings, it is argued by onlookers who saw
most of the game that Cider was bumped out of a
victory in the V.R.C. Derby. The plucky little gee
came back to scale with one shoulder badly barked
where the rails had rubbed, and had Agar Wynne
lodged a protest the wound would have been pretty
strong evidence of interference. But the lawyer took
his grievance philosophically and declined to squeak.
There was never a more stirring boxing
match in the world than the one between Herbert
McCoy and Hock Keys, at the Stadium, on Saturday
night! As long as the Mclntosh can stage “fights”
like that, men will call him blessed, and when he has
gone they will engrave tablets of brass to his memory.
The feature of the fight was the series of wild rallies
in which Keys cut loose, and with amazing energy
slapped and banged and bashed and uppercut and
walloped and jabbed McCoy, till that distracted per-
son’s face became a grinning mask of dazed and
frightened humanity. It was terrific ; nothing ap-
proaching it has ever been seen in the Stadium before.
McCoy fought back desperately, but was out-punched
and bewildered, and would have been outed but for
Keys’s inability to land a really heavy blow. On
the other hand, the plucky, clever Melbourne boxer
over and over again disconcerted Keys’s splendid
attacks. He paid much attention to the Sydney
boxer’s body, but had no hope of making any im-
pression there —Keys covered it with his glove, fore-
arms and elbows. His iaw was also invulnerable —•
he guarded it beautifully, although in doing so he
left his cheek undefended, and McCoy managed to
lay it open. If Keys could have jambed his wallops
home , with the same steam as McCoy, then _ McCoy
had gone to bed in ribbons. Keys’s attack failed for
sheer lack of hitting power ; but he was the attacking
party all the time ; and although his attack failed to
out McCoy, or even mark him, he scored the points
all the same. In this case this writer is a voice cry-
ing in the wilderness; but the truth is mighty and
must get out. In his opinion Keys won the fight,
though McCoy got the verdict. Of course, it all
depends on how one judges points in a boxing match.
It is, however, obvious that a man can be only cer-
tain of winning at the Stadium by knocking the other
fellow right out. Keys couldn’t do it because he
hadn’t the strength, and McCov couldn’t do it because
ho hadn’t the skill. Yet McCoy got the verdict.
The scribe relieves his feelings by remarking “Rab-
bits !”
* * * *
Bing-side bawler to Hock Keys on Satur-
day night: “ ’lt ’im in the belly. Hock; ’e don’ like
’em there!” It was a high, shrill voice. Back came
the answer in a deep bass: “Who th’ ’ell does?” Fur-
ther altercation was drowned by a mighty smack, as
Keys sprang in and did it. The fairest and most
warlike picture in the Australian ring is the coming
of McCov from his corner, after a rest, to take his
gruel. The worse the precedmg _ round, the fairer
and more bravely he comes. He is a game fighter.
» * * *
Johnson’s ( life these days seems to consist
of being run ' in on some fresh charge and being
promptly bailed out by his mother and others. One
admirer who wished to go bail for him inadvertently
represented that the property of a relative was his
own; so, while Johnson is at large, his would-be
bondsman is doing time for making a false declara-
tion. A nigger “down South.” who had been playing
similar pranks was riddled last week, and Johnson
is wondering, no doubt, what sort of thanksgiving he
ought to render his Obi, or Ju-Ju, or Joss, that he
wasn’t down South when his particular storm blew
suddenlv out of the peaceful sky.
* * * *
“Adelaide”: The recent fight between
Paul Til and Frank O’Grady, in this town, was dead
against all The Bulletin has said lately about the
French method being pretty but ineffective. Til was
ineffective all right, but that was just because
O’Gradv was pretty. Til appeared to have a contract
to shift huge" chunks of atmosphere anywhere round
the other man’s head; and he worked hard at it.
Meanwhile. O’Grady worked hard at him. It was a
most gentlemanly affair, with smiles and hand-
shakes, and each man helping the other up when
necessary ; but this does not mean that it wasn’t a
good, willing go. It went the whole 20 rounds, and
O’Grady won comfortably on points. He did it with
his head and his' feet—-particularly the feet.
The news that Hugbie Mebegan, Austra-
lian light-weight champion, is to box Freddy Welsh,
light-weight champion of England, for a purse of
£lOOO, at London National Sporting Club, is an
amazing item. The National Sporting Club is about
the most parsimonious of promoters; and it is almost
a certainty that this wild extravagance will cause
somebody connected with the club to take a blue fit.
In addition to the £lOOO put up by the reckless
club, there is to be a side wager of £lOOO. This
will be easily Mehegan’s star stunt to date; and the
fact that he "should put it up at the National Sport-
ing Club shows that the impossible is not always
unachievable.
* * * *
Healy and Boardman have got back to
Australia after a string of swimming successes in
Europe. One or other won international scratch
races in England, Scotland, Ireland, France, Bel-
gium and Italy. Healy tells interviewers that the
lengthy Kanamouki from Hawaii seems to owe his
terrific turn of speed over a short distance to un-
usually big feet, which he “manipulates like pro-
pellers, and stirs the water behind in much the same
way as a steamer fitted with twin screws.” Healy
is confident that Australian swimmers would do
better at the Olympic games if Europe were not so
keen on fresh water. With its lack of buoyancy, the
Australian crawl is done after about 200 metres,
and then the old trudgeon-stroke holds sway.
“Snell” : “They may be great people, these
Yanks,” is the conclusion to a recent Bulletin par.
that might have been written by Jove; “but they
are precious poor sportsmen.” On this point the
opinion of W. Murray, the Vic. champion ped. just
back from Olympia, via Stockholm, should have some
value. He went to the games strongly prejudiced
against the Yanks; he came away convinced that
there is only one greater bogey than the allegations
against American sportsmanship, and that is the
tradition about British fair play. The Americans, he
says, are keen, enthusiastic, and well organised.
They are in to win, but there was not a dirty trait
in any feature of their work in the Olympic games.
All the unsportsmanlike conduct before, during and
after the international tournaments was English, bar-
ring one little guttural growling from Australians.
If Britishers did not so persistently befoul the other
fellow, their own fairness might appear to be in
need of sand soap.
* * * *
The long-drawn-out match between N.S.W.
Slid Westralian cricket teams came to its much-
deferred end on Monday, when Ma won by 155 runs.
Westralia started well, hitting up 318 to Ma’s 305.
Ma then got "oing again, and, with eight wickets
down for 375, declared the innings closed. This left
Westralia with 363 between it and victory; and as
the team accumulated only 207, it lost by the above-
mentioned 155. #
The voice of one of the Westralian cricket-
ers was heard complaining in Sydney’s marble halls
that the team had been badly slighted and meanly
handled. It had expected to be taken round and
given a good time, whereas it had been treated
worse than the Canadian cadets. When teams went
to the West, they fed ’em up, took ’em round,
tickled their ears and filled their pockets with
smokes, he hinted. At time of writing no reply had
been made. If one ever is made it will be to the
effect that the N.S.W. Cricket Assoc, used to do
that sort of thing also, with the result that the
money intended to foster cricket was spent in riotous
living. Experience of many teams has taught the
Sydney authorities that it is best to take cricket
seriously. Men come here to play cricket —not to
have their stomachs knocked for six with goldtop,
pate de foie-gras and other wild batsmen. And if
cricket, like love, isn’t enough, they should stay at
home.
* * * *
W. P. McElhone’s idea of giving Victor
Trumper a benefit received a sudden bump, ap-
parently ; and that vigorous and war-like personage
is going about wearing a hurt expression. For some
reason or another, “Fighting Mac” had set his
heart on Trumper having that benefit, probably to
demonstrate (1) that the fierce battle he waged
against the Sorry Six was not tinged with personal
feeling; and (2) that in spite of all differences he
has a genuine admiration for the once-mighty bats-
man. What went wrong is a mystery ; but the bene-
fit is alleged to be off. It’s safe to bet that the
old rancor between the ones who were taken and
the others who were left is at the bottom of it.
Either the Sorry Six wouldn’t play the team to save
its life, let alone to give Victor T. a purse, or the
team said it would sooner use the bat on the Sorry
Six than on the ball.
* * * *
If ever there was a batsman’s day it was
Saturday in Sydney. Yet not a single century
appeared in the whole metropolis. It was the ball
that did the coruscating. Hordern, of North Syd-
ney, smashed up no less than nine Gordon wickets
for 53, five of them clean bowled; Tierney, of
Middle Harbor, took three of Sydney’s for 16 ; Stack,
of University, took four of Central Cumberland’s
for 10; Dupain, of Burwood, five of Bal-
main’s for 35; Randell, of Middle Harbor,
six for 41, to the woe of Sydney. The high-
est score of the day was the 86 of Hodgkin-
son, of Sydney. Poidevin, of North Sydney, carric 1
his bat for 70; Lowe, of Middle Harbor, smacked
73. Below these were only mediocrity and diluted
failure. It was the end of the fourth round, and
North Sydney dished Gordon by 123 runs. Univer-
sity made a holy show of Central Cumberland by an
innings and 142 runs.. K Uni. smacked 250, and Cen-
tral Cumberland replied with only 86 and 21. Great
was the rout thereof. Waverley overthrew Redfern
by seven wickets and two runs; Petersham climbed
out on top of Glebe with 59 to spare on the first
innings; Balmain gave Burwood a decisive dishing,
153 on the first innings; and Sydney slammed
Middle Harbor with 65 to spare on the first effort.
Owing to the heavy downpour on Friday
Melbourne’s Saturday cricket consisted of a solemn
procession of batsmen to and from the pitches. Here
and there a lusty youth, with a quick eye and hand,
put up a respectable score, but the bowlers had the
thick end of the stick, though few of them handled
it deftly. Of the smiters, Owen made 72 for Roy,
Ryder 45 for ’Wood, and old, reliable Peter McAlis-
ter 81 for East. Of the trundlers, Anderson, of
’Varsity, took three for 26, and Morsehead and
McLean, of Carlton, six for 47 and three for 22 re-
spectively. The others failed to grasp the nettle of
opportunity by the forelock. Of the 10 teams play-
ing, onlv South and Northcote failed to shape de-
cently, and what will happen to them depends
mainly on next Saturday’s weather. Scores: Cote,
72 v. Carlton’s 86 for five wickets; South, 116 for
nine wickets (declared) v. University’s 44 for three;
’Roy, 185 for six (declared) v. St. Hilda, 30; East,
204 for three v. North, and ’Wood 132 for six (de-
clared) v. Essendon 27 for nine wickets.

Consider the deadliness of this season’s
cricket-ball, as delivered by the subtle wizardry of
H. V. Hordern. That professor of the occult has
bowled for North Svdney in three of the four matches
played to date, and this is the way of it: Eight for
132 • nine for 80 ; 14 for 69 =3l for 281 ; average,
906 No less than 14 of his victims have been
clean bowled, and of the rest, such as were not
caught off pussy strokes, forced on them by the little
red jeering devil in the leather, were lured into
picturesque attitudes in front of the sticks with dis-
astrous results. North Sydney, in consequence, heads
the competition list with nine points; last year s
premier club (University) and Waverley follow.
Next Saturday University and North_ Sydney _ meet,
and wide as the metropolis is the interest in the
clash of the black art of Hordern and the mighty
smiting of Barbour, Willcocks, Tozer, McElhone and
Co. Uni. should also have the services of Roy Min-
nett, just returned in the pink of condition. Can
the wizard hold his one-magian team up against that
crowd? # #
“Sport and Pastime in Australia,” by Gor-
don Inglis, published by Methuen and Co., of London
(from George Robertson and Co.), is a book that no
good Australian should be without. It is beautifully
printed, charmingly illustrated; and (best of all)
brightly written. It is excellently indexed, too, and
thereby establishes its place as a valuable sporting
reference book. Also, it is up to date tells, for
example, how in 1912 Savonara became a Sydney
yacht, and that Mr. Hordern’s Kangaroo is the cham-
pion motor-boat of the continent. Another book of
the same series is “The Complete Swimmer,” by
Frank Sachs. It deals exhaustively with its subject,
but is more instructive than historical, although very
complete yarns are given about some mighty am-
phibians. #
Tasmanian cyclist A. F. Grenda is on the
war-path in the United States, and has announced
his intention of representing the Kangaroo in the
next N.Y. six days’ race, with, probably, E. A. Pye
for a team mate. After that he will pass over to
gay Paris for a couple of months, and then hie back
to'the U.S.A. after the elusive dollar. So, presum-
ably, one will have to write him down as “ex-
Australian” after this. One of the interesting fix-
tures at the meeting at Newark, at which Grenda
has been cutting a very respectable figure, was a
race between the world’s professional champion, F.
L. Kramer, and the world’s amateur boss, Donald
McDougall. The distance was half a mile, and the
race limited to three heats. Kramer won the first
two the first by a length, the second by half a
wheel; and both are said to have been done in rather
slow time. There’s too much finesse about the
modern sprint cycle race. They ought to be set
going after a pacer, and made to take a flying
start when the judge thinks they are really in full
flight. # # # #
The N. S. Wales team of bowlers which
has been roaming through Britannia’s realm is now
spreading itself over the Ma State. It seems to have
been an amazing team. Among other things, it
a cted as an immigration agent, and turned the faces
0 f 50 waitresses this way. They were not too lucky
(the bowlers, not the girls) in their British cam-
paign from a playing point of view, but they kept
up Australia’s name as good, sports. There is a
whisper that the trip cost altogether £21,000.
What a time they must have had!
. * * *
The Australian bowlers are getting ready
for a big kick-up at Melbourne next month. Each
State is to send a team to Willie Watt’s capital,
and when they are all gathered together a regular
trundling Armageddon will transpire, in which
champions will encounter champions in five rounds
of matches The grand finale will be a £2O single
and a £4 0 rink; and, after that, wassail and more
wassail. Bowlers are great on wassail.

fcydney swimmer A. \V. Barry is early
on the warpath, and the records are already in sight,
A week ago he streaked through 110 yards of sun-
light and green liquid in 64 l-ssec.—just on the
verge of a world’s record. On Saturday last he glode
through 66 yards of the hydrosphere in 34 l-ssec.,
which, though it doesn’t count as a record, is the
fastest time for the distance in Australia. Barry
is a hefty lad of 20.

A knowledge of Boxing is no load to carry.
But a WANT of the knowledge is likely to
turn out a very heavy load one day. Learn-
ing to be a skilful Boxer is fine sport. There’s
no other pastime so interesting—none so
healthful —none so useful. You can readily
become proficient in all the most serviceable
hits and stops. And, what is more, you can
learn in your own home.
I TEACH BOXING
POST
My methods are as thorough as is possible
—everything is as plain as plain can be.
The instruction is simple and efficient. It
will teach you to be a hard-hitting Boxer,
with an excellent defence. You will be
taught how to compel your adversary to
leave openings. You’ll cultivate the habit of
keeping yourself “covered” at all times.

I had a spar with a rhap here the other night. He lost his head-piece, and went for

“Hamer” : The linotyper who was rescued
from the fire which occurred at the offices
of the Australian Christian World
(Sydney) came from Victoria, where he
liad been badly burned in a fire which de-
stroyed his home and killed his grand-
mother. Some brands of accidents seem to
pursue certain people. No doubt the man
who meets with tram accidents may merely
have a talent for falling off those jugger-
nauts, or getting in front of them; but in
nearly every railway, shipping or mining
. r. a . . . ...
disaster, fire, flood or explosion, it is re-
corded that several of the victims or the
survivors had been in some or several similar
accidents. It seems as if a man who once
gets blown up T -'tkes a habit of it. Thus
you may remember that one of the passen-
gers on the Elingamit.e had been at sea only
three times, and had been in three wrecks,
I myself know of one Sydney-sider who is
the boat swamps, or the stoke-hole falls on
him—or something. There are, of course,
all-round men in the accident line—people

“Miles”: Re motor cyclists as an armed
force. An able article by “Critical” in the
Military Journal deals with the matter
and disposes of it in this way: “Their only
claim to usefulness is their mobility, which
they don’t possess, because it is impossible
to move along a road at 15 or 20 miles an
hour without scouting to front and flanks
when in contact with the enemy, and this
requires a network of good roads to allow
touch to be maintained.” It is a fair sum-
mary of the case. Cyclists are tied to roads
and have no show with troops not tied to
roads. Suppose the head of the cyclist force
to be attacked. The leading half would ex-
tend and leave their cycles to be wheeled by
the rear half over any sort of country the
occasion furnished. Think of the condition
of men wheeling two motor-cycles about
after a firing line, dodging from cover to
cover. If ambushed on a road, the head of
the column would probably be wiped out
before the rear half (two miles behind
nearly) knew what the disthrbance was
about. Then think of a body of cyclists in
a retreat —the rush for bikes, the confusion,
delay and inevitable disorganisation. Of
course no one disputes their value in the
Signal service; but for general purposes
they are as valueless as the officers who
discarded them decided that they were in
the S’Afric-an unpleasantness.
“Harrison O.”: A fresh attempt is to be
made to wax the crimson thread of kinship
by endeavoring to arrange for a uniform
pronunciation of the tongue that Marie
Corelli speaks. An Imperial Conference of
teachers held recently in the F“g was filled
with horror and dismay at the lack of uni-
formity. So the League of Empire (God
bless it!) has decided to establish a .central
bureau for the interchange of teachers from
all parts of the Empire. Interesting results
are expected from this brilliant scheme. For
one thing, Australian teachers who have
spent a year at a school in Yorkshire or the
Isle of Man will, when they return, be much
in demand at the houses of the Best People
—and even of merely Business People. I
shall take some lessons in “conversation”
myself. Recently I had to interview a
couple of more or less distinguished visitors
to these parts, one a Russian and the other
an Englishman. The vile English of the
Russian was quite intelligible to one who
has all his life been used to hearing the lan-
guage spoken incorrectly; but the cultured
English of the Englishman was almost illeg-
ible, even when examined through a mag-
nifying glass. I dared not use more than
about a quarter of my notes lest I should
report him as expressing himself against
the Maternity Bonus when, as a matter of
fact, he had merely observed that Collins-
street was the finest thoroughfare in the
world, only it was a pity Australian girls
had such large feet. Furthermore, I have
noted that whereas our courts generally
manage to get along without an interpreter
when the accused is a Chow, they are fre-
quently absolutely befogged when the man
in the dock is a newly-arrived immigrant.
This, of course, is because our uncultivated
colonial ears cannot understand correct
English when they hear it. Having regard
to these facts, I call for three cheers for the
League of Empire.
