After that the air was full of language that
looked like chain lightning.
“Scotia” holds out hope for Tasmania: —
If a recent report, that “true salmon” have been
taken in Tasmanian rivers this season, is right, the
fortune at that wilting little island is made. It will
get rich quickly, or, at any rate, parts of it .will,
uespice its Upper House, its dreadful legislators, and
its own Toryism and stupidity. The salmon, the
mighty sporting fish which leaves the sea and climbs
up fresh-water rivers during the summer months,
brings wealth wherever it goes_, nowadays. The ease
of the Mandal River, near Christiansand, in Norway,
is one among many that have come under the writer’s
notice. Fifteen years ago the banks of the Mandal
were inhabited by stone-broke farmers. The town at
the mouth of the stream was quite dead, except for
a little shipping and timber business. Then some
London sporting estate agents discovered that the
salmon fishing was good thereabouts. They leased
the whole river, from the mouth to the fosse, or
waterfall, beyond which the fish couldn’t pass. The
farmers got the most generous terms for the fishing
rights on their properties. It was only stipulated
that they shouldn’t fish for salmon themselves, and
to that bargain they most honorably adhered. They
put their rods, night-lines, etc., aside, and, when-
ever they wanted a salmon thereafter, they got it
by means of dynamite. This, however, by the way.
Wealthy tourists, with retinues of servants, began to
arrive. They thought nothing of paying from five
to 10 guineas a week for the use of a sizeable farm-
house. Also, they bought unlimited supplies of
produce, and worried little about the price. The
stores and hotels started to look up ; and Mandal is
now affluent. There are millions of tourists’ sover-
eigns for Tasmania if it is really able to provide
salmon in fishable rivers. What with its superb
climate, its beauty, and the fact that salmon, if they
do it all there, should do amazingly well, the little
place ought to allure anglers from all over the
world. Attempts to acclimatise salmon in the Com-
monwealth have failed, not because there are no
rivers big enough or cold enough for them, but
because there is, apparently, no deep-sea resort
handy where they may recuperate and get back their
color and condition. (The salmon is, of course, a
sea fish pure and simple. It only comes to the
rivers for breeding purposes.) Millions of fry have
been liberated in Australia at various times. They
did well till instinct led them to the oc“an. After
that they were never seen again. If Tassy has a
marine hospital or rest-cure establishment for salmon
more or less at its gates, it will be of more value to
the island than several gold mines.

taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will

MOONLIGHT AND THE MURRAY.
When the stars put on their dresses
Of twinkling diamond light
And the snow clouds comb their tresses
In the Moon-glass of the night,
Then the trees and banks are softened
With the moonlight’s dignity;
While all things harsh are coffined
Where the River dreams to sea.

Over the residents of Coster Alley
Did rest A gloomy pall,
Alas the captain of the costers
Had passed beyond recall;
While the bells were merrily chiming
On glorious Christmas day
The doleful undertakers called
To bear his corpse away
The costers loved their captain
As a mother loves a son,
He was honest and straightforward
Though fond of beer and fun.
Tim’s soul has gone to glory
Groaned his broken-hearted sal,
When the costers sadly clustered
Around the coffin of their pal.

“Tiaki’s” belated send-off - : —
Years ago, before a land tax had put Capital to
flight, a vessel got caught on that part of Maoriland
which Tamati Tohunga calls “the fish-hook of Maui,”
known to the white man as Cape Kidnappers. The
crew was added to Henare’s larder, and among the
spoil seized was a barrel of gunpowder, at the time
unknown in those parts. Chief Torima informed
his people that it was “korau” (turnip) seed, and
would provif' them with that vegetable for ever.
He immediately ordered his vassals to clean the
ground for planting, and used up all his spare time
invoking the vegetable joss Tahu. Then Rawhiti
happened along and explained that the black seed
was put inside a hollow iron stick, a round hard
substance rammed on the top of that, and, being
fired, the round, hard substance was blurted forth
amid lightning and thunder. Torima was much
impressed, and offered Rawhiti a greenstone mere
and two or three of the show girls of the pa for the
firearm. That gentleman returned per hoof in a
few months’ time with an implement he called a
“ngutu-parera” (duck’s beak), which is Henare’s
name for a flintlock musket. He fed it with the
turnip seed, which he called “paura,” topped it up
with a bullet and fired at a rata tree. Torima dug
the bullet out of the tree with his greenstone axe
and said, “Blime, this is orlright. Bring me six
more and I’ll give you much greenstone and lady.”
The six “duck’s beaks” duly arrived, much to the
joy of Torima, and the terror and depopulation of
the nearest hostile provinces. But Torima had not
heard that famous military maxim, “Keep your pow-
der dry.” His arsenal leaked and his powder became
damp. He had almost succeeded in drying some of
it in a large frying-pan over the cookhouse fire when
he was suddenly called away to attend a tangi. Un-
fortunately, it was his own tangi.


The Message of Sanatogen
No preparation of its kird has ever achieved such
a reputation as Sanatogen.
Many causes have contributed to this unpre-
cedented result.
Pre-eminent among them is the constant prescrip-
tion of Sanatogen by the medical profession which,
as a body, has awarded it the highest praise. More
than fifteen thousand doctors have written in the
most enthusiastic terms to describe the benefits they
have obtained from the use of the preparation
which The Medical Press and Circular states “has
strengthened the physician’s hand a hundred-fold.”
Everyone knows now that it gives
To the Sick —Health
It does this by its power of revitalising the
nervous, vascular, muscular, and digestive systems,
which it rapidly restores from a condition of depression
to one of normal action. By virtue of its composition
—pure milk casein in chemical combination with
organic phosphorus, an essential constituent of the
brain and nervous system —it feeds the brain and
nerves, it improves the condition of the blood, it helps
the stomach to digest other food, and it thereby develops
and strengthens the muscles. In this way it gives
To the Weak —Strength
and that delightful feeling of returning vitality which
tells the sufferer that he has conquered the disease
which had conquered him, and is once more on the
road to health, and the possibility of taking up his
life again where he had laid it down.
Sanatogen, however, does even more than this.
It has its message for those whom disease has not
touched. Everyone is capable of increased vitality and
vigour. By feeding the vital centres Sanatogen gives
To the Healthy—Vigour
so that work becomes a pleasure, toil becomes enjoy-
ment, and the whole system takes on a feeling of
•uoyawey, alertness and energy which is as delightful
as it is unusual.
Sanatogen’s merits have not only been eulogised
by the medical press, and by the medical profession,
but have also been endorsed by hundreds of the besv
known men and women who have testified to the
cures it has wrought in their cases.
Sanatogen may be obtained from all chemists.

NEW SYDNEY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The report of Mr. Hay, the English expert ap-
pointed by the Government to report upon the best
means of dealing with the traffic problem in Sydney,
was laid upon the table of the Legislative Assembly.
At the outset of his report Mr. Hay says: (Usual
mass of expensive remarks.) —Daily paper.
Gome, let us to the North Shore bridge!
It stands up high and wide;
From it a boat looks like a midge
Adrift upon the tide:
And the trams sweep downward from the ridge
Across its spans to glide.
The topmasts of the passing ships
Pass underneath our feet;
The sea wind’s salt upon our lips;
The view, girls say, is “sweet” ;
Yet about us are the cracking whips
And noises of the street.
That noble platform hung in air
Revives our civic pride;
Distinguished strangers stand and stare
In manner dignified ;
But they could not match it anywhere,
Not even if they tried.
Now let us take the Underground
As far as Bondi beach ;
Just at the Quay our train is found—
The station soon we reach.
Yes, our traffic management is sound ;
The whole world we can teach.
The city street above our train
Is thronged, but not too full;
And folk no longer fight to gain
The cars that buck and pull
In the swarming, crooked traffic drain
Planned by the early bull.
The station up at Wynyard Square
Is where the trains set down
The business folk who used to swear
Some distance out of town,
And exhume an extra penny fare
To ride in tramcars brown.
These fine improvements you and I
Will view with pride some day ;
Just now they’re castles in the sky—
We’ve other bills to pay.
When the clouds on Cohen’s brow roll by
We’ll think again of Hay.
N.S.W.
FOE.
“Armes”: After his visit to the Sultan,
the German Emperor recommended a first-
rate man to organise the Turkish army.
This was Goltz, whose fame as a strategist
is world wide. His work doesn’t seem to
have helped things to any sky-scraping ex-
tent. But it has been often so. Colley, of
Majuba-disaster fame, had a huge reputa-
tion, and wrote the principal war articles
for the Encyclopaedia Britannica ; but
in actual strife his record is simply bad.
Moltke, a Dane who had transferred his
services to Prussia, once reorganised the
Turkish army, and actually led Ottoman
forces in Asia Minor. His operations were
not successful, but he caught the Prussian
eye more than ever. Considering the
amount of outside help and advice it has
had, the Turkish military establishment
ought to have been the thing it wa: sup-
posed to be, but isn’t.
Recently some Melbourne friends of the
late 1 red R. Norton, the ornate advertise-
ment canvasser, decided to place a marble
counterpane on bis little bed in Ivew Ceme-
tery. Now, the late Fred B. was nothing if
not convivial. He had graduated from the
English music-hall stage to the Melbourne
advt. market, and conviviality, tempered by
strict attention to business, was his habit
home time previous to his death he re-
marked that the last words of Omar Khay-
yam s Rubaiyat *Turn down an empty
glass 7~ Wo P ld go suitably on his tombstone,
iherefore it was proposed by his memorial
committee that Fred B. should be finally
disposed of with the apt quotation. But the
Ivew Cemetery trustees wouldn’t sanction
Turn down an empty glass.” They con-
sidered it disrespectful to the adjacent dead,
or insufficiently gravelike, or insulting to the
Holy Order of Rechabites, or something.
JLliey compromised, however, by permitting
the penultimate verse of Omar to be in-
scribed on the slab :
Yon rising Moon that looks for us again, '
How oft hereafter will she wax and wane;
How oft hereafter rising look for us
1 hrough this same Garden—and for one in vain!
It takes cemetery trustees to feel the poetry
of the suggestion that Fred B. Norton used
to pass, moonlight nights with his
friends in the Garden of Corruption
at Kew. But perhaps those trustees
had something buzzing in their heads.
The great mausoleum on the right as
you enter the cemetery gates is the residence
of the late David Syme, of the Age, built in
the Egyptian style and worthy of a Pharaoh.
Near by is a tall monument to the memory
of the late Halfey, who fattened 'on the
advts. of the local Herald, which lie owned,
more or less. Nothing that smacks of fri-
volity appears on their tombstones; so, per-
haps, the trustees have an idea that it
would be unseemly for an advt. canvasser
to be mentioned in connection with liquid
refreshment.
“N. or M.”: Tasmania’s King Island—
famed for fat cattle and that explosive little
news sheet, The King Island Register—
wants to change owners and be hitched up
with Victoria. Geographically, it is a little
nearer the mainland (50 miles from Cane
Otway, as against 60 from Cape Grim) ;
but that isn’t much of a reason for bump-
ing into various awkward clauses of the
Commonwealth Constitution. The island,
which takes its name from Governor Gidley
King, had once a close shave of conducting
its affairs under an alien flag. English and
French were running each other pretty
close in this part of the world in 1801, and
Commander Black, . of the Harbinger,
hoisted the Union Jack on the island in the
face of an exasperated French expedition
that had also just landed. Afterwards it
became a pretty good place to keep clear
of. Bands of outlaws refuged there, and
the rocky coast crunched many a ship that
had been lured out of her course by false
lights. And things were bad enough even
without false lights. Between 1835 and
1877 there were 21 wrecks—principally on
the West Coast. Prominent in the cata-
logue was the loss of the female-convict
ship Neva, in 1835, though more tragic was
the later wreck of the Cataraque, which
went down with 414 souls on board.

BRISBANE
IN
BANANA LAND
A DEFINITION.
“He is what you'd call a financial pessimist."
“ What's that? ”
“ He's frightened to look cheerful for fear people
will want to borrow money from him."
“Don Q.” : Bananaland’s Railway Depart-
ment, which boasts that it holds the world’s
record in the matter of immunity from loss
of life in railway accidents, was “rattled”
by a recent smash at Redbank, when a goods
train went up in splinters. Fortunately no
lives were lost. The disquieting circum-
stance was that the goods special, which
smashed into a standing train, was running
just ahead—some 20 odd minutes —of the
Sydney mail. Had the positions been re-
versed, the mail, which runs through Red-
bank at 45 miles an hour, would have piled
up its remains. Railway men pooh-pooh the
idea of any accident happening to the Syd-
ney express, alleging that extra precautions
are taken to clear the road for it; but the
ugly fact is that the block system broke
down on this occasion. As a matter of fact,
for 18 months the block system between
Brisbane and Ipswich has been constantly
hung up or otherwise maltreated ; and there
seems to have been considerable commotion
in the Department in consequence. A week
after the smash the Railway Commissioner
shuffled the staff of traffic managers. The
Southern Division man was transferred to
Townsville, and the Townsville man came
south. Perhaps to make the change look
less significant, McGrath (Central Division)
was transferred to the Downs, and. the
Downs man was sent to Rockhampton.
A Ballade of Bills.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In glad, mad rags did we race away,
Never a care in our souls had we ;
And never a thought of the price we’d pay—
Those were the Bays of the Foaming
Spree.
N ow\ when the dregs of the feast we dree
And sit forlorn in the shivering skin,
Where, oh where, shall the naked flee?
These are the Bays when the Bills come in.
Our nights were lit by a gladsome rav.
Our songs we sang with a drunkard’s
glee ;
And some were giddy and all were gay—
Those 'were the Bays of the Foaming
Spree.
“Care be damned!” was our maxim free;
“End is none to our Uncle’s tin !”
Now he clamors from Oversea—•
These are the Bays when the Bills come in.
And so we frolicked it all the May,
Tripped it merrily round the tree,
And swilled the bowl to a tippler’s lay—
Those were the Bays of the Foaming
Spree,
Now right sorry and sore we be,
Sore and sick as a curdled sin;
Begging grace of the Mortgagee—
These are the Bays when the Bills come in!
Envoi.
Brethren, list to a broken plea—
Just a trifle of ale or gin
Give us, pray, of your oharitee—
These are the Bays when the Bills come
in !
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, November 16, 1912.
My Dear Tiiahgomindah,—
The vice-royal Macgregor, who has been
acting as a sort of publicity agent for
George V. and his bigger half by distribut-
ing their photographs amongst the State
schools, much in the same way as circus
bills are occasionally handed round to the
children, took spell-oh to-day, and appeared
before the multitude at the Hospital sports.
Next week he will take another well-earned
rest from the slog of his daily work, and pro-
ceed to the Downs, where he will study the
simple.life of the perspiring harvester. It
must be fully a fortnight since his Ex. had
a week off.
The holy war of extinction proclaimed by
the Women’s Club against the mosquito is
being waged with vigor. On Thursday,
Health Commissioner Ellcington lectured the
club on the life, habits and quickest methods
of killing the insect; and since then resolute
females have been kerosening tanks, bath-
ing pools and other nurseries of the pest
with encouraging impartiality. It was news
to learn from the lecturer that only lady
mosquitoes bite, although that hard-hearted
animal, man, will no doubt say he is not
the least surprised to hear it.
Miss Astronomer Proctor spoke familiarly
of other worlds than ours to a packed and
perspiring Albert Hall audience on Wednes-
day evening. The building would have burst
asunder had another seeker after knowledge
been admitted, and much good money was
tearfully turned away from the door. The
star lady will endeavor to coax it to the
Palace Gardens, which is an open-air show,
on November 25.
Conductor Leonard Francis put the Lied-
ertafel through its paces on Wednesday night
with good results, the influence of new blood
being plainly noticeable in the choruses and
orchestral work. Mrs. Kitty Wagner Clarke,
in cinnamon satin with an overdress of blue
ninon, was the head-line vocalist, but none
of the songs she chose suited her usually-
pleasing voice.
On Monday morning a confused chatter-
ing in the vicinity of the Town Hall indi-
cated that several womenites were holding a
Wattle Day League meeting. Each com-
mittee lady conversed at high pressure with
her neighbor, and the result was more nerve-
racking than I could set down without the
aid of profane language. As far as I could
gather, the principal business was the ad-
journment for lunch. Anyhow, it was the
most interesting.
A section of Brisbane’s more flighty sex
is working for the establishment of a
Women’s Residential College in connection
with the University.
The Society .of Artists had a very good
show last week. President Colclough and
young Stanhope Hobday (who has inherited
much of his father’s genius) were two of the
most prolific exhibitors. Oscar Fristrom
showed several pictures, of which the most
striking was a very large nymph surrounded
by a very small forest. The veteran, how-
ever sent along some life-like portraits,
which make up for the nymph person.
Tattersall’s races to-day were tame to the
verge of dullness. Of the usual race-goers,
only Mrs. Jack Thomson, well-gowned as
usual, Mrs. Ned Tully, in biscuit and saxe
blue, and Mrs. Stanley Chapman in grey,
were noticed. The wind and the heat, or
both, no doubt accounted for the absence of
other chronic racecoursers.
The weddings of the week involved little
Doris Sellars, of South Brisbane, and
Leonard Lee, of Toowoomba; and Miss
McAllister, daughter of late schoolmaster
McAllister, decided to risk it with a young
Englishman named Smart.
Yours in the languor of early summer,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

for ex P° rt during the season. Which, no

Bluff old E. B. Forrest, addressing his
testimonialisers, recently remarked: “He
had never aspired fig be a leader, but he had
invariably'' followed a leader—sometimes a
good one, sometimes a bad one.” Forrest,
although a tough old Tory, lias always been
a straightforward politician. Nevertheless,
he mouthed the party gospel in all its in-
herent viciousness ! Follow any lead, good
or bad, so long as you stick to the party,
Governor Macgregor, opening the new hos-
pital at Kilcoy, was escorted by 100 local
boys and girls mounted on ponies. The
Chieftain remarked on the novelty of the
proceeding, and said that the only thing like
it in his experience was a Central African
deputation of some thousands of Arabs, fan-
tastically dressed and mounted, who came
out to get an interpretation of Adverse of
the Koran. Macgregor declined to interpret,
which was a pity. Gordon of Khartoum
would have done so promptly, and gained
huge kudos as a prophet.

1911, just issued :
Gold mined, 386,1G40z., valued at £1,640,323
the least since 1887. In 1910, it was 441,4000 z.,
valued at £1,874,955. Value of silver raised,
£56,305 —lowest figures since 1900. Value of cop-
per, £1,151,351 —the record. The nearest to it
was £1,028,179, in 1907. Tin gathered was valued
at £307,817 (£243,271 in 1910). The wheat
crop was 285,109 bushels (the lowest since 1902, and
737,264 bushels less than 1910) ; maize, 3,637,562
bushels (832,744 less than the previous year) ; sugar
made, 173,296 tons (37,460 tons less than 1910) ;
tobacco, 476,5321 b. (72,6141 b. behind 1910) ;
bananas, 1,151,516 bunches (it was 1,121,075 in
1910) ; sheep totalled 20,740,981, being an in-
crease of 409,143 over the flocks of 1910 ; cattle.
5,073,201 (58,498 less) ; horses, '618,954 (25,141
more). The wool produced was 142.382.2691 b. (in
1910 it was 139,250,8021 b. ; and 129,668,2981 b. in
1909).

“We have got to retrench all round. Make
no mistake about it.” — Forsyth , M.L.A.,
Queensland's leading financial authority (or
thereabouts).
According to Queensland’s Chief Protec-
tor of Aborigines, our black brothers at
Lloyds Bay have one beautiful custom. On
the death of a buck his widow must not talk
till her hair has been cut off. The cutting
is often postponed for quite a long time!
The Denham Government’s new Liquor
Bill proposes to allow “ bona-fide lodgers to
have liquor with their midday meals on
Christmas Eve.” But why not, also, some
hair of the dog with afternoon tea, or a
dochan-dorris in the breakfast cup. Every
tipple helps—to win the brewery vote.
Filed his petition in insolvency the other
day, in Townsville, P. F. llanran, Philp’s
one-time colleague. Hanran was probably
the most silent man who ever sat in the Q.
Assembly, and was also one of the most
open-handed business men in Northern Aus-
tralia. His financial fall will waken keen
regret in the hearts of scores whom his liber-
ality helped in time of trouble. t
“Kuscobin”: One of these days we’ll have
a big wreck on the Australian coast, and
the inquest will disclose that no wireless
messages got through. But will it disclose
why? The fact is that our biggest coasters
fitted with wireless carry only one operator,
whose salary is generally 30s. a week and
keep. And talking of wireless. One of the
men on a mammoth coaster told me that he
and two others in the service had compared
figures, and found that Commonwealth ex-
pert Balsillie’s estimate that 90 per cent,
of messaged were of the “love and kisses”
variety, was a wild exaggeration. They
found quite GO per cent, were about the
time of the boat’s arrival, weather, etc.
That’s a nasty one for Cupid !
“I.B.”: Re “Vigilans’s” par. (B.
14/11/T2) about “dummy” auctioneers in
Melbourne. A case arising out of this kind
of conspiracy has just run into its second
week in Brisbane. The person charged was
acquitted, out in the evidence some startling
admissions were made. One witness ad-
mitted that he had come up from Sydney,
where “practices as alleged in this case were
the x-ule.” “If you happen to be asked in
Sydney if such auctions prevail in Bris-
bane, you can say most emphatically they
do not,” was the Chief Justice’s comment
on this. “Puffers” and “rammers” were
two classes of gentry much discussed during
the trial —they were admitted to be big fac-
tors in securing bids.

whn J;.-mK° f r iU f d the short-sighted Labor partisan

Queensland is doing big things in the way of
peopling the North; but most of the new arrivals
settle in the South of Queensland, and decline to.
leave it. Here are some paralysing figures on the
way we are covering tropical Queensland with Euro-
pean settlers. Take the Central district for a start,

annum for a country nearly the size of France. But

astounding still. N.Q. has an area of 310,495 square

McLucas Gold Mines
(NO LIABILITY).
CAPITAL £lO,OOO in 40,000 Shares of ss.
each, of which 8000 Shares are Fully Paid
Up and 32,000 Shares are Contributing, pay-
able 6d. per Share on Application, the bal-
ance of 4s. Gd. is payable by way of calls as
may be required, but not to exceed Two
Pence per Share per month.
McLUCAS GOLD MINES (locally
known as Blackfellow Reef) are situated at
the head of Pikes Creek, 25 miles N.W. from
Dalveen Railway Station, containing 20
acres leasehold. Crushings have given as
high as Boz. per ton, with an average of
3oz. Bdwt. for 200 tons. This is
verified by recent Assays giving Boz. Sdwt.
per ton. About 3000 tons mixed quartz have
been raised and stacked at the mine. A
Report and Survey, by Mr. F. W. Thomson.
Mining Surveyor and Engineer, is attached
to the Prospectus obtainable from members
of Stock Exchanges, or E. Steele, Secretary
nro tem., Ruthven Street, Toowoomba. The
Provisional Directors are: Messrs. D.
Sheahan, Sen., Stanthorpe ; and G. Critten-
den, R, Rosenstengel, J. Sunderland, IT. C.
Pointer and G. Feldmann, of Toowoomba.

AUDITORS’ REPORT.
We report to the Shareholders that we have obtained all the information and explanations we have
required, and that we have examined this Balance-Sheet and Profit and Loss Account, which we fin
framed in accordance with the Returns and Estimates of value received from Australia and the Books oi
the Bank in London ; and we are of opinion that, on the basis thereof, this Balance-Sheet is properp
drawn up, so as to exhibit a true and correct view of the state of the Company s affairs, according
best of our information and the explanations given to us and as shown by the Books of the Company.
TURQUAND, YOUNGS & CO.,
London, October 7, 1912.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restore'
hearing no matter how old you are. No mystery of
secrecy about it \ we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them—the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason-, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds. , • , ~ a ■,
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
1 The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend - - -
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
le&st danger.
The price" is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. AVe do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you

Baby Dreadful Sufferer. Could Not
Keep Him from Scratching. Every
Joint Affected. Used CuticuraSoap
and Ointment and He Is Well,
"Enclosed find my son’s photo and I feel
by writing these few lines to you I am only
doing my duty, as my son was a dreadful
sufferer from eczema. At
the age of two weeks he
began to get covered with
red spots on his legs and
groins, which mother
thought was red gum or
thrush- but day by day
it grew worse until every
joint and crevice were
affected and baby started
screaming for hours day
and night, such a thing
as sleep was out of the
question. I took him to
two of Sydney’s leading doctors; one said it
was one of the worst cases he had seen, the
other did not think it so serious; one ordered
ointment for rubbing in, the other a dusting
powder. I followed their prescriptions for
over four months and still baby kept getting
worse. I could not keep him from scratching
so great was his agony.
“When he was five months old I tried the
Cuticura Remedies and I am very thankful to
say my baby is to-day free from all his suffer-
ing. His groins were bleeding when I started
and other parts affected were the lower part
of his body, under the knees, arms, in arm
joints, eyebrows and neck; but after twice
using Cuticura Ointment I began to see a
difference and by the time I had used one
tin, along with the bathing with Cuticura
Soap, baby was nearly cured. I still kept on
using the Cuticura Soap and Ointment, and
now, thank goodness, he is quite well and,
although he is now ten months old. has not
had any further return of the trouble."
(Signed) Mrs. G. Martin, 2 Knight St.,
Erskineville, Sydney, N.S.W., Mar. 31, 1911.

throughout the world, but a liberal sample ot
Cuticura Ointment, with a 32-page book on
the care and treatment of the skin and hair
will be sent free on application to R. Town#

MO MORE DANGEROUS MOSQUITO BITES.
After one application of “MOSQUITOLINE” no mosquito or other insect will trouble you. Protected
by this wonderful liquid, you can even sleep in the open without a net. Mosquitoes will shun you as
you would shun the plague. Quite agreeable to human beings, but obnoxious to all insects.
THE DISCOVERY.
The world owes much to the
researches of that well-known
scientist —Dr. Gunther, who
has for many years labored
in Central Africa and India
in order to discover a potent
compound that will combat
the deadly Mosquito. At last
these years of effort have been
crowned with success, and he
has produced a very pungent,
although pleasant, liquid that
repels Mosquitoes and other
insects as though they had
been shot from a gun. This
liquid he has named “MOS-
QUITOLINE.” It is quite
harmless to human beings
and animals, but insects can-
not live near it.
FOE TO INSECTS.
MOSQUITOLINE not only re-
pels the attacks of mosqui-
toes, but gives absolute pro-
tection against stings and
bites of Wasps, Ants, Gnats,
Bugs, Fleas and Flies. After
one application you will be
completely immune from the
attention of these pests.
DR. PAUL GUNTHER
DANCER OF BITES.
It is common knowledge that
mosquitoes carry such death-
dealing germs as Malaria,
Marsh Fever and Ague, and
every*.time they bite there ia
the possibility of infection,
which may lead to serious
illness. MOSQUITOLINE will
protect - you from the ravages
;>f disease, even though you
sleep without nets in the
worst malarial districts.
LIFE WORTH LIVING.
With the discovery of MOS-
QUITOLINE, life is now pos-
sible in all parts of the
world where mosquitoes pre-
viously held complete sway.
One can now sleep in the
open without stuffy nets or
curtains, and during the day
attend to work —even though
the region is swarming with
insect life. MOSQUITOLINE
is positively invaluable where
insects have been wont to
make life unbearable.

* * *
“ Old Bill” Warman. allegedly Australia’s most
patriarchal printer, died the other day. Of his 84
years he had spent 69 at the compositor’s case After
his apprenticeship Warman went to Melbourne
Argus, afterwards migrated to the Empire, and next
drifted to the goldfields, where he got into a strike
mess and occupied Darlinghurst for a week. Coming
out he went to the Sydney Herald, and stayed
under Fairfax for a time. That demoralised him,
and he took a pub ; but coming to himself again he
went to the Bananaland Government Printing Office,
and with one or two brief breaks, remained there
ever after, serving under every Government Prmtei
that Queensland has had.
• * * *
“Looking Backward”: “Oldest Inhabitant (B.
24/10’12) is right about Barrington having no claim
to the authorship of the prologue concerning the true
patriots who left their country for their country s
good. It is a question whether Barrington ever
wrote anything. It is probable that piratical pub-
lishers used his name for sensational stuff scrambled
together by ill-educated literary hacks. A Mel-
bourne collector has one specimen which, so far as
I know, is unique in Australia. It is entitled Bio-
graphical Annals of Suicide, or Horrors of Self
Murder, whether impelled by Love, Penury,
Depravity, Melancholy, Bigotry, Remorse or
J ealousy- calculated to deter even the most Wretched
from this Horrible Crime, and enable them to Bear
vvith Fortitude the Ills of This Life by a comparative
y-few of the Miseries of some of their Fellow Crea-
tures; by George Barrington.” This book sets forth
that “the Demon of Suicide has spread its baneful
contagion as far as the inhospitable shores of N
Holland.” The writer describes his love for Anaboo,
the daughter of Parronnan, “an inferior chief.
She saved his life when he was attacked by a wile
dog, but instantly “disappeared with incredible
swiftness.” Nevertheless she fell m _ love with a
newly-married convict. The latter remained true to his
wife and two children, and Anaboo, in despair, Anns
herself over a precipice. She pitched upon a -minted
projection and died in horrible agony. Barrington if
it was Barrington, draws a beautiful moral as to the
miserv which attends sinful love. Harking back to
the Prologue, Alfred Lee, who found the evidence
against Barrington in the shape of Susannah Watts s
collection, discovered also a slight clue to the per
petrator. In the margin of the page on which it
appeared was a note in pencil: By II. Carter. A
study of periodical literature of the day might
reveal something else written by this unknown
gentleman. I have sought, but have sought it vainly.
It is really worth looking for.
“Downward L.”: While we’re discussing short-
hand, I’d like to sidle in the remark that Pitmanites
are very bigoted. One system is as good as another
in the hands of a capable man. The Gregg system
is running Pitman pretty close in America. Sloan-
Duployan, Script, Taylor and Pearman are used by,
some of the best writers in the London courts, and
at Westminster. Pitman’s system, theoretically, very
frequently clashes, and even the most experienced
users of it have to be continually on their
guard A careful outline differentiation diametri-
cally opposed to the rules in the text-book, essen-
tial to accurate reporting at high speed. rheoreti
cally, both Taylor and Gurney are simpler than
Pitman; but in actual practice their devotees have
to bullock harder than Pitmanites to get the stuff
down ; once down, however, it is easily read. George
Walpole, official writer to the Central Criminal
Court, London, for many years a top-notch Pitman
writer, discarded that system/for one of his own,
whereby he links the principal vowels on to his
outline's without lifting the pen, so as to mnxe the
reading-over easier. One of the best wnteis x have
ever worked with is old “Dad Macartney, of Free-
man’s Journal (Dublin), who, m his youth, re-
ported Daniel O’Connell, so he is no chicken. He
was official writer to the Commission on Irish Rail-
ways, 1906-1909. I assisted him in Dublin, and
had many opportunities of observing what a skilful
and accurate note-taker he was; he wrote Haidings
system. The advantage of Pitman is that the notes
of expert writers can be read fluently and accurately
by any writer steeped in the system. Not one of the
note-takers on the House of Commons Committee
staff (and they are nearly all Pitmanites) reads his
own notes; each man has a reader. They get along
all right, though Parliamentary Committee work is
very fast.
* * * *
The endowed municipal newspaper is a fact at
Los Angeles. The Municipal News of that U.S.
city is a weekly, but hopes to become a daily. It
receives a yearly subsidy from the city of over
£7000. Its job is to give accurately the news
concerning the city’s business, and the arguments,
fairly and fully, of the contending sides. Each
political party polling three per cent, of the vote of
the city has the right to use one column in each
issue, free of charge, for the purpose of setting
forth its views on public questions. The governing
committee of each party selects its own editor, to
edit its column, and the matter he hands in for
publication is free from any censorship except that
such matter must be lawful for publication. The
mayor and any member of the council may each
have the use of half a column in any issue. The
paper is under the control of the Municipal News-
paper Commission, composed of three citizens, _ who
serve without pay. The commissioners are appointed
by the mayor and council. The ordinance creating
the newspaper was passed by a direct poll ot the
voters.
A PROSAIC IDYLL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A strike of musterers occurred recently during
shearing time on a Darling River station. The men
wanted 375. 6d. per week and keep, but the manager
refused the request, and informed them that if they
were not satisfied with 30s. per' week they could
hand their saddles in, which 15 promptly did.
—News item.
We’re tired of reading of romance,
And sweetly-lilted pomes,
Composed by bards who ne’er by chance
Forsake their cosy homes.
We’ve read of riders in the morn
Who race across the plains,
And put the flying birds to scorn
With loosely-dangling reins.
And we’ve been told in many a rhyme
How grand it is to ride
From break o’ day till blanket-time
Across the paddocks wide;
To watch the brown bush spiders spin
Their webs from bloom to bloom —
And we were told it were a sin
Unless we gave them room.
’Twas quite romantic, too, to swing
Amongst the sylvan elves;
To hark to Pan, and try to sing
His ragtime tune ourselves.
’Twas quite inspiring; yet, somehow,
No matter how we thrilled,
Our stomachs (be prosaic now!)
Were never really filled!
And now we’ve come to realise
That though they deck with gauds
The morn, the spiders, and the skies,
Those poet chaps are frauds.
The rising sun may be a joy,
The morning incense sweet
But, hang it! even beauties cloy
Unless they’re good to eat!
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.

REXONA.
Amongst the most sensational star items ever seen on the Australian
stage are the aerobatic dances by Miss Ivy Schilling, including the
“Danse du Vaurien,” with Mr. Fred Leslie, in “Our Miss Gibbs’’: the
“Danse de Champagne,” with Mr. Leslie Holland, in “The Quaker Girl”;
also “Ju-no Kata,” with Mr. Fred Leslie, in the revival of “The Quaker
Girl,” now appearing at Her Majesty’s Theatre, Sydney.
While touring with the “Jack and Jill” Pantomime, Miss Schilling
won the Maskelyne and Devant Prize for Beauty at Christchurch (N.Z.)
—a valuable gold watch and chain, which she wears daily.
Miss Ivy Schilling is the winner of West’s Pictures Surf Sirens’ Com-
petition, in which close on 100,000 votes were recorded. Her figure is a
perfect model of athletic symmetry and grace. To preserve its litheness,
its vigor and its strength, she uses REXONA.
This is what the admired acrobatic actress says:—
Theatre Royal, Sydney,
Manager, Rexona, Sydney. March 11, 1911.
Dear Sir, —I have much pleasure in recommending your Rexona, for
I have been using it constantly for some time. As a rub-down after
acrobatic dancing, and as a magical reviver to strained muscles, I have
found it excellent. After surfing it is invaluable as a protection against
sunburn and freckles. It is a wonderful healer, and I always keep a tin
of it handy.
Yours sincerely,
(Sgd.) IVY SCHILLING.

The Swearing Skipper.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THROUGH the dark that wrapped the
city came the whistle of a train,
And' it sounded like a syren....l
could smell the salt again ;
And I saw the waters darken where the sun
had lately kist,
And I heard the dear old skipper swearing
softly in the mist —
“Bally barges!”
(And a barge slipped dowm beside us and
the skipper shook his fist.)
“Bally barges!” Nothing moving in the
words you’d think, and yet
In the wind across the house-roofs I could
feel my eyes grow wet,
And I seemed to see the rigging, sharp
against the mist and clear,
And the dear old swearing skipper—he’s
been dead this seven year—-
Bally barges!
(And the city stretched beneath me seemed
so infinitely drear.)
And I turned my # eyes unto the sea, and then
I turned away,
For I knew the ship I dreamed of wasn’t
lying in the bay,
And that whistle cut the silence, sharp and
resonant and clear....
And I felt the lift of waters, and a voice
said in my ear—
“Bally barges!”
(And.... 1 saw the swearing skipper who’s
been dead this seven year.)
VICTORIA.
PARTRICIA MURCHISON.
John Pascoe Fawkner.
A FOOTNOTE TO AUSTRALIAN HISTORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE day is the 19th October, 1899. I
turn up in a thick volume the page
bearing the day and the month, and I
note at a glance all remarkable or sugges-
tive Australian happenings which have
fallen on this “notch in the year’s calendar
stick,” since Phillip landed his first precious
consignment. And a name once of weight
and always of interest projects itself from
the chronicle, and reminds me:—
On the 19th October, 1803, John Pascoe
Fawkner landed on the shores of Port
Phillip, a lad of 11 years of age; and on the
20th October, 1868, the 76th anniversary of
the birth of that singular being, I, a lad
of 13, was taken by my father “to pay my
respects” to him, at his residence in Colling-
wood, Melbourne. The old man was holding
a sort of levee; and was reminiscent to the
point of garrulity. The whole affair bored
me terribly. I was the only youngster in a
crowd of mature and grey-bearded men; and
my presence was a source of discomfort to
everyone in the room with one exception—
old Fawkner himself. For the old man was
the freest of free talkers. Life had for him
no secrets, and youth no reticence to be re-
spected or sensibilities to be shocked. Men
and women alike were to him nothing more
than women to Odo of Cluny: “sacks of
dung.”
With the narrow vision and uncompromis-
ing canon of youth, I could not see what
there was in that dreadful old vulgarian to
respect, still less to honor. History was a
matter of kings, and kings’ mistresses, and
battles, and difficult-to-remember dates. I
had no sense then that in that bent form,
spare of flesh in body and face, with longish
grey hair covered with velvet skull cap, with
cheeks and lips and brow of corrugated
parchment, there was a Maker of History.
“Come here, sonny,” he said, and pulled me
forward. “Governor Collins did that to me
the day I landed from the Calcutta —that
was 65 years yesterday.” There was life in
the old dog yet, and the pinch hurt. I didn’t
realise that that tweak on the ear links me
to the beginning of things Australian, for
was not Collins Phillips’s secretary, and was
present when Australia was born?
* * * *
And the tweak given, the remembrance of
Collins stirred up currents of such malo-
dorous recollections that some of the other
sinners present, case-hardened though they
were by “good stories” in military mess and
club smoking-room, blushed, I dare swear.
Could I have been hurried out of the room
without offence to the veteran, I should have
been given wings. But there was fire still
in the old man’s eye, and virility in his
curious croak, and besides an interruption
might have set him on some other track —on
the ancient Fawkner-Batman feud, for in-
stance. And the queer stories, and the
shrewd comments, and the bitter invectives
were to all his hearers but myself History—
and Life!
* * * *
Beyond the sentence above quoted, and
one other I remember nothing of the ipsis-
sima verba of the talk. But that Collins
was a vile man, and John Batman viler,
and Governor Arthur the vilest of all man-
kind, I remember distinctly. Joseph Tice
Gellibrand was also wicked, and so was sur-
veyor J. H. Wedge, In fact, anyone who
had come into personal conflict with Fawk-
ner was beyond redemption. Nevertheless,
the world was not all bad. He had good
words for Woodruffe, captain of the Cal-
cutta, Sir Alfred Stephen, and for the
Hentys. Yet I learnt in later years that
Sir Alfred’s two prominent measures, those
affecting the Van Diemen’s Land titles and
newspapers, had all but ruined Fawkner.
Twenty years after that day in ’6B, I
ascertained that a very full account of the
old man’s talk had been written out by my
father. From that MS., with an occasional
reference to other credited sources of infor-
mation, has been compiled the following
note. Something of the virus of Fawkner’s
hatreds has been extracted, and such of his
assertions as cannot be verified, or have not,
at least, a strong substratum of probability
are omitted. The residuum is both history
and matter for history, and only a small
portion of it is available in printed form.
Hence this article.
* * * *
The expedition which was to found the
new colony in the south of the continent
voyaged in two vessels —the Calcutta and the
Ocean. Boy as Fawkner was, his surround-
ings were such that the coarser facts of life
were forced into his knowledge on the
vnvave out. His father, mother, sister and
himself were on board the Calcutta-—a one-
time Indiaman of 1200 tons, which had been
bought by the Admiralty. On the same
vessel went the Governor and stall, includ-
ing the chaplain. “Fairly at sea, every
official, high and low,” said Fawkner, set
all morality at defiance.” Governor Collins
bad left a wife and daughters in England
when he went out with Phillip in _ 1788.
Going out in command in 1803, he did the
same thing —for better reason. He had a
“wife” and family in Sydney; and before
the Calcutta had lost sight of the white
cliffs he had pardoned a prisoner, and taken
the prisoner’s wife —one of 15 married
women who had permission to accompany
their convict husbands —to his embraces.
* * * *
The liaison lasted for some months after
the arrival of the expedition at the Derwent.
Collins, holding that Port Phillip was un-
suitable for the new colony, transferred
ships, men and stores to the site of Hobart
Town in 1804. Then another new charmer
captivated his Honor, and he returned his
erstwhile inamorata to her proper allegi-
ance. He had barely settled down to his
readjusted household arrangements, when
the lady whom Fawkner called his Sydney
“wife” sent him a present in the shape of a
son, aged 12, and a daughter, aged 10! For-
tunately she did not go herself. She had
solaced herself with Crossley, the notorious
convict solicitor —the gentleman who had
got into trouble through swearing that life
was in a certain testator’s body when, he
made his writ. So it was. A live fly had
been inserted in the mouth of the dead man,
while Crossley traced the dead man’s name
with the pen held in the dead fingers.^
Lieutenant Bowen, with a company from
Sydney, had founded the Derwent settle-
ment in August, 1803. As was characteris-
tic of the ruling powers that came out from
England in these days, he attached to his
company (and himself) a young woman,
the wife of a Sydney convict, but the daugh-
ter of a free settler. In a letter still extant,
his Honor Lieutenant-Governor Collins
complained to the Governor in Sydney of the
“ill example set by Lieutenant Bowen.”
Collins was a humorist. Fawkner knew of
Lieutenant Bowen’s affair, but did not
know of this letter —and it is a pity he did
not.
On the way out the Calcutta called at
Capetown, then in possession of the Dutch.
News of the outbreak of war reached the
authorities while the Calcutta was Avater- j
ing, and the Dutch “Admiral of the Port” j
called on the captain (Woodruffe) to sur-
render the vessel and crew. The ship had
originally been fitted with 50 guns, but these
were for the most part in the hold. Wood-
ruffe, with Cohins’s sanction, invited _ as
many of the male convicts as cared to join
in the defence of the ship, and sent a reply to
the Dutchman to “come and take the ship.”
The admiral did not accept the challenge,
giving as his reason that his country would
have to keep the prisoners, and he did not
wish to be overrun by English convicts.
“Bobby,” the captain of the expedition,
was not irreproachable, in his relations with
the weaker sex, and Fawkner stated the fact
with quite surprising emphasis. “Everybody
knew, even us younkers, that the Governor
made the parson take up with Mrs. P., and,
therefore, it was no use anyone grumbling
when he put the Avoman to hear the chil-
dren the catechism. A marine’s wife told
the woman she ‘could read her child the
Commandments as well as another Ayoman
who broke everyone of ’em herself,’ and
Mrs. P. did her best to get the marine court-
martialled. She failed in that, and then
one day, when the parson was sitting as a
magistrate, she laid a false charge against
the other woman. The parson had to dis-
miss it, and then Mrs. P. said right out
before everyone: ‘Oh, I suppose you want
her for your doxy, do you? Are you tired
of me?’ And the parson did not do more
than grin.”
“Collins was a d —d fool, and worse, I
heard my father,” so Fawkner continued,
“say that it was well known before the
landing took place that he would never stay
there, because he was to get 500 guineas
from the Ocean’s owners if he abandoned
the first place he stopped at. So he would
not give Port Phillip a fair trial. He would
not consent to have the country properly
examined for wood and water. He had to
bring timber for sawing 14 or 16 miles, and
to get water Ave had to sink casks perfor-
ated with holes into the sand just beyond
high-water mark, so the water we got was
brackish. A prisoner who had escaped re-
turned, and told the Governor that he had
discovered a river. This must have been the
Yarra. But Collins did no more than pun-
ish the man for bolting.
“No, Collins never intended to stay at the
first place. To gain delay, he sent a crew
in an open six-oared boat to examine the
Tamar River —Port Dalrymple it was
called —in the north of Tasmania. They
reported badly, and then Governor Collins
decided on the Derwent, and engaged the
Ocean at per month to take everybody and
everything across. Five months the ship
took to make two trips across Bass Straits.
* * * -K-
-“When Collins died, Lieutenant Lord, next
in command, locked Government Cottage
doors and allowed no one in except one or
two of his personal friends. He spent the
time in burning nearly all the Government
papers and records. A year or two later he
blossomed into a great landed proprietor.
He had dozens of Collins’s grants in his i
own name and the names of his immediate |
friends and creatures. There Avere no books
to show whether Collins had granted ’em or
not. The books had gone up in thick smoke
through the Cottage chimneys.” Fawkner
said “chim’leys.”
“What about Buckley? Well, he bolted
from us in 1803, and 32 years after the
great ruffian —he was always a savage—was
found with the blacks. He was a scoundrel.
A black named Durrinart was friendly with
one of my party in 1835, and told him that
a large number of the blacks were gathered
for the purpose of murdering all the whites.
Buckley said if he could he would spear
Durrinart for giving us the information.
* * * *
“But, I haven’t done with Collins. He
spent, or cost, the British Government for
that expedition at least £150,000, but he
could not found a settlement, but I founded
a colony without costing the Government
one penny. You Melbourne people have

RUNNING ULCERS
Whole Leg a Swollen Mass of Inflammation—Bedridden for Months
together—Hospital & every description of treatment completely fails
Zam-Buk effects the most Remarkable Triumph of
Skin-growing and Disease-dispelling ever recorded.
As a triumph of skin-healing and disease-
dispelling, the victory by Zam-Buk in the case of
Mrs. S. Clifford, of 67 Gold Street, Collingwood, Mel-
bourne, is without parallel. This lady
says“ While going upstairs I slipped
and scraped the skin off one of my legs. I
neglected to give the wound any special
attention, and simply hound it up, thinking
it would he all right; hut instead of getting
better, the leg gradually grew worse, and
at the end of three months it had developed
alarming symptoms. The wound dis-
charged and presented the appearance of a
running ulcer, the pain being almost un-
bearable. I thought blood-poisoning had
set in.
The climax came one night, when my leg got so
bad and presented such serious indications, that I
made up my mind to go into the hospital tht next
morning. I did so, and there I was a patient, un-
dergoing continuous treatment, for eighteen
months. The doctors scraped the bone in the leg,
and the agony afterwards was indescribable.
“ In the hospital the doctors applied hot fomen-
tations and ointments to draw out the inflammation and
reduce the fearful swelling; but their efforts were
powerless to do me any good. At last my leg got so bad
that I had to become a resident in the hospital, where
the doctors applied hot fomentations to the wound every
hour incessantly for two days.
“ Then the doctors resolved to graft some skin over
the wound. They did so, but the ‘ graft ’ remained on
only two or three days, when it came away and a fresh
wound broke out and formed an outlet near the ankle-
bone, increasing tenfold my already overflowing cup of
misery.
" I was an inmate of the hospital for a month. I then
left that institution for home, and thankful I was to do so.
• “A was in tlie de P ths of tlie direst despair when my mother happened to be talking to a
neighbor about me one day, and telling her how I was suffering. She immediately asked mother
if I had tried Zam Buk, and so great was her faith in it that she strongly advised mother to get
a supply, and let me try it on my leg.
“So mother, fortunately, followed her kindly advice, and procured a pot of Zam-Buk. I
applied a dressing, and under the wonderful influence of Zam-Buk the pain ceased almost im-
mediately, the inflammation and swelling subsided, and I could actually feel the soothing powers
of Zam-Buk at work. With a joyful heart I continued with the treatment of Zam-Buk, and applied a
dressing every two or three hours, each application bringing additional relief. After a week of
the wound on the ankle had healed completely.
“ wa s a harder fight with the larger wound, however, but I knew that my case was a bad
one, and I kept on and persevered with Zam-Buk. The big ulcerated hole began to contract, the
bottom of the wound looked healthier in every way, and at the end of six weeks Zam-Buk had
completed its miraculous work, and the wound was healed right up with clean, new, healthy skin.”

intended. It is invaluable for both children and adults.

As a child Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy cured my coughs and colds
and I have always used it for my children. When Chamberlain’s Tablets
were placed on the market I felt confident that they would do all that was
claimed for them, for I knew that the manufacturers would not risk the
reputation of their Cough Remedy by putting out an inferior line. The
first time I had need of such a medicine I tried Chamberlain’s Tablets and
found them a perfect laxative. Since then I have used them for different
members of my family for indigestion, biliousness, constipation and sick
headache and have found them to be in every way just as good as
Chamberlain s Cough Remedy ; and I know of no higher praise to give
them than that. Chamberlain’s Tablets are a mild and gentle laxative and
we use nothing else in our family. I have found them to be quite as
safe and effective for children as for the grown up members of my family.”
Mrs. Mary Samurpson, Parramatta Rd., Leichhardt, N.S.W.
named your fashionable street after the
rogue who failed because he was a Gover-
nor ; but you are too damned ungrateful to
name one after me—who made the colony.”
I believe to this day, Fawkner’s name is un-
commemorated by any street in Melbourne
proper.
Then came the opening of the flood-gates.
The interminable Batinan-Fawkner contro-
versy as to who founded Victoria and Mel-
bourne once more lashed the old man into
rage.
“Batman stole his idea from me. It was
in March, 1835, I began to prepare to settle
Port Phillip, but I had gathered all the
explorers’ reports, from the men who used
to get mimosa bark at Western Port, from
sealers and whalers at Henty’s on the wes-
tern coast. I knew all that was to be
known. I ordered Underwood, my Launces-
ton broker, to get me a schooner to take a
party across, and all things for a new settle-
ment of free men, away from the hellish
despotism of Governor Arthur. J. A.
Brown, of Launceston, had a schooner trad-
ing between Launceston and Sydney. This
was the Enterprise of 55 tons, and t bought
her. But Brown’s Sydney agent had con-
tracted for coal trips between Newcastle
and Sydney, and I could not get possession
till July 18, 1835. And so Batman with
his party got off first in the Rebecca—and
took with him the rotten deed.” (This is the
famous “Dutigalla” treaty, prepared by J.
T. Gellibrand, ex-Attorney-General of
V.D.L. The original is in the Melbourne
Public Library, and a copy is in the Bri-
tish Museum. There is a curious history of
this document yet to be written.) “It was
as rotten as Batman himself.” (There is a
disagreeable story, unfortunately too well
authenticated, which will explain this allu-
sion ; Fawkner’s explanation was simply un-
quotable.) “He was to become the largest
landed proprietor in the world and to hold
a million acres.
“I got the Enterprise off by the 25th
July, thus loading and sailing down the
Tamar in a week. On the 27th I put out
from Georgetown, but had to put back owing
to foul weather in two days. I was too ill
to go on, and on 30th August I gave full in-
structions to Lancey, first and foremost of
which was to find a permanent river.
“My instructions were acted upon by
Lancey. After making Western Port they
stayed a short time, and left on the 14th
August. They entered Port Phillip on the
15th, and on the 28th they moored in the
Yarra. Batman’s representative, Wedge,
the surveyor, with a lie in his mouth, had
been trying for days to find our party, and
appeared on the scene with some of Bat-
man’s blacks several hours after Lancey,
carrying out his instructions, had marked
out 60 acres, or six lots of 10 acres each.
My party, I say, founded Melbourne, though
I did not get over first trip. Within a
month from the date of my landing I had a
four-roomed weather-boarded house built and
fitted up, and that was the first house fit to
be called a house in Port Phillip.”
* * * *
So Fawkner! And now from an unques-
tioned source the real reason why Fawkner
did not go by the Enterprise in her first
trip. The fact has never been published be-
fore. Fawkner was not the man to publish
it, nor was the gentleman who was the other
principal in the matter. Fawkner, great as
was his due of merit, ever claimed more
than his due, the other man ever less.
* * * *
It was bruited abroad in Launceston that
Fawkner, who had realised everything in
the shape of property that he could, did not
intend to return. He was leaving the town
heavily in debt, not merely for the expedi-
tion’s account, but with respect to previ-
ously-incurred liabilities. An association
consisting of six persons, of whom Fawkner
was chief, was responsible to Underwood for
the Enterprise. He had paid for stores in
part and his co-adventurers in part, but the
whole affair was a jumble of finance. Drop-
ping down the Tamar to the locality now
known as Stephenson’s Bend, the Enter-
prise anchored for a night. Here she was
boarded by a sheriff’s underling represent-
ing a whole group of creditors. There were
at first threats on Fawkner’s part of throw-
ing overboard, but Lancey, bluff but cool-
headed, pointed out that it was not certain
whether Governor Arthur’s authority aid not
cross Bass Straits, and whether the King’s
writ could not run to the proposed settle-
ment. Fawkner remarked that the people
in a place made the law, and if the King’s
writ ran in Port Phillip, it would only be
with the consent of the people there when
the settlement was established. Hunter, the
skipper of the schooner, then declined to up
anchor until the dispute was legally ended.
“He had to work within the law,” he said.
Fawkner then agreed to go to Launceston
and make adjustment of claims, but the only
adjustment he found possible was that the
vessel should not leave till he paid heavy
sums due, unless lie wanted to stay behind.
The former demand could not be met, so he
was perforce obliged to accept the latter.
He had, however, sent some horses to the
mouth of the Tamar, and desired to go down
with the vessel to see them shipped, and to
see her fairly away. Consent was not pro-
curable to the latter proposal until a bond
was entered into by Mr. John Cape, a well-
known merchant of Launceston and brother
of the distinguished schoolmaster William
Timothy Cape, for Mr. Fawkner’s return.
Mr. Cape, although not directly interested
in the adventure, was a creditor, but not a
pressing one, as he had faith in both Fawk-
ner and the scheme. Upon the Cape bond
the recalcitrants were temporarily satisfied.
* * * *
Fawkner, once at George Town, “forgot
the tenor of the bond.” At all events lie
said so later—he said he thought his pro-
mise, and therefore the essence of Mr. Cape’s
bond, was to return to Launceston from Port
Phillip by the return trip of the schooner.
But the fact is as stated, and proof is
afforded by the further fact that Captain
Lancey, on discovering, as he must have
done from Fawkner and from no one else,
the truth of the case, when the Enterprise
was already at sea for a day, insisted upon
the vessel’s return. Then eventuated Fawk-
ner’s most opportune sea-sickness.
Had Lancey and Hunter not been firm
and Cape generous somebody else would
have founded Melbourne and created the
“Empire of Victoria,” as old John Pascoe
Fawkner was not infrequently in the habit
of calling the province he helped to settle.
And had that somebody else been associated
with the ruling oligarchy in London, or Ho-
bart Town, or Sydney, as seemed at one
time imminent, Melbourne would have been
cursed for generations by being surrounded
by huge aggregations of estates virtually
controlled by one conscienceless corporation.
W.
Told at Vila.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
captain and I sat smoking together
I on the deck of his schooner, which
was lying at anchor in Port Vila.
The Capital city of the New Hebrides, with
its two stately stores, roofed with glittering
galvanised iron, and its hotel and its copra
sheds, stretched for many a dizzy yard along
the beach in front of us. Three figures,
dressed in white suits and wearing large
straw hats, moved slowly down the beach
from'the right, and disappeared in the door-
way of the principal store.
“The British section of the business popu-
lation of Vila is taking its forenoon drink,”
explained the captain. “The French crowd
patronises’ the hotel.”
A number of natives were carrying sacks
of copra to a launch that lay alongside the
rubble pier. Several passengers on board a
steamer that flew the house flag of Burns,
Philp and Co. stood with their heads rest-
ing on their arms, and their arms resting on
the steamer’s rail, gazing meditatively shore-
ward. It was a busy and animated scene.
I said so to the captain. He laughed and
remarked that 1 had come to Vila at a busy
time.
“There’s no life, no fun, in these cut-and-
dried hucksterers here. All the starch in’
them is in their collars. I’ve seen more real
fun in a little out-of-the-way port in New
Britain in one week than you’d see here in a
twelvemonth. But that was long ago. A
different sort of men have come down to the
Islands of late years. Well, every dog has
his day, they say. You see that topsail-
schooner lying over there?”
I nodded. ;
“She could tell you some curious yarns if
her old timbers could talk. I was mate of
her once. We were lying at Matupi, in
New Britain. Finlay, an old New Guinea
trader in pearl-shell, beche-de-mer and- —and
other things, was also there. He had sold
his own schooner to another trader, a Ger-
man, and intended to leave the Islands for
good and settle down in Australia as a Re-
spectable Family Man and Law Abiding
Citizen, and all that sort of holy mullock.
He was just about as fit for the part as a
turtle is for walking the tight-rope, but he
had paid for a passage with us, and we said
nothing to discourage him. Anyhow, as he
was worth about £3OOO besides what the
German gave him for the schooner he had
a right to please himself.”
“What sort of a man was he, Captain?”
“Tough, rough, nuggety sort of man with
a hairy chest, and a lot of good nature and
bad language. About 40 years of age, and
strong as a steam-winch. Before we left
Matupi he started on a spree with our Old
Man, Captain Reele, and a trader called
Thorsen, who was also a passenger. Finlay
said it was to be his last spree. It was,
too.”
The captain sighed, and opened a bottle
of lager;
“No reason why we shouldn’t have a
drink, however,” he remarked, filling a
couple of glasses.
“They drank night and day. Three such
champions at the game I never saw before,
and never hope to see again in these poor
temperance-ridden times. Finlay was a Won-
der, and Thorsen was a Terror; but our Old
Man could see them both under the table and
still come up smiling in a spotless white suit,
and with his hair nicely brushed, and his
beard trimmed. He was a picture to look
at —clean-built, six feet high, with a pro-
minent nose and large blue eyes. Just the
sort of man that some lady novelists I’ve
read would choose for a hero. A devil with
the women, too, I believe —white women. I
mean, of course. A white man doesn’t need
much beauty to be a Don Juan in the
Islands —if he likes to take risks.
“Thorsen was another splendid figure of a
mail. _ He was a Norwegian, and was one of
the picked crew that was sent to Chicago at
the time of the Exhibition, with the Viking
ship that was over a thousand years old. A
first-class seaman, and a beautiful performer
on the violin.
“One night I went down below and found
Finlay sitting on a locker with his face and
hands and shirt covered with blood. He had
fallen down and struck his head on the
corner of a box. Thorsen was lying under
the table, dead to the world. But the Old
Man was still sitting erect in his chair with
an empty glass in his hand, and a cold cigar
in his mouth. He was fast asleep. And all
the time the volcano that hangs over Matupi
was smoking like the mouth of . Hell. I
wasn’t very religious, but I can tell you it
made me think of the Last Judgment.
“There happened to be a doctor ashore at
Matupi, and he came aboard and stitched up
Finlay’s head, and told him if he wanted to
keep on drinking he would have to stop
living. Perhaps I’ve got it wrong, but it
sounded something like that. Finlay was
pretty scared, and said he would never touch
another drop. All the liquor on board was
finished, anyhow—as far as we knew at the
time. We made sail that day, and the Old
Man navigated the hooker like an angel.
Then, as bad luck would have it, one of the
hands found a cask of rum stowed away
under some of the cargo in the hold. The
Old Man and Thorsen started in drinking
a | ain - “d, though they tried to keep him
ott it, Finlay insisted on joining them. They
got worse than ever, and one night Thorsen
played the violin down to one string, like
1 aganim, and then said that nobody should
play on it again, and smashed it over Fin-
lay s head. We buried Finlay at Dobo, in
New Guinea. His money went to his rela-
tives in Australia. They sent for his re-
mains, and I had them dug up and brought
them back —packed in a gin-case.”
“And the others?”
“Captain Reele began to have bad luck,
aud lost billet after-billet. He died of drink
at feamarai. Thorsen is still pottering about
New Gumea in a little derelict schooner
like the ghost of the man lie used to be.
we must a U go some day.”
